Question 1 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 70 year old man presented with the complaint of difficulty to recognize the faces. Fundus examination shows some disturbance in the macular area. Which one is the most appropriate test for the diagnosis of his disease?

Options:
  B-Scan ultrasound
  Visual fields
✓ Optical coherence tomography
  Color vision test
  Visual acuity

Explanation:
Difficulty recognizing faces (prosopagnosia) can be related to central visual processing issues, and given the fundus examination showing macular disturbance, conditions affecting the macula are suspected. Optical coherence tomography (OCT) provides high-resolution cross-sectional images of the macula, making it the most appropriate test for detailed diagnosis of various retinal and macular diseases that could affect central vision.

Question 2 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50 years old patient is diagnosed with mature cataract and no view of retina. What investigation will you order first to evaluate the retina and vitreous in such patient?

Options:
  MRI
  CT scan
✓ B-Scan U/S
  OCT
  Tonometery

Explanation:
In a patient with a mature cataract where the view of the retina is obscured, B-scan ultrasonography (U/S) is the standard and first-line investigation. It allows for the visualization and assessment of the retina and vitreous to rule out any posterior segment pathology, such as retinal detachment or vitreous hemorrhage, before proceeding with cataract surgery.

================================================================================
Question 3 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A patient with sudden painless loss of vision and no fundal view was possible. Which of the investigation is helpful to see the retina

Options:
  A-Scan
✓ B- Scan
  Keratometry
  Indirect ophthalmoscopy
  Pachymetry

Explanation:
When there is sudden painless loss of vision and the fundus cannot be visualized (e.g., due to dense vitreous hemorrhage, mature cataract, or other opaque media), B-scan ultrasonography is the appropriate investigation to evaluate the retina and vitreous to determine the cause of vision loss.

Question 4 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 45 years old patient presented with decrease vision in both eye. The patient le uncontrolled diabetic from the last 12 years. On examination you suspected diabetic macular edema. Which investigation modality can better diagnose the macular edema?

Options:
  B Scan
  Fundus autofluorescence
  Fundus fluorescence angiography
✓ Optical coherence tomograph
  Visual fields

Explanation:
Optical coherence tomography (OCT) is the preferred investigation modality for better diagnosing and quantifying macular edema, especially in diabetic patients. It provides detailed cross-sectional views of the retina, allowing for precise assessment of retinal thickness and fluid accumulation within the macula.

Question 5 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 year old male patient presents with facial palsy for last 9 months. On examination he has exposure keratopathy, What will be your 1" line of treatment?

Options:
✓ Frequent topical lubricants
  Permanent Lateral tarsorrhapy
  Amniotic membrane graft
  Taping at night
  Temporary Glue tarsorrhapy

Explanation:
In a patient with facial palsy leading to lagophthalmos and exposure keratopathy, the immediate and most crucial first-line treatment is to protect the cornea from drying and damage. Frequent topical lubricants (artificial tears and lubricating ointments) are essential to maintain corneal hydration and prevent complications like corneal ulceration.

Question 6 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 34 years male patient was subjected to face burn with strong acid, two months later he presents with watering and inability to close his left eye. The explanation of this may be?

Options:
  Machanical ectropion
✓ Cicatricial ectropion
  Ocular cicatricial phamphigoid
  Paralytic ectropion
  Corneal erosion

Explanation:
A severe face burn with strong acid can lead to significant scarring and contraction of the eyelid skin. This cicatricial change can pull the eyelid margin outwards, resulting in cicatricial ectropion, which causes lagophthalmos (inability to close the eye) and watering.

Question 7 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 70 years old female presents with slowly growing mass on upper lid. On histological examination mass shows pale foamy vacuolated lipid-containing hyperchromatic nuclei. What is most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Squamous cell carcinoma
  Basal cell carcinoma
✓ Sebaceous gland carcinoma
  Malignant melanoma
  KAPOSI sarcoma

Explanation:
The histological findings of pale, foamy, vacuolated, lipid-containing cells with hyperchromatic nuclei, especially in a slowly growing mass on the upper eyelid of an elderly patient, are highly characteristic of sebaceous gland carcinoma. This aggressive tumor has a predilection for the upper eyelid because of the higher density of meibomian glands.

Question 8 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 1 years old male child is brought to you with complaint of drooping of right eyelid since birth. Parents say that his eye is half open during sleep as well. There is absent lid crease and poor levator function. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Amblyopia
  Aponeurotic Ptosis
✓ Congenital Ptosis
  Pseudoptosis
  Senile Ptosis

Explanation:
The clinical presentation of a drooping eyelid present since birth, associated with poor levator muscle function (eye remaining half open during sleep) and an absent lid crease, is characteristic of congenital ptosis. This is typically due to a developmental dystrophy of the levator palpebrae superioris muscle.

================================================================================
Question 9 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 2 years old male child is brought to you with complaint of drooping of right eyelid since birth. Parents say that his eye is half open during sleep as well. There is absent lid crease amblyopia and poor levator function. Which procedure you will perform in this patient?

Options:
  Fasanella Servat
  Muller resection
  Levator resection
✓ Permanent Brow Suspension
  Temporary Brow Suspension

Explanation:
For congenital ptosis with poor levator muscle function (typically less than 4-5 mm), as indicated by the 'half open during sleep' and 'poor levator function,' levator resection alone may be insufficient. In such cases, a permanent brow suspension (frontalis suspension) procedure is often performed to connect the eyelid to the frontalis muscle, allowing the child to lift the eyelid by raising their eyebrow. The presence of amblyopia further emphasizes the need for early and effective surgical correction of ptosis to clear the visual axis.

Question 10 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
25 years old female had a trauma to the right lower lid Now she is unable to close the right eye fully and the lower eyelid margin is rotated outward and there is scar mark 2mm below the lower eyelid. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Congenital ectropion
✓ Cicatricial ectropion
  Paralytic ectroipion
  Senile ectropion
  Traumatic entropion

Explanation:
The history of trauma leading to a scar below the right lower eyelid, which has caused the eyelid margin to rotate outward (ectropion) and an inability to fully close the eye, is characteristic of cicatricial ectropion. This type of ectropion is caused by the contraction of scar tissue.

Question 11 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
20 years old male presented with painful swelling on the upper eyelid for the last two weeks. Lesion is red and edematous and tender to touch What's the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Chalazion
  Lipoma
✓ Stye
  Dermoid cyst
  Traumatic entropion

Explanation:
A painful, red, edematous, and tender swelling on the eyelid, especially if acute or subacute (two weeks), is the classic presentation of a stye (hordeolum externum or internum). A stye is an acute suppurative infection of an eyelid gland.

Question 12 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
40 years old female had a history of bells palsy six months ago and then she was unable to open her left eye Now she is able to open her eye but eye is red and watery and lower eye lid is rotated outwards. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Congenital ectropion
  Cicatrical ectropion
✓ Paralytic ectropion
  Senile ectropion
  Traumatic entropion

Explanation:
A history of Bell's palsy (facial nerve paralysis) leading to inability to close the eye (lagophthalmos) and now the lower eyelid rotated outwards (ectropion) with a red and watery eye (exposure keratopathy) is characteristic of paralytic ectropion. The facial nerve innervates the orbicularis oculi muscle, which is essential for eyelid closure and proper lid positioning.

Question 13 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 16 year old patient have chronic irritation itching and mild photophobia. on examination he has small ulcer and redness on lid margine what is yours diagnosis?

Options:
✓ Staphylo-coccal blephritis
  Squamous blephritis
  Seborrheic blephritis
  Posterior blephritis
  Mixed blephritis

Explanation:
The symptoms of chronic irritation, itching, redness on the lid margin, and small ulcers associated with the lash follicles are characteristic of staphylococcal blepharitis (ulcerative anterior blepharitis). *Staphylococcus* species are the most common bacterial cause of this condition.

Question 14 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
What is the gold standard treatment of orbital dermoid cyst?

Options:
  Observation
  Aspiration
  Intra- lesional triamcinolone injection
  Antibiotic
✓ Surgical removal in toto

Explanation:
Orbital dermoid cysts are benign congenital lesions. The gold standard treatment is complete surgical excision (*in toto*, meaning entirely in one piece) without rupturing the cyst. This prevents recurrence and avoids inflammatory reactions from the spillage of cyst contents.

Question 15 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50 year old lady having an ulcer on the lower lid from last 2 years. This lesion has a central ulceration with raised border , What is yours diagnosis?

Options:
  Sebaceous glands carcinoma
  Squamous cell carcinoma
✓ Basal cell carcinoma
  Keratoacanthoma.
  Pyogenic granuloma

Explanation:
A chronic (2 years), non-healing ulcer on the lower eyelid with raised, pearly borders and a central ulceration is highly suggestive of basal cell carcinoma (BCC), which is the most common malignant tumor of the eyelid.

Question 16 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Which of the following is the best initial treatment of chalazion?

Options:
✓ Hot compression
  Dark glasses
  Triamcinolone
  Antibiotic
  Surgery

Explanation:
The best initial treatment for a chalazion is conservative management, which primarily involves frequent application of warm compresses (hot compression) and eyelid massage. This helps to liquefy the inspissated meibomian gland secretions and promote drainage.

Question 17 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
11 months old infant presents with watering right eye since birth. On pressing lacrimal sac causes regurgitation of mucopus material. What is the appropriate treatment option?

Options:
  Dacryocystorhinostomy
✓ Probing with syringing
  Massage with antibiotics up to age of 6 months
  Dacryocystectomy
  Probing

Explanation:
For congenital nasolacrimal duct obstruction (dacryostenosis) that persists beyond 6-9 months of age (or when conservative management with massage and antibiotics has failed), lacrimal duct probing with syringing is the appropriate next step. It aims to open the membrane obstruction at the distal end of the nasolacrimal duct.

Question 18 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 47 years old male presents with watery left eye. On examination she has red swelling over medial canthus, which is tender on palpation What is your diagnosis?

Options:
✓ Acute dacryocystitis
  Chronic dacryocystitis
  Sebaceous gland carcinoma
  Punctal stenosis

Explanation:
A red, tender swelling over the medial canthus, accompanied by epiphora (watery eye), is the classic presentation of acute dacryocystitis. This is an acute infection of the lacrimal sac, usually due to obstruction of the nasolacrimal duct.

Question 19 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
75 years old male labourer presented with watering and mucopurulent discharge for the last two years. He had medial canthal swelling as well What could be the treatment for this patient?

Options:
  Probing
  Syringing
✓ Dacryocystectomy
  Incision and drainage
  Dacryocystectomy

Explanation:
For chronic dacryocystitis in an elderly patient, the definitive surgical treatment is usually dacryocystorhinostomy (DCR), which creates a new drainage pathway. However, DCR is not listed as a unique option. Dacryocystectomy (removal of the lacrimal sac) is an alternative surgical treatment, particularly for older patients or those unfit for DCR, although it results in persistent epiphora. Given the options with 'Dacryocystectomy' repeated, it is the intended answer for a surgical approach to chronic sac issues, as probing/syringing are less definitive for chronic adult obstruction and incision/drainage is for acute abscess management.

Question 20 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 26 years old female presents with mucopurulent discharge and visual impairment for the last two months She used Tobramycin eye drops for one month with no improvement. On examination there are follicles/papillae on the upper tarsal conjunctiva of both eyes and mild superficial keratitis. She gives history of same condition of her hushand and two younger children. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
✓ Trachoma
  VKC
  Bacterial conjunctivitis
  Viral conjunctivitis
  Hypersensitivity to topical medication

Explanation:
The chronic mucopurulent discharge, visual impairment, presence of follicles and papillae on the upper tarsal conjunctiva, mild superficial keratitis, and the familial clustering of similar symptoms are all classic signs of trachoma, a chronic follicular conjunctivitis caused by *Chlamydia trachomatis*.

Question 21 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 16 year female using some drug prescribed my GP in periphery for spring catarrh for :last 5 years Now she presents with posterior subcapsular cataract both eye. Which drug causes such condition?

Options:
  Topical Sodium Chromo gylcate
  Topical Ciclosporine
✓ Topical Steroids
  Topical Anti histamine
  Topical amibiotic

Explanation:
Vernal keratoconjunctivitis (spring catarrh) is often treated with topical steroids for symptomatic relief of severe inflammation. However, long-term use of topical corticosteroids is a well-known risk factor for the development of posterior subcapsular cataracts, as well as glaucoma.

Question 22 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50 year former presents with progressive recurrent pterygium of right eye. He has past history of pterygium excision in same eye. Now what will be your treatment option?

Options:
  Leave it as
  Bare scleral excision
  Excision with mitomycin C
✓ Excision with auto conjunctival graft
  Excision with cryo

Explanation:
For recurrent pterygium, especially after a previous excision, simply excising it again has a high recurrence rate. The most effective treatment to reduce recurrence is excision combined with conjunctival autografting (taking a piece of healthy conjunctiva from the same eye and transplanting it to the bare sclera). Mitomycin C (MMC) can also be used as an adjunct.

Question 23 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 5 year old male child is brought to you with complaint of severe itching that in exacerbated at night You examine him and find dirty grey eyes. He is also not able to open eyes in light. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  AKC
  Anterior Uveitis
  Conjunctivitis
  Keratitis
✓ VKC

Explanation:
The severe itching (exacerbated at night), photophobia (unable to open eyes in light), and the presence of 'dirty grey eyes' (which can describe limbal papillae or other features of severe allergic inflammation) in a child are classic symptoms of Vernal Keratoconjunctivitis (VKC). This chronic, bilateral allergic conjunctivitis is often associated with atopy and seasonal recurrence.

Question 24 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 45 years old Labourer by profession comes to you with a fleshy growth encroaching upon cornea. His vision is ase affected. You diagnose him and advise multiple treatment options. Which of the following is the best management plan?

Options:
  Bare sclera technique
  Beta radiation
✓ Conjunctival autograft
  Inferior fornix rotation technique
  MMC

Explanation:
For symptomatic pterygium (a fleshy growth on the conjunctiva encroaching onto the cornea, affecting vision), surgical excision is the treatment. The best management plan to prevent recurrence, particularly after excision, is the use of a conjunctival autograft, where a piece of healthy conjunctiva is transplanted to the bare sclera after pterygium removal.

================================================================================
Question 25 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 45 years old farmer by profession comes to you with a fleshy growth encroaching upon cornea. His vision is also affected. You diagnose him as a case of pterygium and advise him surgery. Which of the following is not a feature of pterygium?

Options:
  Astigmatism
  Conjunctival Involvement
  Cosmetic Blemish
  Corneal Involvement
✓ UV exposure

Explanation:
Pterygium is a triangular fleshy growth of the conjunctiva that encroaches onto the cornea. It can cause corneal involvement, conjunctival involvement, astigmatism, and cosmetic blemish. UV exposure is a major *risk factor* for the development of pterygium, but it is not a *feature* of the lesion itself.

Question 26 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A young male of 22 years, present with gradual decrease of visual activity in both eyes and frequent change of refractive error on retinoscopy there was high irregular astigmatism. Give likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Keratoglobus
✓ Keratoconus
  Buphthalmos
  Terrien marginal degeneration
  Pellucid marginal degeneration

Explanation:
Progressive decrease in vision, frequent changes in prescription (especially myopic astigmatism), and high irregular astigmatism in a young adult are hallmark features of keratoconus, a progressive ectatic corneal disorder.

Question 27 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 21 years old patient presents with gradual painless blurred vision in right eye which is associated with haloes around lights for the last two weeks. On examination there is central zone of stromal oedema with keratic precipitates underlying the corneal oedema along with reduced corneal sensation. You diagnosed patient as disciform keratitis. What will be best treatment option for this patient?

Options:
✓ Topical antiviral + Topical steroids
  Topical antiviral + Topical antibiotics
  Topical antiviral + Topical cycloplegics
  Topical antibiotics + Topical steroids + cycloplegics
  Topical antibiotics + Topical cycloplegics

Explanation:
Disciform keratitis is an immune-mediated reaction to Herpes Simplex Virus (HSV) antigens in the corneal stroma. The mainstay of treatment is topical corticosteroids to suppress the immune response/inflammation, always covered by topical antivirals to prevent reactivation of active viral replication (dendritic keratitis).

Question 28 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 30 years old farmer presents with a history of trauma to the left eye with a leaf 5 days ago now he presents with pain, photophobia and redness of the eye. On examination he has grey white lesion 'having fluffy margins with hypopyon. What would be the most likely pathology?

Options:
  Anterior uveitis
  Bacterial Conjunctivitis
✓ Fungal keratitis
  Viral keratitis
  Bacterial keratitis

Explanation:
The history of trauma with vegetative matter (leaf) in a farmer, followed by a corneal ulcer with 'fluffy margins' (feathery edges), satellite lesions (often implied), and hypopyon, is highly characteristic of fungal keratitis.

================================================================================
Question 29 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 35-year-old man presented in OPD with history of agricultural trauma 3 days back on examination he has central corneal defect with fluffy margins along with hypopyon. Corneal defect stains with fluorescein. How you will manage this patient.

Options:
  Systemic anti biotics, systemic anti-fungal and systemic steroids
  Topical antibiotics, topical anti-fungal, topical cycloplegics and systemic antibiotics
✓ Topical anti-fungal, topical antibiotics, topical cycloplegics and systemic anti-fungal
  Topical antibiotics, Topical anti-fungal, topical cycloplegics. And systemic steroids
  Topical steroids, topical anti-fungal, topical cycloplegics and systemic steroids

Explanation:
For suspected fungal keratitis (agricultural trauma, fluffy margins), the primary treatment is topical antifungals. Cycloplegics are used for pain and to prevent synechiae. Broad-spectrum topical antibiotics are often added to cover bacterial coinfection. Systemic antifungals may be added for severe cases. Steroids are generally contraindicated in the active phase of fungal keratitis as they can worsen the infection.

Question 30 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 22 years female presents with advanced keratoconus in one eye. What will be best treatment option for this patient?

Options:
  Spectacles
  Rigid contact lenses
  Corneal cross linkage
  Eximer laser
✓ Penetrating keratoplasty

Explanation:
In 'advanced' keratoconus, where corneal scarring is present or the cone is too steep for contact lenses to fit or correct vision adequately, surgical intervention is required. Penetrating keratoplasty (corneal transplant) or deep anterior lamellar keratoplasty (DALK) is the standard treatment for restoring vision in advanced cases. Cross-linking is for progression, not restoring vision in advanced cases. Spectacles and contacts often fail in advanced disease.

Question 31 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 45 years female presents with pain redness and decreased vision left eye from last 4 days on examination she has central corneal epithelial defect with hypopyon. Which duotherapy is recommended for its treatment?

Options:
  Fortified cephalosporin and fluoroquinolones
✓ Fortified cephalosporin and aminoglycoside
  Fortified aminoglycoside and fluoroquinolones
  Fortified aminoglycoside and penicillin
  Fortified macrolides and fluoroquinolones

Explanation:
For severe bacterial corneal ulcers (suggested by hypopyon), broad-spectrum coverage is essential. The standard 'duotherapy' often involves fortified antibiotics to cover both Gram-positive and Gram-negative organisms. A common regimen is a fortified cephalosporin (e.g., cefazolin for Gram-positives) and a fortified aminoglycoside (e.g., tobramycin or gentamicin for Gram-negatives).

Question 32 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 years old patient presents with 2 days history of painful rash on right side of fore head extending down to eyelids. A vesicular skin lesion is also seen near tip of nose. Which of the following is appropriate treatment option?

Options:
  Topical Acyclovir ointment 8 times a day
✓ Oral acyclovir 800mg 5 times a day for 10 days
  Oral acyclovir 400mg 2 times a day for 10 days
  Oral acyclovir 800mg 2 times a day for 10 days
  Oral acyciovir 400mg 5 times a day for 10 days

Explanation:
The patient has Herpes Zoster Ophthalmicus (HZO), indicated by the painful dermatomal rash involving the ophthalmic division of the trigeminal nerve and the Hutchinson's sign (lesion on the tip of the nose). The standard antiviral treatment for HZO to reduce complications and post-herpetic neuralgia is high-dose oral acyclovir: 800 mg 5 times daily for 7-10 days.

Question 33 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 35 years old lady presents with watering, blurring of vision and discomfort Right Eye for the last six days. On examination there are linear branching corneal epithelial defects. The patient gives history of fever and mild trauma he with her son's finger two weeks back. She used tobramycin eye drops with no improvement. Her VA RE is 6/36 and L/E 6/6 what is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Corneal abrasion
  Corneal ulcer
✓ Herpes simplex keratitis
  Herpes zoster keratitis
  Cornnal erosion

Explanation:
A linear branching corneal epithelial defect is the classic description of a dendritic ulcer, which is pathognomonic for Herpes Simplex Keratitis (epithelial type). The history of fever (stressor) or mild trauma can trigger reactivation. The lack of response to antibiotics further supports a viral etiology.

Question 34 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 72 years old male presents with vesicular rash around her left eye. The left eye is red with some degree of photophobia, presumptive diagnosis of herpes zoster ophthalmicus made how will you treat this patient ?

Options:
  Oral acyclovir and topical acyclovir
  Intravenous acyclovir and topical acyclovir
  Oral famcyclovir and topical acyclovir
✓ Oral acyclovir
  Topical acyclovir

Explanation:
Systemic antiviral therapy is the mainstay of treatment for Herpes Zoster Ophthalmicus (HZO). Oral acyclovir (800mg 5x/day), valacyclovir, or famciclovir are used to reduce viral shedding, pain, and ocular complications. Topical antivirals are generally not effective for the skin rash or the systemic viral load and are adjunctive at best for specific corneal involvement, but oral therapy is the primary requirement. Among the options, oral acyclovir is the standard systemic treatment.

Question 35 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
The mother of a one and a half year old child gives history of a white reflex from one eye for the past 1 month. On computed tomography scan of the orbit there is calcification seen within the globe. The most bikely diagnosis is?

Options:
  Congenital cataract
✓ Retinoblastoma
  Endophthalmitis
  Coats disease
  pt.-Ipv (Typo in source, likely meant Persistent Hyperplastic Primary Vitreous PHPV)

Explanation:
Leukocoria (white reflex) in a young child (1.5 years) combined with the finding of intraocular calcification on CT scan is highly diagnostic of Retinoblastoma. Calcification is a hallmark feature of retinoblastoma and helps distinguish it from other causes of leukocoria like Coats disease or PHPV.

Question 36 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 year old woman presented with the complaint of decreased far vision. According to her she was first sorprised with good near vision as she was now able to read Quran Pak without near glasses but her joy was for just lew weeks at now she can't see few meters far her vision is RE 6/60 and LE Counting fingers whats the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Posterior Subcapsular Cataract
  Glaucoma
✓ Nuclear Cataract
  Polar Cataract
  Cortical Cataract

Explanation:
The phenomenon of improved near vision in an elderly presbyopic patient (able to read without glasses), known as 'second sight,' is caused by myopic shift. This myopic shift is characteristic of nuclear sclerosis (nuclear cataract) due to the increased refractive index of the hardening nucleus.

Question 37 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50 year old man presented with painless mild decrease of vision who underwent left eye phacoemulsification 6 weeks ago, it was complication free surgery. Pre op vision was 6/60 and on first post op day it was 6/12 which improved to 6/ 6 after one week post op It was good vision for the next four weeks but now he has again 6/12 vision. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Endophthalmitis
✓ Cystoid Muscular Edema
  PCO
  RD
  Astigmatism

Explanation:
Painless decrease in vision occurring a few weeks (typically 4-12 weeks) after uneventful cataract surgery is classic for Cystoid Macular Edema (CME), also known as Irvine-Gass syndrome. The vision initially improves and then declines.

Question 38 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 years old male patient had history of cataract surgery in the camp one week ago His vision is 6/36 in the operated eye with pinhole vision becomes 6/9. On slitlamp examination you see four interrupted very tight stitches causing identification of peripheral cornea . cornea is clear lens in place and good fundus view. what's the cause of decrease of vision in this patient P

Options:
  Wound dehiscence
  Infection
  Biometry error
✓ With the rule astigmatism.
  Against the rule astigmatism

Explanation:
Tight sutures in the superior cornea (typical for manual cataract surgery incisions) steepen the vertical meridian of the cornea. This results in 'With-The-Rule' (WTR) astigmatism (vertical meridian steeper than horizontal). The improvement with pinhole suggests a refractive cause.

Question 39 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 years old man presented with decreased vision in the outdoor He had phacoemulsification with foldable iol implant two years back he was alright for the two years but now his vision is gradually decreased. On examination his Vision in affected eye is 6/24. His fundus details though hazy appear normal. On retroillumination there are pearl like opacities in posterior chansber of anterior segment What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Corneal edema
  Endophthalmitis
✓ PCO
  PCR
  RD

Explanation:
Gradual decrease in vision months to years after cataract surgery, associated with 'pearl-like opacities' seen on retroillumination (Elschnig pearls), is characteristic of Posterior Capsular Opacification (PCO), also known as secondary cataract or after-cataract.

Question 40 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 years old female patient is diagnosed with mature cataract Her vision in the affected eye is hand movements. Her cornea is also not very healthy. Which of the following is the best procedure for restoration of her vision?

Options:
  FCCE
  ICCE
  Phaco with low power
  Phaco with high power
✓ Small incision cataract surgery

Explanation:
In the presence of a compromised cornea ('not very healthy,' likely implying endothelial issues) and a hard/mature cataract, Phacoemulsification carries a higher risk of corneal decompensation due to the ultrasound energy required. Small Incision Cataract Surgery (SICS) is a safer alternative as it is manual, uses no ultrasound energy, and is very effective for mature cataracts, protecting the endothelium better than high-power phaco.

================================================================================
Question 41 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 years old female patient underwent phaco for her left white cataract, Which of the following is not a post op complication of cataract surgery?

Options:
  Corneal Striations
  PCO
✓ PCR
  RD
  Refractive Surprise

Explanation:
PCR (Posterior Capsular Rupture) is an *intraoperative* complication that occurs during the surgery itself. Corneal striations, PCO (Posterior Capsular Opacification), RD (Retinal Detachment), and Refractive Surprise are all potential *postoperative* complications.

================================================================================
Question 42 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 2 year old female child is brought to you with complaint of white pupil and decrease of vision You examine her and diagnose her as a case of congenital cataract. Which one is the most common presentation?

Options:
  Blue Dot
✓ Eamellar
  Nuclear
  Polar
  Sutural

Explanation:
Lamellar (or Zonular) cataract is the most common type of congenital cataract presenting with visual impairment. It affects a specific layer or zone of the lens.

================================================================================
Question 43 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 years old man presented with right eye cataract. You performed his Biometry and calculated his IOL which is the most important factor in IOL calculation

Options:
  A constant
✓ Axial Length
  Bscan
  Keratoetry
  Tonometry

Explanation:
Axial length is the most critical factor in IOL power calculation. A small error in measuring axial length (e.g., 1 mm) can result in a significant refractive error (approx. 2.5 - 3 Diopters) postoperatively, much more than errors in keratometry.

================================================================================
Question 44 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 40 year old woman presented with gradual decrease of vision for the last few years in the right eye She first started to feel it when she bumped into the objects. Her vision is 6/12 OD and 6/6 OD IOP is 28mmhg OD and 16mmhg OS. There is a red birth mark around the right eye and deep anterior chamber Fundus examination reveals CD 0.7 OD and 0.40S whats the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  POAG
  PACG
✓ Sec OAG
  Sec ACG
  Congenital Glaucoma

Explanation:
The presence of a 'red birthmark around the eye' (Port-Wine Stain/Nevus Flammeus) suggests Sturge-Weber Syndrome. This condition is associated with ipsilateral glaucoma. Given the deep anterior chamber, open angle on gonioscopy (implied by deep AC and context), and raised IOP, it is a Secondary Open Angle Glaucoma (associated with episcleral venous pressure elevation or angle anomalies).

================================================================================
Question 45 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 45 year-old man presented with gradual decrease of vision for the last few months. According to him this vision in the right eye is more decreased as compared to the left eye He first started to feel it when he bumped into the objects His vision is 6/12 00 and 6/6 OD JOP is 19mmhg OD and 16mmhg OS. There is a deep anterior chamber and on fundus examination CD 0.6 OD and 0.50S. The most likely next investigatiom to reach the diagnosis is?

Options:
  Visual Fields and OCT Optic dist
  Visual Fields and HRT
  Visual Fields and CCT
  OCT Optic disc and HRT
✓ CCT and Gonio

Explanation:
Central Corneal Thickness (CCT) is crucial because thin corneas can lead to underestimation of IOP (making 'normal' pressure glaucoma possibly a high pressure one masked by thin cornea) and is an independent risk factor. Gonioscopy is essential to determine if the angle is open or closed, which is fundamental for classification and management. While fields/OCT assess damage, confirming the type (open vs closed) and true pressure risk (pachymetry) are vital diagnostic steps.

================================================================================
Question 46 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50-year-old man presented with gradual decrease of vision for the last few months. According to him his vision in the right eye is more decreased as compared to the left eye His vision is 6/12 RE and 6/9 LE IOP is 24mmhg RE and 18mmhg LE CCT central corneal thickness is 522umRE and 530umLE Gonio shows open angle both eyes CD 0.6RE and 0.4LE Visual fields and OCT optic disc also shows early glaucomatous changes. Which of the following drug will you start?

Options:
  Alpha 2 agonist
  Beta blocker
  Calcium channel blocker
✓ Prostaglandin analogues
  Pilocarpine

Explanation:
Prostaglandin analogues (e.g., Latanoprost) are the first-line medical therapy for Primary Open Angle Glaucoma (POAG) due to their high efficacy in lowering IOP, once-daily dosing, and relatively safe systemic side effect profile.

================================================================================
Question 47 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50-year old man is a newly diagnosed patient of open angle glaucoma He is a poor person labourer by profession. His IOP is 25mmHg in RE and 23mmHg in LE Which drug you should start to keep IOP under control in this particular patient?

Options:
  Alpha 2 agonist
✓ Beta blocker
  Calcium channel blocker
  Prostaglandin analogues
  Pilocarpine

Explanation:
While prostaglandin analogues are first-line, they can be expensive. For a 'poor person labourer,' cost is a significant factor in compliance. Beta-blockers (e.g., Timolol) are effective, widely available, and significantly cheaper than prostaglandins, making them a suitable cost-effective first-line choice in resource-limited settings.

Question 48 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50-year-old woman presented with painful decrease of vision in the right eye. He had a history of vein occlusion in the same eye four months ago. On examination his vision is CF and IOP 44mming and on slitlamp vessels are appreciated at 90 clock on the iris in RE. Gonioscopy reveal closed angle What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
✓ Alpha 2 agonist
  Beta blocker
  Calcium channel blocker
  Prostaglandin analogues
  Pilocarpine

Explanation:
The options provided are drug classes, but the question asks for a diagnosis. The clinical picture—history of CRVO ('90-day glaucoma'), rubeosis iridis (vessels on iris), high IOP, and angle closure (likely synechial)—is classic for Neovascular Glaucoma. If the question implies treatment, none of these alone is definitive for NVG without addressing ischemia (PRP/Anti-VEGF), but the question asks for diagnosis. The provided options are likely mismatched.

================================================================================
Question 49 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 55-year-old woman presented with painful decrease of vision in the left eye. She is having uncontrolled DM for the last 20 years. On examination his vision is CF and IOP 44mmHg and on slitiamp vessels are appreciated at 9 0 clock on the iris in LE Gonioscopy reveal closed angle. On fundoscopy fundal view is hazy but you see vitreous haemorrhage and neovessels on optic disc. Which of the following treatment option is not recommended in its management?

Options:
  Atropine
  Aqueous suppressants
  Pars Plana Vitrectomy
  Photocoagulation
✓ Pilocarpine

Explanation:
In Neovascular Glaucoma (NVG), the angle is closed by a fibrovascular membrane (rubeosis). Pilocarpine is contraindicated because it can increase inflammation, worsen the breakdown of the blood-aqueous barrier, and is ineffective or harmful in synechial angle closure. Atropine (cycloplegic) is used to reduce pain and inflammation. Treatment focuses on reducing IOP (aqueous suppressants) and treating retinal ischemia (PRP/Anti-VEGF/Vitrectomy).

================================================================================
Question 50 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 55-year-old woman presented with decrease of vision in the left eye. On examination his vision is CF and IOP 28mmHg and on slittamp white fibrillary material can be appreciated on the lens capsule. She had cataract as well vessels are appreciated at 9 0 clock on the iris in LE. Gonioscopy reveal whitish material deposits as well On fundoscopy CD in 0.7. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Angie Recession Glaucoma
  Neovascular Glaucoma
  Pigmentary Glaucoma
✓ Pseudoexfoliation Syndrome
  True Exfoliation Syndrome

Explanation:
The presence of 'white fibrillary material' (dandruff-like) on the lens capsule and in the angle is pathognomonic for Pseudoexfoliation Syndrome (PEX). This material clogs the trabecular meshwork, leading to Pseudoexfoliation Glaucoma.

Question 51 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 years old man presented with complaint of painful decrease of vision in right eye for last one week. According to him, he had episode of vein occlusion one year back in the same eye and vision was 6/60 after that. Now it is counting fingers. SL examination shows blood vessels on the iris with corneal edema and red watery eye. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
  Angle closure glaucoma
  Pigmentary glaucoma
  Lens induced glaucoma
  Inflammatory glaucoma
✓ Neovascular Glaucoma

Explanation:
History of retinal vein occlusion, followed by the development of rubeosis iridis (blood vessels on the iris) and painful high IOP (indicated by corneal edema), is the classic presentation of Neovascular Glaucoma.

Question 52 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 20 years old male presents with gradual loss of vision in his right eye. On examination there is right posterior subcapsular cataract. Aqueous shows mild cellular reaction, small grey white scattered K.Ps and vitreo is cells. There is mild colour difference between two eyes. VA right eye 6/60 and left eye 6/6. His IOP is 14 mmHg both eyes. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Anterior uveitis
✓ Fuchs Uveitis Syndrome.
  Intermediate uveitis
  Posterior uveitis
  Chronic uveitis

Explanation:
Fuchs Uveitis Syndrome (Heterochromic Iridocyclitis) typically presents in young adults with unilateral mild uveitis (stellate KPs, mild cells), heterochromia (iris atrophy), and cataract (PSC). It is often asymptomatic until cataract develops.

Question 53 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 35 year's old female presents with decreased vision and photophobia both eyes. On examination her visual acuity is CF, mutton fat K.Ps, aqueous cells and flare in both eyes. She gives history of penetrating trauma right eye ten months back and corneoscleral repair was done under GA. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Vogt-Koyanagi Harada Syndrome
  Lyme disese
  Sarcoidosis
✓ Sympathetic Ophthalmitis.
  Chronic uveitis

Explanation:
Sympathetic Ophthalmitis is a bilateral granulomatous panuveitis that occurs after penetrating trauma to one eye (the exciting eye), leading to inflammation in the fellow eye (the sympathizing eye).

Question 54 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 year old man presentest an emergency with complaints of painful decrease on vision for two days He had history of cataract surgery one week ago. On examination he had red swollen left eye, corneal edema, pus in the anterior chamber and poor fundus glow. His vision is 6/6 OD and CF OS. What's the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
✓ Acute Elephthalmitis (Endophthalmitis)
  Chronic Endophthalmitis
  Acute Keratins (Keratitis)
  Acute anterior Uveitis
  Acute congestive glaucoma

Explanation:
Acute postoperative endophthalmitis typically presents within days to weeks of surgery with pain, redness, hypopyon (pus in AC), and vitreous inflammation (poor fundus glow).

Question 55 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 56 years old patient's presented with sudden loss of vision in his left eye associated with floaters O/E anterior segment was normal, fundus showed vitreous hemorrhage in that eye. All of the following are risk factors for vitreous hemorrhago except?

Options:
  Diabetes
  Retinal vein occlusion
✓ Glaucoma
  Trauma
  Eales disease

Explanation:
Vitreous hemorrhage is caused by bleeding from retinal vessels (e.g., in PDR, CRVO, Eales disease) or trauma. Glaucoma itself does not typically cause vitreous hemorrhage unless it is neovascular glaucoma (secondary to ischemia), but primary glaucoma is not a direct cause.

Question 56 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 55 year old woman presented with painful decrease of vision in the left eye. She is having uncontrolled DM for the last 20 years. On examination his vision is CF and IOP 44mmHg and on slitlamp vessels are appreciated at 9 0 clock on the iris in LE. Gonioscopy reveal closed angle On fundoscopy fundal view is hazy but you see vitreous haemorrhage aml neovessels on optic disc. What is the most likely cause of this condition?

Options:
  Carotic Cavernous Fistula
  CRVO
  Ocular ischemic Syndrome
✓ Proliferative Diabetic Retinopathy
  Tumor in the Eye

Explanation:
The patient has signs of Neovascular Glaucoma (rubeosis, high IOP). The underlying cause is retinal ischemia. Given the history of uncontrolled Diabetes for 20 years and findings of neovessels on the disc and vitreous hemorrhage, Proliferative Diabetic Retinopathy (PDR) is the primary cause.

Question 57 (GMC - 2023)

Question:
A 59 years old hypertensive patient presented with sudden painless loss of vision in right eye. On examination bilateral anterior segments were normal, and right fundus showed flame shaped hemorrhages all around left fundus was Normal What is the most probable diagnosis?

Options:
  Central retinal artery occlusion
✓ Central retinal vein occlusion
  Retinal detachment
  Vitreous hemorrhage
  Hypertensive retinpathy

Explanation:
Sudden painless vision loss with 'flame-shaped hemorrhages all around' (blood and thunder appearance) is the hallmark of Central Retinal Vein Occlusion (CRVO).

Question 58 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Intra-vitreal anti VEGF injections are used in all of the following diseases except:

Options:
✓ Optic neuritis
  Central retinal vein occlusion
  Diabetic retinopathy
  Branch retinal vein occlusion
  Age related macular degeneration

Explanation:
Anti-VEGF is used to treat macular edema and neovascularization (AMD, Diabetic Retinopathy, RVO). Optic neuritis is inflammatory and treated with steroids.

Question 59 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 75 years old cardiac patient presented with sudden painless loss of vision in his right eye. On examination, his vision was perception of light in right eye. There was RAPD in right eye and fundus showed pale looking retina with cherry red spot at fovea. What is your most probable diagnosis?

Options:
✓ Central retinal artery occlusion
  Central retinal vein occlusion
  Diabetic retinopathy
  Branch retinal vein occlusion
  Age related macular degeneration

Explanation:
Pale retina with a cherry-red spot at the fovea and sudden profound vision loss is pathognomonic for Central Retinal Artery Occlusion (CRAO).

Question 60 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A middle aged lady is diagnosed with retinal detachment in her left eye. All the following can be other associated findings in that eye except?

Options:
✓ Raised IOP
  Tobacco dust in vitreous
  Retinal break
  Visual field defect
  Mobile retina

Explanation:
In Rhegmatogenous Retinal Detachment, the IOP is typically lower (hypotony) compared to the fellow eye. Tobacco dust (Shafer's sign) and retinal break are key features.

Question 61 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 45 years old hypertensive businessman presented with defective vision in right eye and was diagnosed as a case of branch retinal vein occlusion in that eye. All the following may be the treatment options for him except?

Options:
  Anti-VEGF injections
  Pars-plana vitrectomy
  Vascular sheathotomy
  Retinal photocoagulation
✓ Scleral buckling

Explanation:
Scleral buckling is a treatment for retinal detachment, not for retinal vein occlusion. Treatment for BRVO focuses on macular edema (Anti-VEGF, Laser) and sometimes surgical sheathotomy.

================================================================================
Question 62 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
All can be the causes of an exudative retinal detachment except?

Options:
  Malignant melanoma
  Hypertension
✓ Diabetic retinopathy
  Posterior scleritis
  Posterior uveitis

Explanation:
Diabetic retinopathy typically causes tractional retinal detachment (due to fibrous bands) or rhegmatogenous RD. Exudative RD is caused by fluid leakage from choroid/vessels due to inflammation (uveitis, scleritis) or tumors (melanoma) or severe hypertension.

Question 63 (GMC - 2023)

Question:
An 18 year's old myopic student presented with sudden painless loss of vision in his L eye. O/E anterior segment wirs normal, L fundus showed detached retina with U-shaped tear. What is your most probabile diagnosis?

Options:
  Exudative RD
✓ Rhegmatogenous RD
  Tractional RO

Explanation:
A retinal detachment associated with a retinal tear (U-shaped tear) is by definition a Rhegmatogenous Retinal Detachment.

Question 64 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 56 years old uncontrolled diabetic patient presented with blurring of vision. O/E there was bilateral mild cataracts and bilateral proliferative diabetic retinopathy, Which is the first suitable option for this patient?

Options:
✓ Panretinal photocoagulation
  Pars-plana vitrectomy
  Cataract surgery
  Observation
  All of the above

Explanation:
The presence of Proliferative Diabetic Retinopathy (PDR) requires prompt Panretinal Photocoagulation (PRP) to prevent severe vision loss from neovascular complications. Cataract surgery can be done later.

  Orbital B scan
  Xray Lumbosacral

Question 77 (GMC - 2023)

Question:
A young boy was presented in the eye clinic with blunt trauma to his left eye with a ball. There was a superficial corneal ulcer involving the inferotemporal quadrant of the cornea. Which of the following is the best management for this young boy?

  Use of antibiotic eye drops

  Use of antibiotic and eye patching
  No management required and it will heal by itself

Explanation:

Question 78 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
12 years old girl is brought to the eye emergency with history of blunt trauma to the RE with kids pellet gun. On examination VA is 6/60 cornea is hazy but epithelium is intact, anterior chamber is half filled with blood pupil is reactive Fundus appears normal Which of the following options is not included in the management?

Options:
✓ Antibiotics drops
  Anti-Glaucoma drops
  Bed Rest
  Cyclopentolate drops
  Steroid drops

Explanation:
In blunt trauma with hyphema (blood in AC) but intact epithelium, antibiotics are not strictly necessary unless there's a risk of infection (open wound). Steroids, cycloplegics, bed rest, and pressure lowering agents are standard hyphema management. Antibiotics are least critical here if globe is intact.

Question 79 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Which of the following does not require emergency ophthalmic treatment?

Options:
  Acute Angle Closure Glaucoma
  Acute Anterior Uveitis
  Giant Cell Arteritis
  Keratitis
✓ Orbital floor fracture

Explanation:
Orbital floor fractures without muscle entrapment or retrobulbar hemorrhage are usually managed conservatively initially (wait for swelling to subside) and repaired later if needed. The others are acute threats to vision.

Question 80 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 6 years male child is brought to the outdoor with complaints of decrease of vision while studying from white hoard in school Parents also noticed that child stands, very close to the television while watching cartoons. You performs retinoscopy anal there is streak moving in opposite directions What's the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Astigmatism
  Emmetropia
  Hyperopia
✓ Myopia
  Strabismus

Explanation:
Difficulty with distance vision (school board) and sitting close to TV are signs of myopia. 'Against' motion (opposite direction) on retinoscopy (assuming a plane mirror effect at working distance) typically indicates Myopia > 1.50 D.

================================================================================
Question 81 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 40 year old female comes to the outdoor with complaints of frontal headache and decrease of vision while reading and sewing She can see perfectly fine at distance What is the mov likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Astigmatism
  Emmetropia
✓ Hyperopia
  Myopia
  Presbyopia

Explanation:
Difficulty with near tasks (reading/sewing) and headaches (asthenopia) with clear distance vision in a 40-year-old could be early Presbyopia or latent Hyperopia. Given the headache component and age, Hyperopia decompensating or early presbyopia are key. Since Presbyopia is strictly age-related loss of accommodation and Hyperopia is a refractive error causing similar near symptoms often earlier, Hyperopia fits well if she needs accommodation for distance too but hides it. However, the classic near-vision difficulty at 40 is Presbyopia.

================================================================================
Question 82 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
You perform a retinoscopy of a patient complaining of decrease of vision. On retinoscopy the streaks moved with the movement Which of the following is most likely false?

Options:
  Astigmatism
  Emmetropia
  Hyperopia
  Myopia
✓ Myopia greater than working distance

Explanation:
'With' movement is seen in Hyperopia, Emmetropia, and low Myopia (less than working distance). It is NOT seen in Myopia greater than working distance (which shows 'against' movement).

Question 83 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A patient presents with right ptosis and the condition is painful. He is recently diagnosed as DM type 11.0/E there is right exotropia in primary position, pupil is dilated and not responding to light.VA 6/6 BE and fundi are grossly normal. There is no past history of trauma. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Aneurysm of anterior communicating artery.
  Aneurysm of middle cerebral artery.
  Aneurysm of posterior cerebral artery.
✓ Aneurysm of posterior communicating artery.
  Aneurysm of superior cerebellar artery.

Explanation:
Painful 3rd nerve palsy with pupil involvement (dilated pupil) is a neurosurgical emergency, most commonly caused by a Posterior Communicating Artery (PCOM) aneurysm compressing the nerve. Diabetic 3rd nerve palsy typically spares the pupil.

Question 84 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 6 mouth old child is brought to the outdoor with complaints of inward deviation of both eyes Right more than left Parents are worried that there is limitations of the eyes as she sees the object on the right with the left eye and object on the left with right eye so she cross fixates Also there is inferior oblique overaction. What is the mest likely diagnos

Options:
  Alternate esotropia
✓ Congenital esotropia
  Congenital exotropia
  Duane type 1
  6th nerve patsy

Explanation:
Large angle esotropia starting before 6 months, with cross-fixation and inferior oblique overaction, is classic for Congenital (Infantile) Esotropia.

Question 85 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A six year old child is brought to the outdoor with complaints of deviation of eyes. On examination she is unable to fixate both eyes wi When she fixates an object with right eye left eye is in exodeviation and when she fixates an object with the left eye right eye becomes exotropic her vision in both eyes is 6/6 What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Alternate esotropia
  Congenital esotropia
  Congenital exotropia
  Cyclic exotropia
✓ Intermittent exotropia

Explanation:
Alternating exodeviation with good vision and likely some control (implied by 'complaints of deviation' perhaps intermittent) suggests Intermittent or Alternating Exotropia.

Question 86 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A two year boys is brought to you with complaint that when he is attentive and looking near his eyes are straight but when he looks far one of the eyes moves outwards. That outward deviation is more marked when he in ill fatigued and tired What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Alternate exotropia
  congenital exotropia
  Constant exotropia
✓ Intermittent exotropia
  Periodic exotropia

Explanation:
Exotropia that manifests primarily at distance or when tired/ill (breakdown of fusion) is classic Intermittent Exotropia.

Question 87 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
60 years old made patient had sixth nerve palsy two years back and esotropia of 35PD. Now his esotropic has Improved to 20PD in two years and now not much improving His Lateral rectus has become wasted and weak. Which of the folowing is not muscle strengthening procedure?

Options:
  Conjunctival resection
✓ Muscle myotomy
  Muscle resection
  Muscle Transposition
  Muscle Tucking

Explanation:
Muscle myotomy is a weakening procedure (cutting the muscle). Resection, Tucking, and Transposition are strengthening/augmenting procedures.

Question 164 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Citelli's angle is?

Options:
  Solid angle
  Cerebellopontine angle
✓ Sinodural angle
  Genu of facial nerve
  second Genu of facial nerve

Explanation:
Citelli's angle is an anatomical landmark in the temporal bone, specifically referring to the sinodural angle, which is the angle formed between the sigmoid sinus and the middle fossa dura plate.

A young patient presents with history of dysphagia more to liquids than solids. The first investigation you will do is?

Options:
✓ Barium swallow
  Esophagoscopy
  Laryngoscopy
  CT chest
  MRI

Explanation:
For dysphagia, especially with a history suggesting a motility disorder (liquids > solids), a Barium swallow is often the first investigation to assess esophageal anatomy and motility.

The mother noticed that her child snores during sleep and has repeated attacks of chest infection. What could be best initial investigation for treatment?

Options:
  Chest X-ray
  Complete Blood count
  Pure tone audiometry
  Tympanometry
✓ X-ray lateral view nose, nasopharynx

Explanation:
The symptoms (nasal obstruction, snoring, infections) suggest Adenoid Hypertrophy. A lateral neck X-ray is a standard initial investigation to visualize adenoid size.

Question 167 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50 years old female complains of dysphagia while taking solid foods. On examination she was anemic. What could be the best initial screening test to diagnose :

Options:
✓ Chest x-ray
  CT scan
  MRI scan
  X-ray lateral view neck
  MRI scan with contrast

Explanation:
A Barium Swallow (often implied as initial X-ray study for dysphagia, though Chest X-ray is listed) or Chest X-ray can screen for structural causes, but Barium is specific. Given options, Chest X-ray is a basic screen, but Barium is better.

Question 168 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Which of the following disease is not diagnosed by OCT Macula?

Options:
  Age Related Macular Degeneration
  Cystoid Macular Edema
  Epi Retinal Membrane
✓ Glaucoma
  Macular Hole

Explanation:
Optical coherence tomography (OCT) of the macula is primarily used for conditions affecting the macula and retina, such as Age-Related Macular Degeneration, Cystoid Macular Edema, Epi Retinal Membrane, and Macular Hole. Glaucoma is an optic neuropathy that primarily affects the optic nerve head and retinal nerve fiber layer, for which OCT of the optic nerve head and RNFL is used, not specifically OCT of the macula for primary diagnosis.

Question 169 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
70 year old male patient with mature cataract present to you for surgery. On examination his VA is Hand Movements, Pupil is sluggish reactive. You are unable to visualize his fundus. Which investigation you will perform to establish health of the retina?

Options:
  A scan
✓ B scan
  Keratometry
  OCT
  Visual Fields

Explanation:
When a mature cataract prevents visualization of the fundus, B-scan ultrasonography is essential to assess the structural integrity and health of the retina and vitreous. This is crucial before cataract surgery to identify any pre-existing posterior segment abnormalities like retinal detachment or vitreous hemorrhage.

Question 170 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 56 years old diabetic patient presented with decrease vision in both eye from the last one year. On examination the anterior segment was normal. Fundoscopy showed retinal hemorrhages in all 4 quadrants with diabetic macular edema How you will measure the amount of macular edema in this patient?

Options:
  B-Scan ultrasonography
  Fundus autofluorescence
  Fundus Fluorescein angiography
✓ Optical coherence tomography
  Pachymetry

Explanation:
Optical coherence tomography (OCT) is the gold standard for diagnosing, quantifying, and monitoring diabetic macular edema. It provides high-resolution, cross-sectional images of the retinal layers, allowing for precise measurement of retinal thickness and detection of intraretinal fluid, which is essential for guiding treatment.

Question 171 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 75 years old farmer has a lesion on the Tower lid on the medial side for a few years There is a history of recurrent bleeding. On roiled margins with examination there surface telangiectasia. After performing biopsy the histology report is positive for BCC. What is the most appropriate treatment for a biopsy positive basal cell carcinoma of the lower eyelid?

Options:
  Cryotherapy to the cancer and margins
  Local antimetabolite treatment
  Radiations with 2500 rads to the lesion and margins
✓ Excision with frozen section control of margins
  Cautery to the margins

Explanation:
For biopsy-proven basal cell carcinoma (BCC) of the eyelid, the most appropriate and effective treatment is complete surgical excision with clear margin control. Techniques like frozen section control (or Mohs micrographic surgery) are preferred as they allow for immediate microscopic examination of surgical margins, ensuring complete tumor removal while preserving maximum healthy tissue, which is crucial for eyelid reconstruction.

Question 172 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 12 years old female child is brought by the mother to the eye OPD for the complaint of drooping of the right upper eyelid since birth. The ophthalmologist diagnosed her as a case of right congenital ptosis. Which of the following best explains why when a ptotic lid is lifted the contralateral lid falls?

Options:
  Inhibition of Mullerls muscle
  Sherrington's law of reciprocal innervation
  Relaxation of the Frontalis muscle
✓ Hering's law of equal innervation
  Relaxation of Muller's muscle

Explanation:
Hering's law of equal innervation states that the levator muscles of both eyelids receive equal and simultaneous innervation. In cases of unilateral ptosis, the brain compensates by sending increased innervation to both levators. When the ptotic lid is surgically corrected or manually lifted, this compensatory excess innervation to the contralateral levator is unmasked, causing the contralateral (previously normal) eyelid to drop.

Question 173 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 65 years old female presents with inward rolling of the right lower eyelid. There is foreign body sensation and watering due to constant rubbing of the eyelashes against the cornea. Which of the following factors is least likely to contribute to the development of entropion

Options:
  Preseptal orbicularis override
  Horizontal lid laxity
  Posterior Lamella foreshortening
  Capsulopalpebral fascia disinsertion
✓ Lateral lid laxity

Explanation:
Involutional entropion (inward rolling of the eyelid) is primarily caused by age-related factors such as preseptal orbicularis override (strong contraction of the orbicularis muscle), horizontal lid laxity (generalized), disinsertion of the lower lid retractors (capsulopalpebral fascia disinsertion), and sometimes posterior lamellar foreshortening. Lateral lid laxity, however, is a factor that contributes more directly to ectropion (outward rolling) by allowing the lid to sag laterally, rather than rolling inward.

Question 174 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50yrs poor & literate patient comes to tye and with irritation both eyes for many times. On examination there is mild conjunctival redness. His vision is 6/6 both eyes. Eyes are dirty & there are some moving objects in the eye lashes. What is the most probable cause?

Options:
  Allergic Conjunctivitis
  Anterior Blepharitis
✓ Phthiriasis bulbi
  Posterior blepharitis
  Viral keratitis

Explanation:
The presence of irritation, dirty eyes, and 'moving objects in the eyelashes' (which are often nits or adult lice) in a patient with otherwise normal vision is highly suggestive of phthiriasis palpebrarum (pubic lice infestation of the eyelashes and eyelid margins), commonly caused by *Phthirus pubis*.

Question:
A male patient was complaining of continuous redness of bath eyes, foreign body sensation, and frequent loss of lashes. On examination, the lid margins were hyperemic, and the lashes were matted with yellow crusts, which left painful ulcers on trying to move. The most reliable diagnosis is

Question 182 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A young patient presents with painless swelling in upper lid for 3 months. The chalazion is a

Options:
  Non granulomatous inflammation
✓ Lipogrenulomatous inflammation
  Caseating granuloma
  Non caseating granuloma
  Hypersensitivity reaction

Explanation:
A chalazion is a chronic, sterile, lipogranulomatous inflammation of the meibomian gland. It develops due to the obstruction of a meibomian gland duct, leading to the leakage of lipid contents into the surrounding tissue, which then elicits a granulomatous inflammatory response.

Question 183 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:
A 60 years old male came to the OPD with mass on the medial canthal area On histological evaluation it came to be squamous cell carcinoma. The tumour was resected but irradiation was also required for complete cure from the carcinoma. What is complication of irradiation to medial canthal area?

Options:
  Skin damage
  Madarosis
✓ Nasolacrimal duct stenosis
  Dry eye
  Keratopathy

Explanation:
Irradiation (radiotherapy) to the medial canthal area for eyelid malignancies can damage the delicate tissues of the nasolacrimal drainage system, leading to fibrosis and subsequent stenosis (narrowing) of the nasolacrimal duct. This results in epiphora (excessive tearing) as a common complication.

Question:
A mother brings her three months baby with capillary hemangioma of Rt upper lid. The most effective treatment for this condition is?

Options:
  Intralesional Avastin injection
  Intralesional steroid injection
  Laser ablation
✓ Oral propranolol therapy
  Surgical excision

Explanation:
For problematic infantile capillary hemangiomas (strawberry hemangiomas), especially those affecting the eyelid and potentially causing amblyopia or visual axis obstruction, oral propranolol therapy has become the first-line and most effective treatment. It typically causes rapid regression of the hemangioma.

Question 186 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:
A 40 year old female has been complaining of epiphors of the right eye for six months. On examination the regurgitation test is positive. There is obstruction of nasolacrimal duct on sac wash and syringing. When performing DCR, The asheum is created at the level of the :

Options:
  Superior turbinate
✓ Middle turbinate
  Inferior turbinate
  Posterior lacrimal crest
  Anterior lacrimal crest

Explanation:
During a dacryocystorhinostomy (DCR) procedure, an ostium (opening) is created in the nasal bone, connecting the lacrimal sac to the nasal cavity. This opening is typically made at the level of the middle turbinate to ensure effective tear drainage and patency.

Question 187 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A patient 50 yrs female with history of Anorexia Nervosa is aroughs by the attendant tor some ocular problem. On examination her vision is 6/6 both eyes. There are some conjunctival dry & lusterless points which show Vitamin A deficiency. Which layer of the tear film is affected by this condition.?

Options:
  Aqueous layer
  lipid layer
  Lactoferrin
  Mixed layer
✓ Mucin layer

Explanation:
Vitamin A deficiency primarily affects the goblet cells of the conjunctiva, which are responsible for producing mucin. Mucin forms the innermost layer of the tear film and is crucial for wetting the corneal and conjunctival surfaces. A deficiency leads to xerosis (dryness) and lusterless conjunctival points (Bitot spots).

Question 188 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 4 months baby brought by the mother complaining of watering since birth. You diagnosed the baby as having congenital nasolacrimal duct obstruction. What is the first line of treatment.???

Options:
  DCR
  DCR with intubation
  Intubation without DCR
✓ Lacrimal massage with antibiotics
  Probing

Explanation:
For congenital nasolacrimal duct obstruction in a 4-month-old infant, the first-line treatment is conservative management. This includes daily lacrimal sac massage (Crigler massage) and topical broad-spectrum antibiotics to prevent or treat secondary dacryocystitis, as most cases resolve spontaneously within the first year of life.

Question 189 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A patient 25 yrs female complaining of irritation, redness, foreign body sensation for many times. On examination her vision is 6/6 both eyes, posterior blepharitis is noted. Which layer of the tear film is mostly affected in this condition?

Options:
✓ Anterior layer
  Inner layer
  Middle layer
  All layers
  No layer is affected

Explanation:
Posterior blepharitis is an inflammation of the eyelid margin involving the meibomian glands. These glands produce the oily (lipid) layer of the tear film, which forms the outermost (anterior) layer of the tear film. Dysfunction of these glands leads to tear film instability and evaporative dry eye symptoms.

  Dermoid Cyst
  Dacryoadenitis
  Blepharitis

The clinical picture of chronic epiphora (watering eye), a non-tender cystic swelling in the right medial canthal region, and a positive regurgitation test (mucopurulent discharge from the puncta on pressing the lacrimal sac) is diagnostic of chronic dacryocystitis. This is a chronic inflammation of the lacrimal sac, usually due to nasolacrimal duct obstruction.

Question 191 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:
An Ophthalmologist was giving lecture on tear film to 3rd year MBBS students. Regarding the tear film which statement is true?

Options:
✓ Has there layers
  The middle layer is from the meibomian glands
  Is predominantly mucous
  Suffers evaporative lost of 50%
  Becomes hypotonic with evaporation

Explanation:
The tear film is composed of three distinct layers: an outer lipid layer (produced by meibomian glands), a middle aqueous layer (produced by the lacrimal glands), and an inner mucin layer (produced by conjunctival goblet cells).

Question:

  Above the medial palpebral ligament
  Lateral to medial palpebral ligament

  Behind the medial palpebral ligament

Question:
A father brings a shree marithe bay for complaints of watering both eyes since birth. Examination under anaesthesia was planned. What is the most important test?

  Anterior segment examination

Question 195 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:

  Meningioma
  Dermoid cyst
  Neurofibromatosis type 2

Question:
A 10yrs bay is brought by his father comes to eye opd with redness irritation photophobia for the last to many times. These signs and symptoms typically increase in summer & decrease in winter. His vision is 6/6 in both eyes. There is watery discharge with it. What is the most probable cause

Question 200 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:
A patient 50 yrs female complaining of vritation redness foreign body sensation. On examination her vision is 6/6 both eyes you suspect dry eye and you want to do Schirmer test for 5 minutes for confirmation. What is the parameter for confirmation?

Options:
  Less than 5 mm
✓ Less than 10 mm
  Less than 15mm
  Less than 20mm
  Less than 25mm

Explanation:
The Schirmer test measures tear production. A reading of less than 10 mm of wetting of the filter paper strip after 5 minutes is generally considered indicative of tear deficiency (dry eye). Some criteria use less than 5 mm for severe dry eye, but less than 10 mm is a common diagnostic cutoff.

Question 201 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50yrs patient comes to eye opd with redness irritation photophobia and severe pain Left eye for the last 45 times. He gives history of agriculture trauma while working in farms. His vision is 6/5 eye and in his Left eye. There is severe redness, corneal defect & hypopyon in his left eye. There is a purulent discharge with it. What is the most probable cause:

Options:
  Conjunctivitis
  Blepharitis
✓ Bacterial keratitis
  Viral keratitis
  Sub conjunctival hg

Explanation:
Although agricultural trauma often points to fungal etiology, the presence of 'purulent discharge' and a severe acute presentation is often more characteristic of bacterial keratitis. Fungal keratitis typically has a drier, necrotic appearance with feathery margins. However, fungal is also a strong differential. Given 'purulent discharge' specifically mentioned, bacterial keratitis is a very strong candidate, often secondary to vegetative trauma as well. Often questions distinguish fungal by 'fluffy/feathery' and bacterial by 'purulent/suppurative'.

Question:

  Observation
  Oral analgesic
  Topical antibiotic
  Topical antifungal
✓ Topical antiviral

Explanation:
A branching epithelial corneal defect is a dendritic ulcer, diagnostic of Herpes Simplex Keratitis. The treatment of choice is a topical antiviral agent, such as acyclovir or ganciclovir ointment/gel.

Question 203 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 32 years old patient presented with decreased vision in the right eye associated with foreign body sensation and decreased visies and watering You examined the patient and found a carneal sicer. Which of the following drugs are contraindicated in a corneal ulcer patient?

Options:
  Antibiotics
  Cycloplegics
✓ Steroids
  Analgesics
  Antifungals

Explanation:
Topical steroids are generally contraindicated in the active stage of an infectious corneal ulcer (especially bacterial, fungal, or herpetic epithelial) because they inhibit the immune response, collagen synthesis, and wound healing, potentially leading to perforation or worsening of the infection.

Question:

✓ Intensive topical antibiotics

  Oral analgesics
