Question 205 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 25 years male Phakic patient came for the follow up checkup after pars plana vitrectomy with silicon oil surgery. What is the most common refractive change that occurs with silicon oil?

Options:
  Less hypermetropic
  Less myopic
✓ More hypermetropic
  More myopic
  No refractive changes

Explanation:
In a phakic eye, silicone oil (which has a higher refractive index than vitreous but lower than the lens) alters the refraction. The interface between the posterior lens surface and the silicone oil acts as a negative lens, causing a hyperopic shift (more hypermetropic).

Question 206 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50-year-old female with a history of well- controlled diabetes recently underwent uncomplicated phaco- emulsification with a posterior chamber intraocular lens in her left eye and now she complains of blurry vision. Her Best- corrected visual acuity (BCVA) is 20/80 in her left tys. Her FFA shows a flower petal pattern of hyper fluorescence. Which retinal layer accounts for the appearance of this patient's vision loss?

Options:
  Inner plexiform
  Outer nuclear
✓ Outer plexiform
  Nerve fiber layer
  Retinal pigment epithelium

Explanation:
The flower petal pattern on FFA is diagnostic of Cystoid Macular Edema (CME). In CME, fluid accumulates primarily in the Outer Plexiform Layer (Henle's layer) of the retina, creating cyst-like spaces arranged radially around the fovea.

Question 207 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:
A 35 years aphakic male patient underwent pars plana vitrectomy. While putting silicone oil there is a great chance of pupillary black glaucoma. What is the best procedure during surgery to prevent pupillary black glaucoma in this patient?

✓ Performing iridectomy
  Performing laser
  No extra precaution needed

  Trabeculectomy

Explanation:
In aphakic eyes filled with silicone oil, the oil bubble can block the pupil, preventing aqueous flow (pupillary block) and causing acute glaucoma. To prevent this, a surgical inferior peripheral iridectomy (Ando iridectomy) is performed at the 6 o'clock position to allow aqueous to pass into the anterior chamber even if the pupil is blocked by the buoyant oil.

================================================================================
Question 208 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A female patient who was operated for cataract six months Back comes to you With foggy vision. On examination there was posterior capsular thickening. Which mode of treatment is most useful?

Options:
  Argon laser
  Diode laser
  Excimer laser
  Kryptonizier
✓ YAG Laser

Explanation:
Posterior Capsular Opacification (PCO) is treated by creating an opening in the opacified capsule using an Nd:YAG laser (Neodymium-doped Yttrium Aluminum Garnet). This procedure is called YAG laser capsulotomy.

================================================================================
Question 209 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50 years old patient undergone phacoemulsification for cataract extraction 3 days back presented with severe pain, redness and decrease vision in operated eye. There is also hypopyon and no view of Fundus. He you will treat this patient?

Options:
  Intracameral antibiotics
  Intravenous antibiotics
✓ Intravitreal antibiotics
  Topical antibiotics
  Observation

Explanation:
Severe pain, redness, hypopyon, and loss of vision 3 days post-cataract surgery indicates acute postoperative endophthalmitis. This is an ocular emergency. The standard of care is immediate intravitreal injection of antibiotics (e.g., vancomycin and ceftazidime) to achieve high local concentrations directly at the site of infection.

Question 210 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 4 year old child presents with cataract in both eyes and systemic illness. On examination he has Oil droplet cataract which occur in :

Options:
  Toxoplasmosis
  Marfan syndrome
  Rubell
✓ Galactoasemia
  Down syndrome

Explanation:
'Oil droplet' cataract is a classic sign of Galactosemia, a metabolic disorder. It is often reversible if detected early and dietary galactose is restricted.

Question 211 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50 year old presents with severe pain 3 days after cataract surgery. Most common organism involved in acute postoperative endophthalmitis is

Options:
✓ Gram positive organisms
  Gram negative organisms
  Aspergillux
  Fusarium
  anaerobes

Explanation:
Gram-positive organisms are responsible for the vast majority (approx. 70-80% or more) of acute postoperative endophthalmitis cases. *Staphylococcus epidermidis* (coagulase-negative staph) is the single most common isolate, derived from the patient's own eyelid flora.

Question 212 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 15 year old boy with musculoskeletal and cardiac abnormalities presents with Ectopia lentis which occur in

Options:
✓ Marfan syndrome
  Hypertensive patients
  Diabetic patients
  Myesthenia gravis
  Down syndrome

Explanation:
Ectopia lentis (subluxation of the lens), typically superior and temporal, combined with musculoskeletal (tall, arachnodactyly) and cardiac (aortic dilation) abnormalities is the classic triad of Marfan syndrome.

================================================================================
Question 213 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 40 years male patient with glaucoma came to the end with a compliant of pain and decreased vision. On examination his visual acuity is 8/24 and 6/60 in light and left eyn respectively. Posterior segment examination shows bilateral optic disc cupping. His IOP in the right eye is 26mmHg and in the left eye is 30 mm Hg. Which of the following visual field defects are not associated with glaucoma?

Options:
  Nasal step
  Paracenral defect
  Arcuate lesions
✓ Homonymous hemianopia
  Ring scotoma

Explanation:
Homonymous hemianopia is a neurological field defect caused by lesions in the retro-chiasmal visual pathway (e.g., stroke, tumor). It respects the vertical midline. Glaucomatous defects (nasal step, arcuate/Bjerrum scotoma, paracentral scotoma, ring scotoma) are nerve fiber bundle defects that typically respect the horizontal midline.

Question 214 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Amjad, a 60-year-old man, visits an eye clinic for a routine checkup. He has no significant medical history. During the examination, the ophthalmologist measures his intraocular pressure (IOP), which is found to be 28 mm Hg in both eyes and open angles on gonioscopy. The rest of the eye exam and investigations are normal. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Open-angle glaucoma
✓ Ocular Hypertension
  Angle-closure glaucoma
  Secondary glaucoma
  Lens induced glaucoma

Explanation:
Ocular Hypertension is defined as elevated IOP (>21 mmHg) with normal optic nerves and visual fields, and open angles, in the absence of any other ocular or systemic cause. Since the rest of the exam is normal, this fits the definition.

Question 215 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 55-year-old man with a family history of glaucoma, regularly visits his eye doctor for checkups. During his last visit, the ophthalmologist noted increased cupping of the optic disc and visual field defects, even though his IOP has consistently been within the normal range. What type of glaucoma should be considered in his case?

Options:
  Open angle glaucoma
✓ Normal-tension glaucoma
  Angle-closure glaucoma
  Secondary glaucoma
  Pseudoexfoliation glaucoma

Explanation:
Normal-Tension Glaucoma (NTG) is a form of open-angle glaucoma characterized by glaucomatous optic nerve damage and visual field loss despite IOP consistently remaining within the normal range (<21 mmHg).

Question 216 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 70-year-old woman, has been diagnosed with open-angle glaucoma and has been prescribed toplest fatanoprost to lower her IOP. Despite taking her medication regularly, her IOP remains high. What might be the next step in her treatment?

Options:
  lncrease the dosage of her current medication
✓ Add a different class of glaucoma medication
  Schedule her for immediate surgery
  Discontinue glaucoma medications and monitor closely
  continue same medications

Explanation:
If monotherapy (like latanoprost) is insufficient to reach the target IOP, the standard next step is to either switch to another class or, more commonly, add a second drug from a different class (e.g., beta-blocker, alpha-agonist, or CAI) to achieve better control.

Question 217 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50-year-old man has been experiencing gradual peripheral vision loss in both eyes. His IOP is elevated, open angles on gonioscopy and the ophthalmologist observes characteristic changes in his optic nerve head. There is no other ocular or systemic disease. Which type of glaucoma is most likely affecting him?

Options:
✓ Open-angle glaucoma
  Normal-tension glaucoma
  Angle-closure glaucoma
  Secondary glaucoma
  ocular hypertension

Explanation:
The clinical picture of elevated IOP, open angles, glaucomatous optic nerve damage, and visual field loss (peripheral loss), without identifiable secondary causes, fits the definition of Primary Open-Angle Glaucoma (POAG).

Question 218 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 56 years old man presented with gradual pregressive decrease vision in the right from last 8 months. On examination the vision in 6/60 in right eye and 6/9 in left eye with no Improvement with pinhole. Fundus examination is hazy but seems to be normal. What is the most probable diagnosis?

Options:
  Age related macular degeneration
  Corneal opacity
✓ Cataract
  Diathetic retinopathy
  Glaucoma

Explanation:
Gradual painless vision loss with 'hazy fundus view' (media opacity) and no improvement with pinhole is highly suggestive of a cataract. Glaucoma affects peripheral vision first and usually leaves central vision until late. ARMD affects the macula but media would be clear unless there's hemorrhage.

Question 223 (KGMC - 2023)

✓ Fundus Fluorescein angiography

Explanation:

  Mucocele
  Orbital cellulitis

Question 254 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Principle of scleral buckling surgery in rhegmatogenous retinal detachment:

Options:
  Flatten retina by removing vitreous traction
  Create chorioretinal scar around the break
✓ Indent the sclera to support the retinal break
  Replace vitreous with silicone oil
  Drain subretinal fluid completely

Explanation:
The primary goal of scleral buckling is to indent the sclera (buckle it inward) to relieve vitreous traction and support the retinal break, allowing the RPE to pump out subretinal fluid and reattach the retina.

✓ Likely Mucormycosis (Rhinocerebral)

Question 256 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 45-year-old man presents with slowly progressive painless proptosis and restricted elevation. CT orbit shows an expansile lesion in frontal sinus eroding into orbit. Most likely diagnosis:

Options:
  Dermoid cyst
✓ Frontoethmoidal mucocele
  Lymphangioma
  Pleomorphic adenoma
  Cavernous hemangioma

Explanation:
Mucoceles are cystic lesions that arise from obstruction of sinus ostia. They are slowly expansile and can erode through bone (frontal sinus into the orbit), causing proptosis and mass effect.

================================================================================
Question 257 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60-year-old male presents with sudden painless loss of vision. Fundus shows vitreous hemorrhage. Cause?

Options:
  Retinal detachment
  Central retinal artery occlusion
  Central retinal vein occlusion
✓ Proliferative diabetic retinopathy
  Central serous retinopathy

Explanation:
Proliferative Diabetic Retinopathy (PDR) involves the growth of fragile new blood vessels (neovascularization) which commonly rupture, causing spontaneous vitreous hemorrhage and sudden vision loss.

================================================================================
Question 258 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Which muscle function is lost in abducent nerve palsy?

Options:
✓ Lateral rectus
  Medial rectus

Explanation:
The Abducent nerve (CN VI) innervates the Lateral Rectus muscle. Palsy results in loss of abduction (inability to look outward).

================================================================================
Question 259 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
In myotonic dystrophy, what type of ptosis is seen?

Options:
  Mechanical
  Congenital
  Aponeurotic
✓ Myogenic

Explanation:
Myotonic dystrophy is a muscular dystrophy; the ptosis is caused by primary weakness of the levator muscle fibers, making it a myogenic ptosis.

================================================================================
Question 260 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60-year-old man presents to ER with ptosis, dilated pupil, eye down and out. Which cranial nerve is affected?

Options:
  Abducens
  Trochlear
✓ Oculomotor
  Optic
  OFL

Explanation:
These are classic signs of a complete Oculomotor (CN III) nerve palsy: Ptosis (levator), mydriasis (sphincter pupillae), and 'down and out' position (unopposed action of SO and LR).

================================================================================
Question 261 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50-year-old female with long-standing hypertension shows only arteriovenous nicking and general narrowing of arterioles. No hemorrhages or exudates. Which grade of retinopathy?

Options:
✓ Grade 1
  Grade 2
  Grade 3
  Grade 4
  Hypertensive choroidopathy

Explanation:
Generalized narrowing is Grade 1. AV nicking is typically the hallmark of Grade 2. However, in the absence of hemorrhages/exudates (Grade 3) or papilledema (Grade 4), this represents early hypertensive changes. The provided key selects Grade 1.

================================================================================
Question 262 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Which finding suggests afferent pupillary defect (Marcus Gunn pupil)?

Options:
✓ Pupil dilates when light is moved from normal eye

Explanation:
In an APD, the affected eye perceives less light. When the flashlight is swung from the healthy eye to the affected eye, the consensual constriction signal is lost, and the pupil paradoxically dilates.

================================================================================
Question 263 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Tractional retinal detachment occurs commonly in association with:

Options:
  High myopia
  Trauma
✓ Diabetic retinopathy
  Retinoschisis
  Posterior uveitis

Explanation:
Proliferative Diabetic Retinopathy leads to the formation of fibrovascular membranes. As these membranes contract, they pull on the retina, causing Tractional Retinal Detachment.

================================================================================
Question 264 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Best investigation for CSR:

Options:
✓ OCT

Explanation:
Optical Coherence Tomography (OCT) is the gold standard for diagnosing and monitoring CSR as it clearly visualizes the subretinal fluid and retinal elevation.

Question 265 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
60-year-old patient comes for glaucoma evaluation: IOP 34 & 24 mmHg; OCT RNFL and field changes; no other disease. Type of glaucoma?

Options:
  Normal tension glaucoma
  Ocular hypertension
  Pigmentary glaucoma
✓ Primary open-angle glaucoma
  Secondary open-angle glaucoma

Explanation:
The presence of elevated IOP (>21 mmHg) combined with characteristic optic nerve damage (RNFL changes) and visual field loss, without a secondary cause, indicates Primary Open-Angle Glaucoma.

================================================================================
Question 266 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Which feature differentiates benign intracranial hypertension from a space-occupying lesion?

Options:
  Headache
  Papilledema
✓ Normal neuroimaging
  Visual field defect
  Vomiting

Explanation:
Both BIH (Pseudotumor Cerebri) and Space-Occupying Lesions (tumors) cause raised ICP (headache, papilledema). The distinguishing feature is that BIH has normal neuroimaging (no mass seen).

================================================================================
Question 267 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Lesions at optic chiasma cause:

Options:
✓ Bitemporal hemianopia

Explanation:
The optic chiasma contains the crossing nasal retinal fibers (responsible for temporal vision). A lesion here affects both crossing pathways, leading to blindness in the temporal halves of both visual fields.

Question 268 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
5-year-old boy with white pupillary reflex and calcified fundus mass. Diagnosis:

Options:
✓ Retinoblastoma
  Toxocariasis
  Coats disease
  ROP
  Retinal detachment

Explanation:
Leukocoria (white reflex) in a young child combined with intraocular calcification is the hallmark presentation of Retinoblastoma, the most common intraocular malignancy in children.

Question 269 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Down and in movement of eye is done by:

Options:
  Superior rectus
  Medial rectus
✓ Superior oblique
  Inferior oblique
  Inferior rectus

Explanation:
The primary action of the Superior Oblique is intorsion, but its depressive action is maximal when the eye is adducted (looking in). Therefore, it moves the adducted eye down.

Question 270 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Indications for pars plana vitrectomy:

Options:
  Epiretinal membrane
  Diabetic retinopathy
✓ Rhegmatogenous retinal detachment

Explanation:
PPV is used for all these conditions, but in the context of the key, it is the primary surgical choice for complicated Rhegmatogenous Retinal Detachments (e.g., with PVR, giant tears) or when buckling isn't suitable.

================================================================================
Question 271 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
The principle of scleral buckling surgery in rhegmatogenous retinal detachment is to:

Options:
  Flatten the retina by removal of vitreous
  Create chorioretinal scar around break
✓ Indent the sclera to support retinal break externally
  Replace vitreous with silicone dye
  Drain subretinal fluid completely

Explanation:
Scleral buckling works by externally indenting the eye wall to close the retinal break, reducing the distance between the RPE and the detached retina.

Question 272 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Patient presented with swelling, dryness, loss of lash follicle, mass at lid margin, painless and immobile. Diagnosis:

Options:
✓ Chalazion
  Hordeolum
  Dermoid cyst
  Epidermoid cyst
  Sebaceous cyst

Explanation:
A Chalazion is a chronic, painless lipogranuloma of the meibomian gland. While 'loss of lash follicle' can imply malignancy, in the context of common exams, a firm, painless, immobile lid mass is typically identified as a Chalazion.

Question 273 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60-year-old male presented with sudden painless loss of vision. Fundus shows vitreous hemorrhage. Cause:

Options:
  Retinal detachment
  Central retinal artery occlusion
  Central retinal vein occlusion
✓ Proliferative diabetic retinopathy
  Central serous retinopathy

Explanation:
Spontaneous vitreous hemorrhage in a 60-year-old is most commonly caused by Proliferative Diabetic Retinopathy due to the rupture of fragile neovascular vessels.

Question 274 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 5-year-old with constant right eye deviation and poor vision; deviation is constant and visual acuity is 6/60 despite refractive correction. Cause of poor vision:

Options:
  Optic neuritis
  Retinal detachment
  Retinoblastoma
✓ Amblyopia

Explanation:
This is Strabismic Amblyopia. The constant deviation causes the brain to suppress the image from the deviating eye to avoid diplopia, leading to poor visual development in that eye.

A 5-year-old boy with white pupillary reflex and calcified fundus mass. Diagnosis:

✓ Retinoblastoma

  ROP
  Retinal detachment

Explanation:
The combination of leukocoria and calcification in a 5-year-old is highly specific for Retinoblastoma.

Question 276 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 34-year-old man presents with gradual painless vision loss, mass superotemporally in orbit compressing orbit inferonasally. CT shows smooth lobulated bony lesion. Diagnosis:

Options:
  Dermoid cyst
  Epidermoid cyst
✓ Lacrimal gland pleomorphic adenoma

Explanation:
The Lacrimal Gland is located in the superotemporal orbit. A 'smooth lobulated bony lesion' (fossa formation) with a gradual painless course is characteristic of a benign Pleomorphic Adenoma.

Question 277 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 25-year-old male presents with double vision for 2 days. On examination, his right eye shows ptosis and is deviated downwards and outwards. Pupillary reaction is normal. Most likely diagnosis:

Options:
  Complete 3rd nerve palsy
✓ Partial 3rd nerve palsy
  4th nerve palsy
  6th nerve palsy
  Myasthenia gravis

Explanation:
The findings are consistent with CN III palsy (ptosis, down and out deviation). The normal pupillary reaction (pupil sparing) distinguishes it as a partial palsy, often microvascular in origin (though less common in a 25yo, it rules out compression which affects the pupil).

Question 278 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 58-year-old hypertensive patient presented with blurring of vision. Fundus shows wedge-shaped area of superficial retinal hemorrhages limited to the superotemporal quadrant with macular edema. Diagnosis:

Options:
  CRVO
  CRAO
✓ BRVO
  Hypertensive retinopathy
  Proliferative diabetic retinopathy

Explanation:
The hemorrhages are sectoral (wedge-shaped) and limited to one quadrant, indicating the occlusion is in a branch vein (Branch Retinal Vein Occlusion), typically at an AV crossing.

================================================================================
Question 279 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
In pneumatic retinopexy, the retinal break should be located:

Options:
  Inferior
  Posterior to equator
✓ Superior within 1 hour of 12 o’clock
  At the ora serrata
  Near the macula

Explanation:
Pneumatic retinopexy uses an intravitreal gas bubble to tamponade the break. Since gas rises against gravity, the break must be located in the superior retina (ideally between 11 and 1 o'clock) for the bubble to cover it effectively.

================================================================================
Question 280 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 23-year-old woman has -4.00 Dsph and -2.00 Dcyl at 90°, 6/6 vision, normal cornea. Fundus shows slightly tilted optic disc. Best treatment option:

Options:
  Clear Lens Exchange
✓ LASIK
  Phakic Anterior Chamber IOL
  Phakic Posterior Chamber IOL
  Photorefractive Keratectomy (PRK)

Explanation:
For moderate myopia/astigmatism with a normal cornea and age, LASIK is the standard refractive procedure. Intraocular surgeries (Phakic IOL, CLE) are usually reserved for higher errors or corneas unsuitable for laser.

================================================================================
Question 281 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 30-year-old high myope presents with sudden onset floaters and flashes, followed by vision loss. Total retinal detachment with giant retinal tear. Procedure of choice:

Options:
  Cryotherapy
✓ Pars plana vitrectomy
  Photocoagulation
  Pneumatic retinopexy
  Scleral buckling

Explanation:
Giant Retinal Tears (tears >90 degrees or 3 clock hours) are complex and require Pars Plana Vitrectomy (PPV) to unroll the folded retinal flap, often utilizing perfluorocarbon liquids and tamponade.

Question 282 (KGMC - 2025)

Question:
A 65-year-old hypertensive male presents with sudden painless loss of vision in right eye. Fundus shows engorged veins, flame-shaped hemorrhages, cotton wool spots. Diagnosis:

Options:
✓ CRVO
  CRAO
  BRVO
  Diabetic retinopathy
  Hypertensive grade 4

Explanation:
The classic 'blood and thunder' fundus appearance (diffuse hemorrhages, engorged tortuous veins, CWS) is diagnostic of Central Retinal Vein Occlusion (CRVO).

Question 283 (KGMC - 2025)

Question:
A 45-year-old female presents with severe pain, redness, and colored halos around lights in the right eye. Examination reveals a cloudy cornea, shallow anterior chamber, and a fixed mid-dilated pupil. IOP is 50 mmHg. What is the diagnosis?

Options:
  Acute Anterior Uveitis
✓ Acute Congestive Glaucoma (Angle Closure)
  Neovascular Glaucoma
  Open Angle Glaucoma
  Viral Keratitis

Explanation:
The classic triad of symptoms (pain, red eye, halos) combined with signs like corneal edema (cloudy), shallow AC, fixed mid-dilated pupil, and very high IOP is diagnostic for Acute Angle Closure Glaucoma.

  Fungal Keratitis
  Bacterial Keratitis
  Herpes Zoster Ophthalmicus
  Adenoviral Keratoconjunctivitis

  Acute Conjunctivitis
✓ Acute Anterior Uveitis (Iridocyclitis)
  Acute Glaucoma
  Corneal Ulcer
  Scleritis

Question 288 (KGMC - 2025)

Question:
Three days after cataract surgery, a patient presents with severe pain and loss of vision. Examination shows lid edema, corneal haze, and hypopyon. The most likely diagnosis is:

Options:
  Post-operative Uveitis
✓ Endophthalmitis
  Expulsive Hemorrhage
  Toxic Anterior Segment Syndrome (TASS)
  Retinal Detachment

Explanation:
Acute postoperative bacterial endophthalmitis is a vision-threatening emergency presenting with pain, vision loss, and hypopyon (pus in AC) usually within the first week after surgery.

Question 289 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A factory worker splashes alkali into his right eye. The immediate management step at the site of injury should be:

Options:
  Check visual acuity
  Apply antibiotic ointment
✓ Copious irrigation with water
  Neutralize with mild acid
  Rush to hospital without touching the eye

Explanation:
Chemical injuries are the only true ocular emergency where treatment begins before history or exam. Immediate, copious irrigation with water/saline is vital to dilute and remove the chemical.

Question 290 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
An 8-month-old child is brought with complaints of photophobia, watering, and large hazy corneas. The most likely diagnosis is:

Options:
  Congenital Cataract
✓ Congenital Glaucoma
  Nasolacrimal Duct Obstruction
  Keratoconus
  Megalocornea

Explanation:
The classic triad of Congenital Glaucoma is epiphora (watering), photophobia, and blepharospasm. Elevated IOP causes the elastic infant cornea to enlarge (buphthalmos) and become hazy.

Question 291 (KGMC - 2025)

Question:
A patient presents with a triangular fibrovascular growth of conjunctiva encroaching onto the nasal side of the cornea. Diagnosis?

Options:
  Pinguecula
✓ Pterygium
  Phlycten
  Dermoid
  Squamous Cell Carcinoma

Explanation:
A Pterygium is a wing-shaped fibrovascular degeneration of the conjunctiva that grows across the limbus onto the cornea, typically on the nasal side due to UV exposure.

Question 292 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 22-year-old female complains of frequent changes in spectacle prescription and distorted vision. Retinoscopy shows a scissoring reflex and examination reveals Munson’s sign. Diagnosis?

Options:
  High Myopia
✓ Keratoconus
  Pathological Myopia
  Astigmatism
  Corneal Dystrophy

Explanation:
Keratoconus is a progressive thinning and ectasia of the cornea. Clinical signs include scissoring reflex on retinoscopy, Munson’s sign (V-shape of lower lid on downgaze), and Fleischer rings.

Question 293 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 30-year-old female presents with pain on eye movement and sudden loss of central vision in one eye. Examination reveals a relative afferent pupillary defect (RAPD) and normal fundus. Most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Papilledema
✓ Retrobulbar Neuritis
  Central Serous Retinopathy
  CRAO
  Anterior Ischemic Optic Neuropathy

Explanation:
Retrobulbar neuritis (a form of optic neuritis) typically presents in young adults with pain on eye movement, central vision loss, and RAPD. 'The patient sees nothing, and the doctor sees nothing' (normal fundus).

Question 294 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Which of the following findings is characteristic of Retinitis Pigmentosa?

Options:
  Cherry red spot
✓ Bone-spicule pigmentation
  Cotton wool spots
  Hard exudates
  Disc Neovascularization

Explanation:
Retinitis Pigmentosa is a hereditary dystrophy characterized by night blindness (nyctalopia) and the triad of: bone-spicule retinal pigmentation, arteriolar attenuation, and waxy disc pallor.

Question 295 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 70-year-old patient complains of gritty sensation and dryness in eyes. Schirmer’s test 1 shows <5 mm wetting in 5 minutes. Diagnosis?

Options:
  Chronic Dacryocystitis
✓ Keratoconjunctivitis Sicca (Dry Eye)
  Blepharitis
  Allergic Conjunctivitis
  Epiphora

Explanation:
Keratoconjunctivitis Sicca (Dry Eye Syndrome) is diagnosed when tear production is insufficient. A Schirmer's test result of less than 5mm in 5 minutes confirms severe aqueous deficiency.

Question 296 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A patient involved in a fistfight complains of double vision on looking up and numbness over the cheek. This suggests fracture of:

Options:
  Orbital roof
  Medial wall
✓ Orbital floor
  Lateral wall
  Optic canal

Explanation:
A 'blowout fracture' most commonly affects the orbital floor. Entrapment of the inferior rectus muscle causes diplopia on upgaze, and damage to the infraorbital nerve causes anesthesia of the cheek.

Question 297 (KGMC - 2025)

Question:
An elderly male presents with inward turning of the lower eyelid margin, causing lashes to rub against the cornea. This condition is called:

Options:
  Ectropion
✓ Entropion
  Trichiasis
  Distichiasis
  Ptosis

Explanation:
Entropion is the inversion (inward turning) of the eyelid margin. This often causes trichiasis (lashes rubbing the cornea), leading to irritation and potential ulceration.

Question 298 (KGMC - 2025)

A 45-year-old accountant complains of difficulty reading small print but has clear distance vision. This is due to:

Options:
  Myopia
  Hypermetropia
✓ Presbyopia
  Astigmatism
  Amblyopia

Explanation:
Presbyopia is the physiological loss of accommodation due to aging lens hardening, typically manifesting around age 40-45 as difficulty with near tasks (reading).

Question 300 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A mother brings her 3-year-old child noting a 'white reflex' in the right eye. Which of the following is NOT a differential diagnosis for Leukocoria?

Options:
  Retinoblastoma
  Congenital Cataract
  Retinopathy of Prematurity (ROP)
✓ Primary Open Angle Glaucoma
  Coats Disease

Explanation:
Leukocoria (white pupil) differentials include Retinoblastoma, Cataract, ROP, Coats disease, and Persistent Hyperplastic Primary Vitreous. POAG does not cause a white pupil; it is asymptomatic or causes field loss.

Question 301 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A young female presents with ptosis that worsens towards the evening and improves with an ice-pack test. The likely diagnosis is:

Options:
  Horner’s Syndrome
  3rd Nerve Palsy
✓ Myasthenia Gravis
  Congenital Ptosis
  Marcus Gunn Jaw Winking

Explanation:
Myasthenia Gravis is characterized by fatigable muscle weakness. Ptosis that worsens with fatigue (evening) and improves with cold (ice pack test inhibits acetylcholinesterase) is diagnostic.

Question 302 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50-year-old female complains of severe, boring pain in the eye, radiating to the forehead, which wakes her up at night. The eye has a deep violet hue. Diagnosis?

Options:
  Episcleritis
✓ Scleritis
  Conjunctivitis
  Iritis
  Glaucoma

Explanation:
Scleritis is a serious inflammation characterized by severe, boring pain (often disturbing sleep) and a deep red/violet discoloration. Episcleritis causes mild discomfort, not severe pain.

Question 303 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Bitot’s spots and night blindness in a malnourished child are indicative of deficiency of:

Options:
  Vitamin B12
  Vitamin C
✓ Vitamin A
  Vitamin D
  Vitamin E

Explanation:
Vitamin A deficiency causes Xerophthalmia. Early signs include nyctalopia (night blindness) and Bitot’s spots (foamy keratinized patches on the conjunctiva).

Question 304 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Sympathetic Ophthalmia is a bilateral granulomatous uveitis occurring after:

Options:
  Viral conjunctivitis
✓ Penetrating trauma to one eye
  Blunt trauma
  Chemical burn
  Laser surgery

Explanation:
Sympathetic Ophthalmia is a rare autoimmune reaction where penetrating injury to one eye (exciting eye) leads to inflammation in the non-injured fellow eye (sympathizing eye).

Question 305 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Painful swelling over the medial canthus with regurgitation of mucopurulent material on pressure suggests:

Options:
✓ Acute Dacryocystitis
  Chalazion
  Hordeolum
  Preseptal Cellulitis
  Dacryoadenitis

Explanation:
Dacryocystitis is infection of the lacrimal sac. It presents as a painful swelling at the medial canthus (sac area) and regurgitation of pus through the puncta when pressed.

Question 306 (KGMC - 2025)

Question:
Which dye is used intravenously to perform angiography for visualizing retinal leakage and neovascularization?

Options:
  Rose Bengal
  Lissamine Green
✓ Fluorescein Sodium
  Indocyanine Green
  Trypan Blue

Explanation:
Fundus Fluorescein Angiography (FFA) utilizes Fluorescein Sodium dye to image the retinal circulation and detect leakage (as in Diabetic Retinopathy or ARMD).

Question 307 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
According to the WHO classification, 'Blindness' is defined as best corrected visual acuity in the better eye of less than:

Options:
  6/18
  6/60
✓ 3/60
  1/60
  No Light Perception

Explanation:
WHO defines blindness as visual acuity of less than 3/60 (or counting fingers at 3 meters) in the better eye with best possible correction.

Question 308 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Bilateral swelling of the optic disc secondary to raised intracranial pressure is termed:

Options:
  Optic Neuritis
✓ Papilledema
  Optic Atrophy
  Glaucomatous cupping
  Drusen

Explanation:
Papilledema specifically refers to optic disc swelling caused by increased intracranial pressure (e.g., from a brain tumor or idiopathic hypertension). Other causes of swelling are just 'disc edema'.

Question 309 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
The most common complication of long-term topical corticosteroid use in the eye is:

Options:
  Retinal detachment
✓ Secondary Glaucoma and Cataract
  Corneal vascularization
  Uveitis
  Scleritis

Explanation:
Steroid responders can develop high IOP (Secondary Glaucoma). Long-term use also causes Posterior Subcapsular Cataracts. Infection potentiation is another risk.

Question 310 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 6-month-old infant has a constantly inturned eye (crossed eye). The corneal light reflex is displaced temporally. Diagnosis?

Options:
  Exotropia
✓ Esotropia
  Hypertropia
  Pseudostrabismus
  Phoria

Explanation:
Esotropia is the inward deviation of the eye. Hirschberg test (corneal light reflex) will show the reflection on the temporal side of the pupil in an esotropic eye.

Question 311 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Which of the following refractive conditions is characterized by light rays focusing *behind* the retina when accommodation is at rest?

Options:
  Myopia
✓ Hypermetropia
  Emmetropia
  Astigmatism
  Presbyopia

Explanation:
In Hypermetropia (farsightedness), the eyeball is too short or the cornea too flat, causing parallel rays of light to focus behind the retina.

Question 312 (KGMC - 2025)

Question:
The investigation of choice to measure the axial length of the eye for IOL power calculation (Biometry) is:

Options:
✓ A-Scan Ultrasonography
  B-Scan Ultrasonography
  Keratometry
  Perimetry
  Pachymetry

Explanation:
A-Scan (Amplitude Scan) ultrasound or optical biometry is used to measure axial length, which is a crucial variable in calculating the power of the Intraocular Lens (IOL) for cataract surgery.

Question 313 (KGMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A 30-year-old hockey player struck with a puck on the left side of his face complains of diplopia in the left eye. What is the diagnosis?

Options:
  a. Traumatic 3rd nerve palsy
  b. Traumatic 4th nerve palsy
  c. Orbital fracture
✓ d. Blowout fracture
  e. Retinal detachment

Explanation:
A history of blunt trauma to the orbit (hockey puck) causing diplopia is characteristic of an orbital blowout fracture, typically involving the orbital floor and entrapment of the inferior rectus muscle.

================================================================================
Question 314 (KGMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A 40-year-old woman presented with exophthalmos in both eyes associated with decreased vision. The vision in both eyes is 6/12. On examination, there is scleral show with restriction of extraocular movements along with hand tremors. The patient is not taking medications for any disease. What single investigation will you advise for this patient?

Options:
  a. Carotid Doppler
  b. Full blood count
  c. MRI orbit
  d. Peripheral smear
✓ e. Thyroid function tests

Explanation:
The clinical presentation of bilateral exophthalmos, restrictive myopathy (movement restriction), scleral show, and systemic signs like hand tremors is highly suggestive of Thyroid Eye Disease (Graves' ophthalmopathy). Thyroid function tests are the primary investigation.

================================================================================
Question 315 (KGMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Fluorescein angiography is a diagnostic procedure that uses fluorescence and a special camera to record blood flow in which structure?

Options:
✓ a. Retina
  b. Sclera
  c. Cornea
  d. Brain
  e. Heart

Explanation:
Fundus Fluorescein Angiography (FFA) is used to examine the circulation of the retina and choroid.

================================================================================
Question 316 (KGMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A 3-year-old has a left eye white pupil reflex and micro-ophthalmia since birth. The right eye is normal, with no calcification on a BT scan. What is the diagnosis?

Options:
  a. Congenital glaucoma
  b. Coats disease
✓ c. PHPV (Persistent Fetal Vasculature Syndrome)
  d. Retinoblastoma
  e. Retinopathy of prematurity

Explanation:
The triad of leukocoria (white pupil), microphthalmia (small eye), and absence of calcification on imaging is characteristic of Persistent Hyperplastic Primary Vitreous (PHPV), also known as Persistent Fetal Vasculature (PFV).

================================================================================
Question 317 (KGMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A 70-year-old male presents with bilateral lower lid inversion for 6 months. What is the diagnosis?

Options:
  a. Ptosis
✓ b. Entropion
  c. Ectropion
  d. Trichiasis
  e. Trachoma

Explanation:
Inversion (inward turning) of the eyelid margin is defined as Entropion.

================================================================================
Question 318 (KGMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
All are true about dermoids except?

Options:
  a. Located at the superolateral margin of the lid
✓ b. Common in early adulthood
  c. Present at birth
  d. Treatment is excision
  e. Excise in toto to prevent risk of granulomatous inflammation

Explanation:
Dermoid cysts are congenital choristomas, meaning they are present at birth and typically present in childhood, not early adulthood. They are commonly located at the superolateral orbital rim.

================================================================================
Question 319 (KGMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A patient has developed acute necrotizing scleritis associated with a systemic disease. What will be the mortality rate after the onset of scleritis in this patient?

Options:
  a. 15%
  b. 20%
✓ c. 25%
  d. 35%
  e. 45%

Explanation:
Necrotizing scleritis with associated systemic vasculitis (e.g., Rheumatoid Arthritis, GPA) carries a poor prognosis. Historically, the 5-year mortality rate is cited around 25% to 30% due to systemic complications.

================================================================================
Question 320 (KGMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A patient with homogentisate 1,2-dioxygenase enzyme deficiency was sent by a physician for an eye examination. On slit lamp examination, he had focal scleral discoloration near the horizontal recti insertion. What is the most common systemic association with this condition?

Options:
  a. Rheumatoid arthritis
  b. Ankylosing spondylitis
  c. Osteoarthritis
✓ d. Alkaptonuria
  e. Reactive arthritis

Explanation:
The enzyme deficiency described causes Alkaptonuria. The ocular sign is scleral pigmentation (Osler's sign). While the condition causes severe ochronotic arthropathy (resembling osteoarthritis), 'Alkaptonuria' is the name of the condition itself and is the most direct answer linking the enzyme and the signs.

================================================================================
Question 321 (KGMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A young boy from DI Khan came to the OPD with bilateral conjunctivitis associated with itching, burning, and lacrimation along with polygonal raised areas on the palpebra. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
✓ a. Vernal keratoconjunctivitis
  b. Viral conjunctivitis
  c. Mucopurulent conjunctivitis
  d. Trachoma
  e. Phylectenular conjunctivitis

Explanation:
The presence of 'polygonal raised areas' (giant papillae/cobblestone appearance) on the palpebral conjunctiva, combined with intense itching in a young boy, is diagnostic of Vernal Keratoconjunctivitis (VKC).

================================================================================
Question 322 (KGMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A patient with AIDS was sent by a physician to the eye OPD for anterior segment examination to exclude a low-grade malignancy. On slit lamp examination, there is a flat red lesion in the inferior fornix resembling subconjunctival hemorrhages. What is the most probable diagnosis?

Options:
  a. Limbal dermoid
  b. Ciliary staphyloma
  c. Pinguecula
✓ d. Kaposi sarcoma
  e. Lymphoma

Explanation:
Kaposi sarcoma is an AIDS-defining malignancy that often presents as a flat, red or purple vascular lesion on the conjunctiva or eyelids, mimicking a hemorrhage.

  b. Detect ARC after image test
  c. Detect eccentric fixation
  d. Assess anisocoria
  e. Assess physiological diplopia

The Synoptophore is an instrument used to assess binocular vision, measure the angle of deviation, and measure Angle Kappa.

Question 324 (KIMS - 2023)

Options:
✓ a. Endogenous allergy
  b. Exogenous allergy
  c. Degeneration
  d. None of the above

Explanation:
Phlyctenular conjunctivitis is typically a delayed hypersensitivity (Type IV) reaction to endogenous microbial proteins (e.g., Staph or TB).

Question 325 (KIMS - 2023)

  d. Phlyctenular conjunctivitis

This clinical picture (fever, pharyngitis, follicular conjunctivitis, lymphadenopathy) describes Pharyngoconjunctival Fever (PCF), caused by Adenovirus.

  b. Tear breakup time
  c. Rose Bengal staining

Question 330 (KIMS - 2023)

Question:
A patient is presented with a dendritic corneal ulcer and severe pain and has a history of contact lens wear. Which organism is most likely responsible for the condition?

Options:
✓ a. Herpes simplex
  b. Acanthamoeba
  c. Candida
  d. Both a and b

Explanation:
Dendritic ulcers are classic for Herpes Simplex Keratitis. While contact lens wear and severe pain are also associated with Acanthamoeba, the specific finding of a dendritic ulcer strongly points to HSV (Acanthamoeba causes pseudo-dendrites).

  b. Spherical shape

Question 334 (KIMS - 2023)

Bandage of the eyes is contraindicated in:

Options:

  b. Bacterial corneal ulcer
✓ c. Mucopurulent conjunctivitis
  d. After glaucoma surgery
  e. Inferior fornix

Question 335 (KIMS - 2023)

Papilloedema has all the following characteristics except:

Options:
✓ a. Marked loss of vision
  b. Blurring of disc margins
  c. Hyperemia of disc
  d. Field defect

Explanation:
In acute papilloedema, visual acuity is typically preserved. Marked loss of vision occurs in late stages (optic atrophy).

Question 336 (KIMS - 2023)

Homonymous hemianopia is the result of a lesion in:

Options:
  a. Optic chiasma
  b. Retina
✓ c. Optic tract
  d. Optic nerve

Explanation:
Lesions posterior to the chiasm (e.g., Optic Tract) cause contralateral homonymous hemianopia.

================================================================================
Question 337 (KIMS - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Lagophthalmos can occur in all of the following except:

Options:
  a. 7th cranial nerve paralysis
✓ b. 5th cranial nerve paralysis
  c. Thyrotoxic exophthalmos
  d. Symblepharon

Explanation:
5th nerve paralysis affects sensation (neurotrophic), not lid closure. 7th nerve paralysis (Bell's palsy) affects the orbicularis oculi, causing lagophthalmos.

================================================================================
Question 338 (KIMS - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
All the following are extraocular muscles of the eye except:

Options:
  a. Superior rectus
✓ b. Ciliary muscle
  c. Inferior oblique
  d. Superior oblique

Explanation:
The ciliary muscle is an intraocular smooth muscle involved in accommodation, not eye movement.

Question 339 (KIMS - 2023)

Question:
The action of inferior oblique is:

Options:
  a. Depression, extorsion, abduction
  b. Depression, extorsion, adduction
  c. Elevation, extorsion, adduction
✓ d. Elevation, extorsion, abduction

Explanation:
The Inferior Oblique acts to elevate, extort, and abduct the eye.

Question 340 (KIMS - 2023)

Question:
Ptosis and mydriasis are seen in:

Options:
  a. Facial palsy
  b. Peripheral neuritis
✓ c. Oculomotor palsy
  d. Sympathetic palsy

Explanation:
Oculomotor (CN III) palsy causes ptosis (levator paralysis) and mydriasis (parasympathetic loss).

================================================================================
Question 341 (KIMS - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
In paralytic squint, the difference between primary and secondary deviation in the gaze of direction of the paralytic muscle:

Options:
✓ a. Increases
  b. Decreases
  c. Remains the same
  d. None

Explanation:
In paralytic squint, secondary deviation (when the paretic eye fixes) is greater than primary deviation. The deviation increases in the field of action of the paralyzed muscle.

================================================================================
Question 342 (KIMS - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Paralytic ectropion occurs in:

Options:
  a. Third nerve paralysis
  b. Trigeminal nerve paralysis
✓ c. Facial nerve paralysis
  d. Trochlear nerve paralysis

Explanation:
Facial nerve (CN VII) paralysis causes loss of tone in the orbicularis oculi, leading to paralytic ectropion.

Question 344 (KIMS - 2023)

Question 346 (KIMS - 2023)

  d. Removal of orbit contents

Evisceration involves removing the intraocular contents while leaving the sclera and extraocular muscles intact.

Question 347 (KIMS - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A four-year-old boy is seen in the Emergency Department with an 8mm laceration of the upper lid not involving the lid margin. He says that he injured his eye on the corner of the table. He has mild ptosis on the left side and there is herniation of orbital fat through the laceration. The eye examination is normal. Which of the following is the next MOST appropriate step?

Options:
✓ a. CT of the orbit
  b. Glue the laceration
  c. MRI of the orbit
  d. Ultrasound the orbit

Explanation:
Herniation of orbital fat indicates a breach of the orbital septum. A CT scan is necessary to rule out foreign bodies or deeper orbital injury/fractures.

  b. Zygomatic (lateral wall)
  c. Lachrymal (medial wall)
  d. Frontal (roof)

The orbital floor (Maxillary bone) is the weakest and most commonly fractured in blowout fractures.

  d. 12

Question 358 (KIMS - 2023)

Question:
In CRAO, a cherry red spot is due to:

Options:

  c. Narrow angle
  d. Visual acuity and refractive error

Optic disc cupping (and field defects) is the hallmark structural damage in open-angle glaucoma. Shallow/narrow angles suggest angle-closure.

The average axial length of the adult human eye is approximately 24 mm.

Question 379 (KIMS - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Ciliary body helps in

Options:
  a. Maintaining structure of eye
  b. Focusing ability of eye
  c. Crystalline lens accommodation
✓ d. All of the above

Explanation:
The ciliary body produces aqueous (structure) and contains the ciliary muscle which controls accommodation (focusing).

Question 380 (KIMS - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
When the ciliary muscles contract, it results in

Options:
  a. Increased tension on zonular fibers
  b. Decreased tension on zonular fibers
  c. Decreased tension on the lens
✓ d. Both b and c

Explanation:
Contraction of the ciliary muscle moves the ciliary body forward/inward, relaxing the zonules (decreased tension), which allows the lens to become more spherical.

✓ c. Immediate wash with plain water
  d. Instilling a drop of surface anesthetic into the eye

Question 382 (KIMS - 2023)

Normal radius of curvature of the posterior corneal surface is:

Options:
  a. 7.8mm
✓ b. 6.8mm
  c. 7.9mm
  d. 6.0mm

Explanation:
The posterior corneal surface (approx 6.5-6.8mm) is steeper than the anterior surface (approx 7.8mm).

A patient has an upper lid trichiasis with a history of chronic eye irritation. The most common causes are:

  a. Stye
✓ b. Trachoma
  c. Infected chalazion
  d. Spring catarrh

Question 384 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 30 years old man presents to Eye OPD with diplopia and bilateral droopy eyelids which worsen in the evening. There is also a history of fatigability. On examination, his visual acuity is 6/6 in both eyes. Anterior and posterior segment examination is unremarkable. There is bilateral ptosis partially obstructing the visual axis and 20 prism diopter esotropia. Which of the following tests would you perform next?

Options:
  Chest X-ray
  CT head
✓ Ice pack test
  MRI brain
  Serum antibodies

Explanation:
The patient's symptoms of fatigable weakness, specifically ptosis and diplopia that worsen as the day progresses, are classic for ocular Myasthenia Gravis. The ice pack test is a simple, quick, and specific bedside test for this condition. Applying ice to the droopy eyelid for 2-5 minutes can temporarily improve the ptosis in myasthenic patients, as cold improves neuromuscular transmission.

Question 385 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
An 80-year-old Caucasian smoker woman complains of recent problems with reading vision, specifically words appearing distorted and blank patches being present. Her vision is often good in brighter light. On examination, her vision is 6/36 both eyes with no further improvement with refraction. Anterior segment examination is unremarkable. Pupils are reactive to light. Fundus shows numerous intermediate-size soft drusen in both eyes with choroidal neovascularization (CNV). How will you manage?

Options:
  Intravitreal antibiotics
✓ Intravitreal antiVEGF
  Multivitamins
  Subconjunctival decadron
  Subtenon triamcinolone

Explanation:
The diagnosis is wet (neovascular) age-related macular degeneration (AMD), confirmed by the presence of choroidal neovascularization (CNV). The standard of care and first-line treatment for wet AMD is intravitreal injections of anti-Vascular Endothelial Growth Factor (anti-VEGF) agents. These drugs inhibit the growth of abnormal blood vessels, reducing leakage and preserving vision.

Question 386 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 years old man presents to the Eye clinic with a 3 days history of left eye pain. On examination, her VA is CF in the left eye and 6/18 in the right eye. Left anterior chamber is deep and shows cells and flare. There are bilateral cataracts. The left cataract is hypermature and IOP in the same eye is 50mmHg. Which of the following is the likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Lens particle glaucoma
  Phacoanaphylactic glaucoma
  Phacoantigenic glaucoma
✓ Phacolytic glaucoma
  Phacomorphic glaucoma

Explanation:
Phacolytic glaucoma occurs when a hypermature cataract leaks soluble lens proteins through an intact lens capsule. These proteins are engulfed by macrophages, which then obstruct the trabecular meshwork, leading to acute open-angle glaucoma with very high IOP and inflammation. Phacomorphic glaucoma involves a swollen lens pushing the iris forward, causing angle closure.

================================================================================
Question 387 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 10 years old boy presents with dimness of vision in the right eye for a long time. On examination, his visual acuity is 6/36 in the right eye, there is right RAPD. Anterior segment examination is unremarkable. Right fundus shows optic atrophy. Laboratory workup is not significant. You are suspecting a space-occupying lesion. What is the most appropriate next step of investigation in this patient?

Options:
  CT
  CTA
  CTV
  MRA
✓ MRI

Explanation:
Magnetic Resonance Imaging (MRI) provides superior soft-tissue contrast compared to CT, making it the investigation of choice for visualizing the optic nerves, chiasm, and brain. In a child with unilateral optic atrophy, an MRI is essential to rule out a compressive lesion like an optic nerve glioma or craniopharyngioma.

Question 388 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 40 years old male came to OPD with sudden blurring of vision in his right eye. Visual acuity (VA) in right eye is 6/9 and VA in left eye is 6/6. On fundus examination, there are superior temporal venous engorgement, flame-shaped hemorrhages and cotton wool spots. Your diagnosis is branch retinal vein occlusion. What is the most appropriate treatment in this case?

Options:
  AntiVEGF
  Argon laser
  Intravitreal dexamethasone
  Intravitreal triamcinolone
✓ Observation

Explanation:
In a case of branch retinal vein occlusion (BRVO) where the visual acuity is still good (6/9) and there is no significant macular edema or neovascularization, the standard initial management is observation. Treatment with anti-VEGF or laser is typically initiated only if vision-threatening complications develop.

Question 389 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 42 years old man presented with watering and redness of right eye from the last 6 months. On examination there was restricted extraocular movements with lid lag and scleral show. The patient was found to have proptosis. What is the best way to measure the amount of his proptosis?

Options:
  B-Scan ultrasonography
  Computed Tomography (CT)
  Corneal topography
✓ Exophthalmo-metery
  Magnetic Resonance Imaging (MRI)

Explanation:
Exophthalmometry is the specific clinical method used to measure the degree of proptosis (forward protrusion of the eyeball). An instrument like the Hertel exophthalmometer is used to quantify the distance from the lateral orbital rim to the corneal apex, allowing for objective measurement and monitoring.

Question 390 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 3-old child is brought to Pediatric Ophthalmic OPD with leucokoria. Two of her siblings also have same problem. On examination, there are bilateral cataracts. There is no fundus view due to this media opacity. B-scan is normal. How will you manage?

Options:
  Glasses prescription
  Laser refractive surgery
✓ Lensectomy with intraocular lens implantation
  Observation
  Pars plana vitrectomy

Explanation:
Visually significant congenital or infantile cataracts are an ophthalmic emergency as they can lead to irreversible amblyopia (lazy eye). The definitive treatment is surgical removal of the cataractous lens (lensectomy), typically combined with primary intraocular lens (IOL) implantation in a 3-year-old child to correct the vision.

Question 391 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 years old man presents to you with a mass on the left lower eyelid for 10 years. On examination, there is a well-circumscribed mass on the lower eyelid with rolled edges and central ulceration. You are suspecting basal cell carcinoma (BCC). What is most common complication that can be expected in this patient?

Options:
  Hematogenous metastasis to the brain
  Hematogenous metastasis to the liver
  Hematogenous metastasis to the lungs
✓ Local invasion of skull and CNS
  Lymphatic metastasis

Explanation:
Basal cell carcinoma (BCC) is the most common malignancy of the eyelid. It is characterized by slow growth and local invasion. While metastasis is extremely rare, the most significant complication is its potential for direct, local invasion into the orbit, surrounding facial bones, skull, and central nervous system if left untreated.

Question 392 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 years male presented to you with sudden painless loss of vision in his right eye. Visual acuity in right eye is CF 4m while in left eye it is 6/6. On fundus examination, there were flame shaped hemorrhages in all quadrants, dilated tortuous vessels and swollen disc. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
  Anterior ischemic optic neuropathy
  Central retinal artery occlusion
✓ Central retinal vein occlusion
  Macular hole
  Retinal detachment

Explanation:
The characteristic fundus appearance described as 'blood and thunder'—involving hemorrhages in all four quadrants, venous dilation and tortuosity, and optic disc swelling—is pathognomonic for a Central Retinal Vein Occlusion (CRVO).

Question 393 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 19 years old boy presents to your clinic with droopy eyelids on right side. Visual acuities are 6/5 in the right eye and 6/6 in the left eye. On examination, there is mild right ptosis. On slit lamp examination you notice that the iris is slightly lighter in colour on the right side. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
  Aponeurotic ptosis
  Blepharophimosis syndrome
✓ Congenital horners syndrome
  Congenital myogenic ptosis
  Neurogenic ptosis

Explanation:
The combination of unilateral ptosis and iris heterochromia (lighter colored iris on the affected side) are key features of Congenital Horner's Syndrome. This condition results from a disruption of the sympathetic nerve supply to the eye during development.

Question 394 (KMC - 2023)

Question:
A 30-year-old male presented to you with red eyes and watering. Initially it involved right eye and after two days it spread to the left eye. His young daughter develops a similar problem other day. On examination, VA is 6/6. There is conjunctival redness with ropy discharge. Rest of the ocular examination is unremarkable. What is the most probable diagnosis?

Options:
  Allergic conjunctivitis
  Bacterial conjunctivitis
  Cicatricial conjunctivitis
  Fungal conjunctivitis
✓ Viral conjunctivitis

Explanation:
The history is classic for viral conjunctivitis: an acute onset, spreading from one eye to the other, a watery or ropy discharge (not purulent), and a history of a close contact (daughter) also being affected, indicating high contagiousness.

Question 395 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 30-year-old male presented with loss of vision in his left eye after trauma while left eye is normal. VA right eye is HM while 6/6 in left eye with right RAPD. On examination red reflex is absent in right eye, anterior segment is normal. There are pigmented cells in vitreous. Retina is elevated with tear in the superatemporal retina. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
  Exudative retinal detachment
  Macular hole
  Optic atrophy
✓ Rhegmatogenous retinal detachment
  Tractional retinal detachment

Explanation:
A rhegmatogenous retinal detachment is defined as a detachment caused by a full-thickness break (a hole or tear) in the retina, which allows vitreous fluid to seep underneath and separate the retina from the underlying tissue. The history of trauma and the finding of a retinal tear confirm this diagnosis.

Question 396 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 65 years old hypertensive patient is complaining of decrease vision in both eyes. You performed visual field examination on the patient and found right homonymous hemianopia which is confirmed on perimetry as well. Which of the following is the most probable site of lesion?

Options:
  Left optic nerve
✓ Left optic tract
  Optic chiasma
  Right optic tract
  Right optic nerve

Explanation:
A right homonymous hemianopia is the loss of the right visual field in both eyes. This type of visual field defect is caused by a lesion in the visual pathway behind the optic chiasm on the contralateral (opposite) side. Therefore, the lesion must be in the left optic tract, left optic radiations, or left visual cortex. The most anterior of these is the left optic tract.

Question 397 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 48 years diabetic patient presented with diplopia from the last 5 days. On examination, you find that the patient is having left hypertropia with a head tilt to the right side. On extraocular motility, the hypertropia increases in right gaze. What is the most probable diagnosis?

Options:
  Right inferior oblique palsy
  Right superior oblique palsy
  Left inferior rectus palsy
  Left inferior oblique palsy
✓ Left superior oblique palsy

Explanation:
This clinical scenario fits the Parks-Bielschowsky three-step test for a superior oblique palsy. The left hypertropia (step 1) which worsens on contralateral gaze (right gaze, step 2) and is typically associated with a contralateral head tilt (right tilt, step 3) is characteristic of a left superior oblique (IVth nerve) palsy.

Question 398 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50 years old woman presents to Eye OPD with severe pain and dimness of vision in her right eye. She has a history of right phacoemulsification with intraocular lens implantation 05 days ago. On examination, her visual acuity is 6/60 in the right eye with eyelid swelling, conjunctival congestion and chemosis. There is fibrinous exudate and hypopyon in the right eye. Fundus view is hazy. Which is the most likely involved pathogen?

Options:
  Proteus spp.
  Pseudomonas spp.
  S. aureus
✓ S. epidermidis
  Streptococcus spp.

Explanation:
The presentation is classic for acute post-operative endophthalmitis. The most common causative organisms are bacteria from the patient's own ocular surface flora. Coagulase-negative staphylococci, specifically *Staphylococcus epidermidis*, are the most frequently isolated pathogens in this setting.

Question 399 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 15 years old girl presents to the eye clinic with a 2 days history of blurring in the right eye with pain on ocular movements. Examination reveals a visual acuity of 6/36 in the right eye and 6/6 in the left eye. There is RAPD. Fundus examination shows right swollen optic disc. Left fundus is normal. Systemic history and examination are not significant. What is the most appropriate diagnosis?

Options:
  Acute angle closure glaucoma
  Anterior ischemic optic neuropathy
  Cavernous sinus thrombosis
  Giant cell arteritis
✓ Optic neuritis

Explanation:
The classic triad of acute unilateral vision loss, pain with eye movements, and a relative afferent pupillary defect (RAPD) in a young person is pathognomonic for optic neuritis. The swollen optic disc indicates papillitis, a common finding in this condition.

Question 400 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
An 80 years old female presented to Eye OPD with sudden onset painful right eye with decreased vision and associated nausea. On examination she has right corneal edema with mid dilated pupil and IOP of 60 mmHg. Left eye is normal except having shallow anterior chamber. What is the immediate next step of management?

Options:
✓ Intravenous Mannitol
  Laser iridotomy in right eye
  Oral steroids
  Topical antibiotics in right eye
  Trabeculectomy in right eye

Explanation:
This is a classic presentation of acute angle-closure glaucoma, an ophthalmic emergency. The immediate goal is to lower the extremely high intraocular pressure (IOP) to prevent irreversible optic nerve damage. Intravenous mannitol is a hyperosmotic agent that rapidly reduces IOP by drawing fluid out of the vitreous humor.

Question 401 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 5 years old child went on spring vacations to his village and was playing cricket. He got cricket ball injury and developed decreased vision in left eye. On slit lamp examination, there is blood in anterior chamber. What is this condition called as?

Options:
  Endophthalmitis
✓ Hyphaema
  Hypopyon
  Keratitis
  Uveitis

Explanation:
Hyphaema is the medical term for blood collecting in the anterior chamber of the eye, the space between the cornea and the iris. It is a common result of blunt trauma, such as being hit by a ball.

Question 402 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 58-year-old male presented with pain, dimness of vision and watering in left eye. On examination, he has visual acuity of 6/6 in right eye and 6/60 in his left eye. He has a 2.3x2.8mm corneal ulcer. Which of the following measures should be avoided in this patient?

Options:
  Antibiotics
  Antifungals
✓ Bandage contact lens
  Cycloplegics
  Irrigation with saline

Explanation:
In the case of a suspected infectious corneal ulcer, placing a bandage contact lens (BCL) is contraindicated. A BCL can trap microorganisms against the cornea, creating a warm, moist environment that can accelerate microbial replication and worsen the infection.

Question 403 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 56 years old diabetic patient presented with decrease vision in both eyes from the last one year. On examination, the anterior segment was normal. Fundoscopy showed retinal hemorrhages in all 4 quadrants with diabetic macular edema. How you will measure the amount of macular edema in this patient?

Options:
  B-Scan ultrasonography
  Fundus autofluorescence (FAF)
  Fundus Fluorescein angiography (FFA)
✓ Optical coherence tomography (OCT)
  Pachymetry

Explanation:
Optical Coherence Tomography (OCT) is the gold standard for diagnosing and quantifying diabetic macular edema. It provides high-resolution, cross-sectional images of the retina, allowing for precise measurement of retinal thickness and visualization of intraretinal fluid.

Question 404 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 52-year-old hypertensive patient presented with sudden onset diplopia and face turn to the left. On examination there is left abduction deficit. The rest of extraocular movements are normal. Which of the following nerve is involved in this condition?

Options:
  2nd nerve
  3rd nerve
  4th nerve
  5th nerve
✓ 6th nerve

Explanation:
The 6th cranial nerve (Abducens nerve) innervates the lateral rectus muscle, which is solely responsible for abduction (outward movement) of the eye. A deficit in abduction is a classic sign of a 6th nerve palsy. The face turn is a compensatory mechanism to avoid diplopia.

Question 405 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 2 weeks old infant is brought to eye OPD with white pupillary reflex in both eyes since birth. He is systemically normal. On examination, child cannot fix and follow. There are bilateral cataracts and microcorneas. There is no view of posterior segment. B-scans are normal. What will be your next step of management?

Options:
  Corneal topography
  CT brain
  Mantoux test
  OCT macula
✓ TORCH serology

Explanation:
In an infant with bilateral congenital cataracts, it is crucial to investigate for an underlying systemic cause before proceeding with surgery. Congenital infections are a significant cause, and a TORCH serology panel (Toxoplasmosis, Other, Rubella, Cytomegalovirus, Herpes) is a standard and essential part of the workup.

Question 406 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 09-year-old boy has 30 prism diopter of left esotropia. On full-time wear of +5 diopter glasses, the deviation decreased to 15 prism diopter. What is the next treatment option at this stage?

Options:
  Contact Lenses
  Low vision devices
  Multivitamin Syrup
✓ Surgery for remaining squint
  Telescopes

Explanation:
This patient has a partially accommodative esotropia. The glasses correct the accommodative component of the squint, but a residual non-accommodative deviation of 15 prism diopters remains. This residual squint requires surgical correction (strabismus surgery) to align the eyes.

Question 407 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
An 18 years old girl had severe headaches since six months, along with defective vision on temporal sides of her visual fields. What is the most probably diagnosis?

Options:
  Occipital infarct
  Optic nerves glioma
  Optic neuritis
  Optic tract glioma
✓ Pituitary adenoma

Explanation:
The loss of vision on the temporal (outer) sides of both visual fields is known as a bitemporal hemianopia. This specific visual field defect is the classic sign of a lesion compressing the optic chiasm. The pituitary gland is located directly beneath the chiasm, making a pituitary adenoma the most common cause.

Question 408 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 40-year-old lady complains of left eye redness of 2 days duration associated with photophobia and watery discharge. On exam, visual acuity is 6/12 both eyes. Cornea reveals dendritic lesion that stain well with fluorescein, Corneal sensations are reduced in left eye. Which of the following is the most appropriate management?

Options:
  Artificial tears
✓ Topical antivirals
  Topical cyclosporine
  Topical NSAIDS
  Topical steroids

Explanation:
A dendritic ulcer on the cornea that stains with fluorescein, combined with reduced corneal sensation, is pathognomonic for Herpes Simplex Virus (HSV) keratitis. The mainstay of treatment is a topical antiviral agent (e.g., ganciclovir gel). Topical steroids are strongly contraindicated as they can worsen the infection.

Question 409 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 55-year-old patient presented with decreased vision in the right eye from the last 3 days associated with watering and foreign body sensation. There is also pustules and blisters involving the right side of forehead, lid up to the tip of the nose with severe pain and tingling sensation. Ocular examination shows corneal dendritic ulceration. What is the most probable diagnosis in this case?

Options:
  Atopic keratoconjunctivitis
  Fungal infection
  Herpes simplex infection
✓ Herpes zoster ophthalmicus
  Steven Johnson syndrome

Explanation:
The unilateral, dermatomal vesicular rash involving the forehead and tip of the nose (Hutchinson's sign) is characteristic of shingles in the ophthalmic division of the trigeminal nerve. This condition, known as Herpes Zoster Ophthalmicus (HZO), frequently involves the eye, causing conditions like keratitis (dendritic ulcer).

Question 410 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A young man recently shifted to a new home and was doing white wash of the walls. Suddenly he slipped and the white wash fell in his both eyes. What immediate treatment is needed?

Options:
✓ Copious wash of eyes with water/saline
  Pad both eyes
  Put local anesthesia eye drops in eyes
  Start antibiotic eye drops
  Start antiglaucoma medicines

Explanation:
White wash contains lime, which is a strong alkali. Alkali chemical burns are an ophthalmic emergency. The single most important and immediate step is to dilute and remove the chemical by performing copious and prolonged irrigation of the eyes with any clean water available, or sterile saline if accessible.

Question 411 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 30-year old female presented to eye OPD with sudden onset redness in right eye (RE) with photophobia and decreased vision. Systemic history is positive for low back pain. On examination, her visual acuity is 6/18 in RE. There is conjunctival congestion, keratic precipitates (kps) and +4 cells in anterior chamber. Fundus examination is unremarkable. What is the primary treatment of this patient?

Options:
  Oral antibiotics
  Oral steroids
  Subtenon steroids
  Topical antibiotics
✓ Topical steroids

Explanation:
The patient has acute anterior uveitis, characterized by inflammation in the anterior chamber (cells, KPs). The primary goal of treatment is to control this inflammation to prevent complications like synechiae and glaucoma. Intensive topical corticosteroids are the first-line and mainstay of treatment.
