Question 412 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 years old man presented to eye OPD with transient visual loss in left eye that lasts for few minutes. These episodes occur several times a day. Ocular examination is unremarkable. Which of the following is most appropriate initial investigation?

Options:
  Chest X-ray
  CT brain
✓ Electrocardiogram
  Renal function tests
  Thyroid function tests

Explanation:
The symptom described is amaurosis fugax, a form of transient ischemic attack (TIA) affecting the eye. It is often caused by emboli from the carotid arteries or the heart. A standard workup for TIA includes investigating the heart for sources of emboli, such as atrial fibrillation. An Electrocardiogram (ECG) is a crucial initial test for this purpose.

Question 413 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 30-years old farmer presented to you with pain and redness in right eye for the last 02 days. On inquiry he had trauma to his right eye with a bush of tree. On examination, visual acuity in his right eye is 6/36. He has a 2.5x3mm corneal ulcer with satellite lesions. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
  Acanthamoeba keratitis
  Bacterial conjunctivitis
✓ Fungal keratitis
  Marginal keratitis
  Viral keratitis

Explanation:
The combination of a history of trauma with vegetative matter (a tree bush) and the clinical finding of satellite lesions surrounding a main corneal infiltrate is highly characteristic of fungal keratitis. This presentation should be considered a fungal infection until proven otherwise.

Question 414 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 35 years old obese woman presented to eye OPD with severe headache especially in morning. It worsens on bending forward. She also complains of visual obscuration lasting few seconds. She is taking oral contraceptive pills for the last 1 year. She is non-diabetic and non-hypertensive. On examination, her visual acuity is 6/6. There is no RAPD. Anterior segment examination is unremarkable. Fundus examination shows, bilateral swollen discs with hemorrhages and exudates. What will be your next step of management?

Options:
  CT brain
✓ Lumber puncture
  MRI brain
  Oral acetazolamide
  Oral topiramate

Explanation:
The patient's presentation is classic for Idiopathic Intracranial Hypertension (IIH). The first step is neuroimaging (MRI is preferred over CT) to rule out a mass lesion. Once a mass is ruled out, a lumbar puncture (LP) is the essential next step to confirm the diagnosis by measuring an elevated opening pressure and to analyze the cerebrospinal fluid.

Question 415 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 6 months old infant is brought by parents with complaint of watering, redness, photophobia and blepharospasm. On examination, he has nystagmus with enlarged hazy cornea. Fundus examination is not possible due to poor view. IOP is 30 in both eyes under sedation. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
  Conjunctivitis
✓ Congenital glaucoma
  Congenital myopia
  Congenital nasolacrimal duct obstruction
  Congenital rubella keratitis

Explanation:
The classic triad of symptoms (epiphora, photophobia, blepharospasm) combined with the signs of elevated intraocular pressure (enlarged, hazy cornea known as buphthalmos) in an infant is pathognomonic for primary congenital glaucoma.

Question 416 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 4-year-old girl has convergent squint of left eye. On examination she has left amblyopia. What is the best treatment option for her?

Options:
  Glasses
  Multivitamins
  Observation
✓ Patch therapy
  Surgery

Explanation:
The primary goal before correcting the squint is to treat the amblyopia (lazy eye). The standard and most effective treatment for amblyopia is occlusion therapy, which involves patching the better-seeing eye to force the brain to use and develop the neural pathways for the amblyopic eye.

Question 417 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 30-year-old female presented to eye OPD with sudden onset redness in right eye (RE) with photophobia and decreased vision. Systemic history is positive for low back pain. On examination, her visual acuity is 6/18 in RE. There is conjunctival congestion, keratic precipitates (kps) and +4 cells in the anterior chamber. Fundus examination is unremarkable. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
✓ Anterior uveitis
  Endophthalmitis
  Intermediate uveitis
  Panuveitis
  Posterior uveitis

Explanation:
The presence of inflammatory signs confined to the anterior segment—specifically keratic precipitates (KPs) on the endothelium and inflammatory cells in the anterior chamber—defines the condition as anterior uveitis. The association with low back pain suggests a possible systemic inflammatory condition like ankylosing spondylitis.

Question 418 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 5 years old child is brought by her mother to you with deviation of right eye since birth. On Hirschberg test, the light reflex was falling on inner (nasal) border of right pupil. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
  Esotropia 15 degrees
  Esotropia 15 prism diopters
✓ Exotropia 15 degrees
  Exotropia 15 prism diopters
  Hypertropia 15 prism diopter

Explanation:
In the Hirschberg test, the corneal light reflex is assessed. If the eye is deviated outward (exotropia), the light reflex will be displaced inward (nasally) from the center of the pupil. A reflex falling on the border of the pupil corresponds to a deviation of approximately 15 degrees.

Question 419 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 04 months old infant is brought by parents to Eye OPD with white pupillary reflex in right eye since birth. Infant is not fixing and following light with right eye. EUA reveals rubeosis irides and a retrolental mass with vitreous seeds in right eye. Intraocular pressure is 35 in right eye and 8 in left eye. Left eye examination is unremarkable. MRI shows no invasion of optic nerve. How will you treat this infant?

Options:
  Chemotherapy
  Cryotherapy
✓ Enucleation
  Laser photocoagulation
  Radiotherapy

Explanation:
The findings are classic for advanced, unilateral retinoblastoma. Given the poor visual potential, vitreous seeding, and secondary glaucoma (high IOP with rubeosis irides), the safest and most definitive treatment is enucleation (surgical removal of the eye). This is life-saving as it prevents the tumor from spreading outside the eye.

================================================================================
Question 420 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 70 years old woman came to OPD with complaints of sudden decrease in vision in left eye with associated headache. The patient was also complaining of jaw claudication and pain on combing hair. On examination, her visual acuity is counting finger in left eye with RAPD and swollen optic disc. Her ESR was 60. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
✓ Arteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy
  Neuroretinitis
  Non-Arteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy
  Optic neuritis
  Posterior ischemic optic neuropathy

Explanation:
The combination of systemic symptoms (headache, jaw claudication, scalp tenderness) with acute, severe vision loss, a swollen optic disc, and a significantly elevated Erythrocyte Sedimentation Rate (ESR) in an elderly patient is the classic presentation of Arteritic Anterior Ischemic Optic Neuropathy (A-AION) caused by Giant Cell Arteritis (GCA).

================================================================================
Question 421 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50 years old man presented to you with watering and redness in both eyes for the last 01 year. He had a previous history of cicatricial conjunctivitis. On examination, you found inward rotation of lower eyelids. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
  Lower lid ectropion
✓ Lower lid entropion
  Lower lid epiblepharon
  Lower lid ptosis
  Lower lid trichiasis

Explanation:
Entropion is the medical term for the inward turning of the eyelid margin. This causes the eyelashes to rub against the globe, leading to chronic irritation, redness, and watering. It can be a consequence of scarring (cicatricial) changes to the conjunctiva.

================================================================================
Question 422 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 year uncontrolled diabetic woman presented with sudden decrease vision in right eye. On examination, there are bilateral cataracts. There is no RAPD. Fundus view is not clear. What investigation will you advice to assess any associated posterior segment pathology?

Options:
✓ B-Scan ultrasonography
  Fundus autoflourescence
  Fundus fluorescein angiography
  Optical coherence tomography
  Retinal photography

Explanation:
When the view of the posterior segment is obscured by a dense media opacity like a mature cataract, B-scan ultrasonography is the investigation of choice. It uses sound waves to create a two-dimensional image of the inside of the eye, allowing for the detection of pathologies like retinal detachment or vitreous hemorrhage.

Question 423 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 56 years old man presented with gradual progressive decrease vision in the right from last 8 months. On examination the vision is 6/60 in right eye and 6/9 in left eye with no improvement with pinhole. Fundus examination is hazy but seems to be normal. What is the most probable diagnosis?

Options:
  Age-related macular degeneration
✓ Cataract
  Diabetic retinopathy
  Glaucoma
  Vitreous hemorrhage

Explanation:
The history of a gradual, progressive, and painless decline in vision that does not improve with a pinhole is the classic presentation of a cataract. The cataract itself is the media opacity that would cause the fundus examination to be hazy.

================================================================================
Question 424 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 65 years old man presented with decreased vision in both eyes from the last 2 years. On examination, he has a visual acuity of CF 3 meters in both eyes. On examination anterior segment is normal in both eyes and there is retinal pigment epithelial atrophy with scars in the macular region. He is non-diabetic and non-hypertensive. What is the most probable diagnosis?

Options:
✓ Age-related macular degeneration
  Central serous chorioretinopathy
  Exudative retinal detachment
  Lattice degeneration
  Retinitis pigmentosa

Explanation:
The clinical profile of an elderly patient with bilateral, progressive central vision loss and fundus findings of atrophy and scarring confined to the macula is characteristic of atrophic (dry) Age-related Macular Degeneration (AMD).

================================================================================
Question 425 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 35 years old male presented to Eye OPD with sudden loss of vision in his right eye. He has a history of trauma to the right eye with a cricket ball 1 month ago. On examination, his visual acuity is 6/36 in right eye and 6/6 in left eye. He is having right eye RAPD. What is the likely cause of his right eye RAPD?

Options:
  Cataract
  Episcleritis
  Keratitis
✓ Retinal detachment
  Scleritis

Explanation:
A Relative Afferent Pupillary Defect (RAPD) indicates significant damage to the afferent visual pathway (retina or optic nerve). Following severe blunt trauma, a traumatic retinal detachment is a common cause of profound vision loss and would result in widespread retinal dysfunction, leading to a RAPD.

================================================================================
Question 426 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 45 years old labourer presented to your clinic with complain of watering and irritation, more frequently during sun exposure. On slit lamp examination, you observe a wedge-shaped growth of the conjunctiva that extends onto the nasal cornea. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
  Limbal Dermoid
  Nodular Phlyctenule
  Pinguecula
✓ Pterygium
  Scleritis

Explanation:
A pterygium is a fibrovascular growth of the conjunctiva that is typically triangular or wedge-shaped and extends onto the cornea, most commonly from the nasal side. It is strongly associated with chronic exposure to ultraviolet light, dust, and wind.

================================================================================
Question 427 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 65 years old woman presented with pain in left eye since yesterday. She is giving history of left phacoemulsification 3 days back. On examination the vision is counting fingers in left eye with conjunctival congestion and corneal edema along with hypopyon in the anterior chamber. What is the most appropriate treatment?

Options:
✓ Intravitreal antibiotics
  Peribulbar antibiotics
  Subtenon antibiotics
  Systemic antibiotics
  Topical antibiotics

Explanation:
This is a classic case of acute post-operative endophthalmitis, an ophthalmic emergency. The standard of care is immediate intervention with a vitreous tap for cultures followed by the injection of broad-spectrum antibiotics directly into the vitreous cavity (intravitreal injection) to achieve high, therapeutic drug levels at the site of infection.

================================================================================
Question 428 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 years old man presents to you with sudden loss of vision in his left eye. He is a known cardiac patient. On examination, VA is PL+ in left eye. Anterior segment is normal while fundus shows white cloudy retina with cherry red spot. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
  Branch retinal artery occlusion
  Branch retinal vein occlusion
✓ Central retinal artery occlusion
  Central retinal vein occlusion
  Hemiretinal vein occlusion

Explanation:
The funduscopic finding of a diffusely pale, ischemic retina with a contrasting 'cherry-red spot' at the macula is pathognomonic for a Central Retinal Artery Occlusion (CRAO). The cherry-red appearance is due to the intact choroidal circulation showing through the thin fovea against the backdrop of the opacified retina.

================================================================================
Question 429 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 55 years old female patient presented with decrease vision in the left eye along with complaint of watering from left eye. On examination, you found that the patient is having mature cataract in the left eye with positive regurgitation test of mucopurulent discharge on same side. Which procedure you will prefer to do first?

Options:
  Both cataract and DCR surgery in the same setting
  Cataract surgery alone
✓ Dacryocystorhinostomy Surgery alone
  Give antibiotics for chronic dacryocystitis and do cataract surgery
  No surgical intervention at the moment

Explanation:
Performing intraocular surgery (like cataract extraction) in the presence of an active infection in the nasolacrimal system (chronic dacryocystitis) carries a very high risk of sight-threatening postoperative endophthalmitis. It is an absolute requirement to resolve the infection first, which is done surgically with a dacryocystorhinostomy (DCR). The cataract surgery can be performed at a later date.

================================================================================
Question 430 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50 years old woman presents to you with gradual onset dimness of vision in both eyes for the last 01 year. She is known diabetic for the last 10 years. On examination, her visual acuity is 6/24 in both eyes. Anterior segment examination is within normal limits. Fundus examination shows proliferative diabetic retinopathy with macular edema. How will you treat this patient?

Options:
✓ AntiVEGF
  Cryotherapy
  Diffuse laser
  Focal laser
  Pars plana vitrectomy

Explanation:
This patient has two vision-threatening conditions: proliferative diabetic retinopathy (PDR) and diabetic macular edema (DME). Intravitreal anti-VEGF therapy is a first-line treatment that effectively addresses both problems. It reduces macular edema and can also cause regression of the abnormal new blood vessels associated with PDR.

================================================================================
Question 431 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60-year-old male presented to you with sudden painless loss of vision in his right eye. VA in right eye is CF while it is 6/6 in left eye. On fundus examination, there were flame-shaped hemorrhages in all quadrants, dilated tortuous vessels and swollen disc. Which complication will you expect in this patient at 3 months after the event?

Options:
  Angle closure glaucoma
  Choroidal detachment
✓ Neovascular glaucoma
  Open angle glaucoma
  Retinal detachment

Explanation:
The patient has a Central Retinal Vein Occlusion (CRVO). A severe, ischemic CRVO leads to retinal hypoxia, which stimulates the production of VEGF. This can lead to the growth of new, abnormal blood vessels on the iris and in the angle, causing a severe secondary glaucoma known as neovascular glaucoma. This complication typically develops around 90 days (3 months) after the initial event.

================================================================================
Question 432 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 48-year-old woman came to you with difficulty in reading. She is non-diabetic and non-hypertensive. On examination, VA in both eyes is 6/6 for distance. Ocular examination is unremarkable. What is the cause of her reduced near vision?

Options:
  Amblyopia
  Astigmatism
  Hypermetropia
  Myopia
✓ Presbyopia

Explanation:
Presbyopia is the normal, age-related loss of the eye's ability to focus on near objects. It typically starts in the early to mid-40s and is caused by the hardening of the lens. The patient's age and symptom of isolated difficulty with near vision while distance vision remains clear are classic for presbyopia.

================================================================================
Question 433 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 12 years old child was brought to you with complain of irritation of eyes that is most severe in summer season and recurrent. On examination, the child is having VA of 6/12 in right eye and 6/9 in left eye. On eversion of lid you find giant papillae, conjunctival congestion most marked in the limbal region with associated pseudogerontoxon. What is the most probable diagnosis in this case?

Options:
  Adenoviral conjunctivitis
  Bacterial conjunctivitis
  Gonococcal conjunctivitis
  Trachoma
✓ Vernal keratoconjunctivitis

Explanation:
The combination of seasonal (summer) recurrence, intense itching, and the classic signs of giant 'cobblestone' papillae on the upper tarsal conjunctiva and limbal inflammation are pathognomonic for Vernal Keratoconjunctivitis (VKC), a severe form of allergic eye disease.

================================================================================
Question 434 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 16-year-old boy is brought to you with trauma by scissor when he was playing with it. On examination, you found that his cornea is perforated. What is your 1st step of management as a community doctor?

Options:
  Repairing of the cornea
  Refer the patient without doing anything
  Steroid ointment, then refer the patient
  Topical steroids then refer the patient
✓ Topical antibiotics, pad the eye then refer

Explanation:
In a case of an open globe injury (corneal perforation), the immediate priorities for a non-specialist are to prevent infection and protect the eye from further damage. This involves applying a topical broad-spectrum antibiotic, placing a protective shield (or a very gentle pad) over the eye without applying pressure, and arranging for immediate referral to an ophthalmologist for surgical repair.

================================================================================
Question 435 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 65-year-old patient presented with vertical diplopia for the last week. She has a history of road traffic accident 01 week ago. On examination, vision in both eyes is 6/6. There is hypertropia of right eye and the patient cannot depress the eye in adduction. Which nerve is most probably involved?

Options:
  Abducent nerve
  Nasociliary nerve
  Oculomotor nerve
  Optic nerve
✓ Trochlear nerve

Explanation:
The superior oblique muscle is responsible for depressing the eye when it is in the adducted (turned in) position. This muscle is innervated by the trochlear nerve (IVth cranial nerve). An inability to depress the adducted eye is the classic sign of a trochlear nerve palsy, which is common after head trauma due to the nerve's long intracranial course.

================================================================================
Question 436 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 58 years old man underwent uneventful phacoemulsification surgery for cataract in his right eye. After 3 days, he presented with severe pain and loss of vision in the right eye. On examination, he was having VA of perception of light in right eye and 6/9 in the left eye. He has congested conjunctiva, hazy cornea and hypopyon with no view of posterior segment of the eye. What is the most probable diagnosis?

Options:
  Acute angle closure glaucoma
  Loss of lens fragments into posterior segment of the eye
✓ Post-operative endophthalmitis
  Post-operative uveitis
  Post-operative vitreous hemorrhage

Explanation:
The acute onset of severe pain, profound vision loss, and intense intraocular inflammation (indicated by a hypopyon) within days of an intraocular surgery like cataract extraction is the classic presentation of post-operative endophthalmitis, a severe infection inside the eye.

================================================================================
Question 437 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 47 years old man presented with ocular pain in the right eye from the last 1 week. There is redness of the right eye with associated pain. On phenylephrine eye drops installation the redness is not reduced. The patient is also giving a history of rheumatoid arthritis. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
  Blepharitis
  Conjunctivitis
  Corneal ulcer
  Episcleritis
✓ Scleritis

Explanation:
Scleritis is characterized by deep, boring ocular pain and redness. A key diagnostic sign is that the redness, which originates from the deep episcleral vascular plexus, does not blanch with the application of a topical vasoconstrictor like phenylephrine. There is also a strong association between scleritis and systemic autoimmune diseases like rheumatoid arthritis.

================================================================================
Question 438 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#1. Eyelid margin anatomy distortion and loss of eyelashes should alert one to the possibility of what following?

Options:
  Facial nerve (VII) palsy
  Chronic eyelid webbing
  Involuntional ectropion
✓ Malignancy
  Senile changes

Explanation:
Distortion of the eyelid margin anatomy (e.g., notching) and madarosis (loss of eyelashes) are classic warning signs of a malignant tumor, such as basal cell carcinoma or sebaceous gland carcinoma, infiltrating and destroying local tissue structures.

================================================================================
Question 439 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#2. A 2-year-old male child is brought to you with a complaint of drooping of the right eyelid since birth. His eye is half open during sleep, there is an absent lid crease, amblyopia, and poor levator function. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
✓ Congenital Ptosis
  Jaw winking ptosis
  Aponeurotic Ptosis
  Traumatic Ptosis
  Myasthenia Gravis

Explanation:
The key features—ptosis present since birth, an absent or poorly formed eyelid crease, and poor function of the levator palpebrae superioris muscle—are the classic triad for congenital ptosis, which results from developmental dystrophy of the levator muscle.

================================================================================
Question 440 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#3. In a Dacryocystorhinostomy (DCR) operation, the window in the lateral nasal wall is made at the:

Options:
  Ethmoidal sinus
✓ Middle meatus
  Superior meatus
  Inferior meatus
  Lacrimal fossa

Explanation:
DCR surgery creates a new drainage pathway for tears by making a connection (anastomosis) between the lacrimal sac and the nasal cavity through a bony opening (osteotomy) into the middle meatus.

================================================================================
Question 441 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#4. A young lady with blepharitis presents with a swelling on the right upper lid for the last one month. The most common painless lid swelling is:

Options:
✓ Chalazion
  Stye
  Cellulitis
  Zeiss Cyst
  Moll Cyst

Explanation:
A chalazion is a sterile, granulomatous inflammation caused by a blocked meibomian gland. It typically presents as a firm, non-tender, painless nodule within the eyelid, making it the most likely diagnosis for a painless swelling lasting a month. A stye (hordeolum) is an acute, painful infection.

================================================================================
Question 442 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#5. A 70-year-old lady has an ulcerated nodular mass on her right medial lower eyelid for the last 4 years. The central ulceration has raised rolled edges with dilated blood vessels over the lateral margins. Preauricular and submandibular lymph nodes are not palpable. The most likely diagnosis is:

Options:
✓ Basal Cell Carcinoma
  Cicatricial ectropion
  Paralytic ectropion
  Sebaceous Gland Carcinoma
  Squamous Cell Carcinoma

Explanation:
The clinical description of a long-standing, nodular mass with central ulceration, raised or 'rolled' edges, and telangiectasias (dilated blood vessels) is the classic presentation of a nodulo-ulcerative Basal Cell Carcinoma (BCC).

================================================================================
Question 443 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#6. A 25-year-old female had a trauma to the right lower lid. Now she is unable to close the right eye fully, the lower eyelid margin is rotated outward, and there is a scar mark 2mm below the lower eyelid. What's the likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Congenital ectropion
✓ Cicatricial ectropion
  Paralytic ectropion
  Senile ectropion
  Traumatic entropion

Explanation:
Cicatricial ectropion is the outward turning of the eyelid margin due to scarring and contracture of the skin. The history of trauma and the presence of a scar directly link the condition to a cicatricial cause.

================================================================================
Question 444 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#7. A 2-year-old female child is brought to you with a complaint of a white pupil and decrease of vision. You examine her and diagnose her as a case of congenital cataract. Which one is the most common presentation/type?

Options:
  Blue Dot
✓ Lamellar
  Nuclear
  Polar
  Sutural

Explanation:
While many morphological types exist, the lamellar (or zonular) cataract is the most common type of clinically significant congenital cataract, often affecting vision and requiring intervention.

================================================================================
Question 445 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#8. A 60-year-old man presented with a right eye cataract. You performed his biometry and calculated his IOL power. Which is the most important factor in IOL calculation?

Options:
  A Constant
✓ Axial Length
  B Scan
  Keratometry
  Tonometry

Explanation:
While both axial length and keratometry are critical for IOL power calculation, small errors in axial length measurement have a much greater impact on the final refractive outcome than errors in keratometry. Therefore, axial length is considered the most important factor.

================================================================================
Question 446 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#9. A patient presented with recurrent pterygium. Which of the following is the best management plan?

Options:
  Bare Sclera technique
  Excision with Conjunctival autograft
✓ Excision with MMC

Explanation:
For recurrent pterygium, simple excision has a high recurrence rate. Excision combined with a conjunctival autograft is the gold standard, as it provides the lowest recurrence rates by creating a barrier to fibrovascular regrowth. Mitomycin C (MMC) is also used but has a higher risk of complications like scleral melt.

================================================================================
Question 447 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#10. A patient presented with photophobia and decreased vision in the left eye. On examination, there is conjunctival circumferential congestion and a constricted pupil. What is the likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Acute congestive glaucoma
  Anterior uveitis
✓ Conjunctivitis

Explanation:
The triad of photophobia, ciliary (circumferential) congestion, and a constricted (miotic) pupil are classic signs of acute anterior uveitis (iritis). Acute glaucoma typically presents with a mid-dilated pupil, and conjunctivitis usually has diffuse congestion without significant pain or vision loss.

================================================================================
Question 448 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#11. All can be possible complications of CRVO except:

Options:
  Glaucoma
  Rubeosis iridis
  Macular edema
  Intraocular hemorrhage

Explanation:
Intraocular hemorrhage (specifically retinal hemorrhages) is a primary sign of Central Retinal Vein Occlusion (CRVO), not a complication that develops later. Macular edema, rubeosis iridis, and subsequent neovascular glaucoma are the major complications.

================================================================================
Question 449 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#12. All are risk factors for CRVO except:

Options:
  Oral Contraceptives
✓ Myopia
  Macroglobulinemia
  Diabetes mellitus

Explanation:
Systemic vascular diseases like diabetes, hypertension, and hyperviscosity states (e.g., macroglobulinemia) are major risk factors for CRVO. Myopia is a significant risk factor for retinal detachment and myopic macular degeneration, but not typically for CRVO.

================================================================================
Question 450 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#13. All can be the causes of an exudative retinal detachment except:

Options:
  Malignant melanoma
✓ High Myopia
  Diabetic retinopathy
  Posterior uveitis

Explanation:
Exudative retinal detachment is caused by fluid accumulating under the retina due to inflammation, tumors (melanoma), or vascular leakage (diabetic retinopathy). High myopia is a primary cause of rhegmatogenous retinal detachment, which is caused by a retinal break or tear allowing vitreous fluid to seep underneath.

================================================================================
Question 451 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#14. All can be the signs of a rhegmatogenous retinal detachment except:

Options:
✓ Raised IOP
  Mobile retina
  Retinal break

Explanation:
Rhegmatogenous retinal detachment is typically associated with low intraocular pressure (hypotony) due to ciliary body detachment or increased uveoscleral outflow. A raised IOP is not a characteristic sign. A retinal break and a mobile, undulating retina are hallmark features.

================================================================================
Question 452 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#15. All can be the causes of macular edema except:

Options:
  Cataract surgery
✓ High Myopia
  Diabetes
  Trauma

Explanation:
Macular edema can be caused by diabetes, post-cataract surgery (Irvine-Gass syndrome), and trauma. High myopia is associated with conditions like choroidal neovascularization or macular schisis, but it is not a direct cause of macular edema in the same way the other conditions are.

================================================================================
Question 453 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#16. All may be considered as treatment options for retinoblastoma except:

Options:
✓ Enucleation
  Laser Photocoagulation
  Brachytherapy

Explanation:
This question is ambiguous as enucleation, laser photocoagulation, and brachytherapy are all established treatment options for retinoblastoma, depending on the tumor's size, location, and stage. There is no clear 'exception' among the given choices. A definitive answer cannot be determined from the options provided.

================================================================================
Question 454 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#17. All are the tests for pre-perimetric glaucoma except:

Options:
  Visual Fields and Optic disc
  OCT optic disc and HRT
  Visual Fields and HRT
✓ OCT and Gonio

Explanation:
'Pre-perimetric' glaucoma means structural damage (like nerve fiber layer thinning) has occurred without detectable functional loss on a standard visual field test. OCT and HRT are key tests to detect this structural damage. Gonioscopy (Gonio) assesses the drainage angle to classify glaucoma type but doesn't detect pre-perimetric damage itself. Thus, the combination of OCT and Gonio is the exception.

================================================================================
Question 455 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#21. A 6-year-old male child is brought to the outdoor clinic with complaints of decrease of vision while looking at the whiteboard in school. Parents also noticed that the child stands very close to the television. You perform retinoscopy and there is a streak moving in the opposite direction. What is the diagnosis?

Options:
  Astigmatism
  Hyperopia
  Emmetropia
✓ Myopia
  Strabismus

Explanation:
The clinical history of difficulty with distance vision (whiteboard, television) is classic for myopia (nearsightedness). On retinoscopy, a streak of light moving 'against' or in the opposite direction of the retinoscope's movement confirms the diagnosis of myopia.

================================================================================
Question 456 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#22. You perform a retinoscopy of a patient complaining of decrease of vision. On retinoscopy the reflex moves with the movement. Which of the following is most likely false:

Options:
  Astigmatism
  Hyperopia
  Emmetropia
✓ Myopia greater than working distance

Explanation:
A 'with' movement in retinoscopy indicates hyperopia, emmetropia, or myopia that is less than the dioptric power of the working distance. A myopic eye with a refractive error greater than the working distance would produce an 'against' movement. Therefore, 'Myopia greater than working distance' is false.

================================================================================
Question 457 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#23. A 4-year-old child is brought to you with the complaints of deviation of eyes. On the extraocular movements, the eyes are moving fine. What is the next most important step in the management?

Options:
✓ Cycloplegic refraction
  Fundus examination
  Autorefraction
  Pupil reaction

Explanation:
In a child with strabismus (deviation of eyes), the most important next step is to perform a cycloplegic refraction. This paralyzes accommodation, allowing for an accurate measurement of the child's true refractive error. Uncorrected refractive errors, particularly hyperopia, are a common cause of accommodative esotropia.

================================================================================
Question 458 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#24. A patient presents with right ptosis and the condition is painful. He is recently diagnosed. There is right exotropia in primary position, the pupil is dilated and not responding to light. VA 6/6 BE and fundi are grossly normal. There is no past history of trauma. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Aneurysm of posterior cerebral artery
  Aneurysm of posterior communicating artery

Explanation:
This patient presents with a painful, pupil-involving third nerve palsy (ptosis, 'down and out' eye position/exotropia, dilated pupil). The parasympathetic fibers that constrict the pupil travel on the outside of the third nerve, making them susceptible to compression. A posterior communicating artery (PCOM) aneurysm is a classic cause of compressive, painful, pupil-involving third nerve palsy.

================================================================================
Question 459 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#25. All are the signs of acute iridocyclitis except:

Options:
  Aqueous flare
  Small pupil
  Limbal congestion
✓ Patch of iris atrophy

Explanation:
Aqueous flare (due to protein leakage), a small/miotic pupil (due to sphincter muscle spasm), and limbal/ciliary congestion are all classic signs of acute iridocyclitis (anterior uveitis). A patch of iris atrophy is typically a sign of previous inflammation or herpetic uveitis, not an acute sign.

================================================================================
Question 460 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#26. Anterior chamber is shallow in:

Options:
  Open angle glaucoma
  Aphakia
  Myopia
✓ Angle closure glaucoma

Explanation:
A shallow anterior chamber is the key anatomical predisposition for angle-closure glaucoma. In contrast, myopia and aphakia are associated with a deep anterior chamber, and open-angle glaucoma is not defined by anterior chamber depth.

================================================================================
Question 461 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#27. A 55-year-old hypertensive lady develops sudden loss of vision in her left eye. She has widespread flame-shaped hemorrhages in the retina. The most likely diagnosis is:

Options:
  Central retinal artery occlusion
  Diabetic retinopathy
✓ Central retinal vein occlusion

Explanation:
The presentation of sudden, painless vision loss with widespread flame-shaped hemorrhages throughout all four quadrants of the retina is the classic fundoscopic appearance of a Central Retinal Vein Occlusion (CRVO). Hypertension is a major risk factor.

================================================================================
Question 462 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#28. Unequal size of two pupils is called as:

Options:
✓ Anisocoria
  Anisometropia
  Aniridia
  Heterochromia

Explanation:
Anisocoria is the medical term for unequal pupil sizes. Anisometropia is unequal refractive error, aniridia is absence of the iris, and heterochromia is a difference in iris color.

================================================================================
Question 463 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#29. Yellowish color of the macula is because of the presence of:

Options:
  Hemoglobin
  Fluorescein
  Melanin
✓ Xanthophyll pigments (Lutein and Zeaxanthin)

Explanation:
The macula lutea ('yellow spot') gets its characteristic yellowish color from high concentrations of xanthophyll carotenoid pigments, specifically lutein and zeaxanthin, which help filter blue light. As 'None of the above' is an option and the specific pigments are not listed, this is the most accurate answer. Assuming 'd' was intended to be the correct choice.

================================================================================
Question 464 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#31. A 70-year-old lady has a shiny nodular mass on her right lower eyelid for the last 4 months. The lesion is growing rapidly. Preauricular and submandibular lymph nodes are palpable. Histopathology shows keratin pearls. The most likely diagnosis is:

Options:
  Basal cell carcinoma
  Sebaceous gland carcinoma
  Kaposi Sarcoma
✓ Squamous cell carcinoma

Explanation:
While BCC is more common, the rapid growth, palpable lymph nodes (indicating metastasis), and especially the histopathological finding of 'keratin pearls' are characteristic features of Squamous Cell Carcinoma (SCC).

================================================================================
Question 465 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#32. A 25-year-old male comes to the clinic with a 3-day history of painful swelling on his right upper lid margin. On examination, there is blepharitis, a red tender upper eyelid with an infected eyelash. What is your most probable diagnosis?

Options:
  Chalazion
  Haemangioma
  Dermoid Cyst
  Papilloma
✓ Stye

Explanation:
A stye, or external hordeolum, is an acute, painful staphylococcal infection of an eyelash follicle or its associated glands (Zeis or Moll). The description of a painful, red, tender swelling at the lid margin involving an eyelash is classic for a stye.

================================================================================
Question 466 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#33. A three-year-old child is presented with bilateral subconjunctival hemorrhages. He has been suffering from fever, severe cough, and chest infection. The commonest cause of subconjunctival hemorrhages in this case would be:

Options:
  Fever
✓ Severe Cough

Explanation:
Severe coughing fits cause a sudden increase in intrathoracic and venous pressure (a Valsalva-like maneuver), which can rupture the small, fragile conjunctival blood vessels, leading to a subconjunctival hemorrhage. The fever is part of the illness but the cough is the direct mechanical cause.

================================================================================
Question 467 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#34. An 8-year-old child is brought by his parents with chief complaints of mucopurulent discharge, redness, and swelling of both eyes for the last two months. On examination, there are follicles on the upper tarsal conjunctiva and a superior corneal pannus. What is the most probable diagnosis?

Options:
  Allergic conjunctivitis
✓ Trachoma

Explanation:
The presence of follicles on the upper tarsal conjunctiva combined with the development of a superior corneal pannus (vascularization and scarring of the upper cornea) are pathognomonic signs of trachoma, a chronic conjunctivitis caused by Chlamydia trachomatis.

================================================================================
Question 468 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#35. A house officer was asked about the causes of a dilated pupil. What is the most common cause of a dilated pupil seen in a clinical setting?

Options:
  Trauma
  Third cranial nerve palsy
✓ Pharmacological dilatation
  Acute angle closure glaucoma

Explanation:
In a general clinical or emergency setting, the most common reason for a patient to present with a fixed, dilated pupil is iatrogenic—the intentional use of mydriatic (dilating) eye drops like tropicamide or cyclopentolate for a fundus examination.

================================================================================
Question 469 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#36. A 7-year-old child presents every spring season for the last 4 years with itching, redness, and watering. There are no follicles but papillae on both upper tarsal conjunctivae. There are no preauricular lymph nodes. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Adenoviral conjunctivitis
  Bacterial conjunctivitis
✓ Vernal keratoconjunctivitis

Explanation:
The combination of severe itching, a seasonal (spring) recurrence, and the presence of giant papillae ('cobblestone' appearance) on the upper tarsal conjunctiva are the hallmark features of Vernal Keratoconjunctivitis (VKC), a severe form of allergic conjunctivitis.

================================================================================
Question 470 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#37. A patient presented with photophobia and watering in the left eye for the last week. On examination with fluorescein, a dendritic ulcer was revealed. This is caused by:

Options:
  Fungus
  Herpes zoster virus
✓ Herpes simplex virus

Explanation:
A dendritic ulcer, which has a characteristic branching, tree-like pattern when stained with fluorescein, is the pathognomonic sign of active epithelial keratitis caused by the Herpes Simplex Virus (HSV).

================================================================================
Question 471 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#38. A young boy presented in the emergency department with watering and photophobia in his right eye after a trauma. Which test is most appropriate?

Options:
  Applanation tonometry
✓ Fluorescein staining
  Rose Bengal staining
  Schirmer's test

Explanation:
After an eye trauma, symptoms of watering and photophobia are highly suggestive of a corneal abrasion. Fluorescein staining is the essential diagnostic test to visualize any epithelial defects on the cornea, which will stain bright green under a cobalt blue light.

================================================================================
Question 472 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#40. A 15-year-old boy presents with progressive decrease in vision. He suffers from vernal keratoconjunctivitis. Which medication is likely responsible for his vision loss?

Options:
  Dexamethasone

Explanation:
Vernal keratoconjunctivitis is often treated with topical steroids like Dexamethasone. Long-term steroid use is a well-known cause of posterior subcapsular cataracts and elevated intraocular pressure (steroid-induced glaucoma), both of which can lead to a progressive decrease in vision.

================================================================================
Question 473 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#41. A 30-year-old man, while working in a factory, suffered an electric shock but survived. What could be the most possible effect on his eye?

Options:
✓ Bilateral cataracts

Explanation:
A high-voltage electric shock can cause a specific type of cataract, often developing months to years after the injury. The cataracts are typically bilateral and may have a characteristic snowflake or diffuse milky appearance.

================================================================================
Question 474 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#42. The test used to calculate the power of the intraocular lens prior to cataract surgery is:

Options:
  B-Scan
✓ Biometry

Explanation:
Ocular biometry is the process of measuring the eye's characteristics—specifically axial length and corneal curvature (keratometry)—to calculate the appropriate power for the intraocular lens (IOL) implant.

================================================================================
Question 475 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#43. A 55-year-old lady suffers from rheumatoid arthritis. She has developed intensely painful red eyes. Visual acuity is 6/12 with pinhole. In her left eye, she has a red nodular swelling approximately 2mm from the limbus. The swelling is tender and fixed and does not blanch with the use of phenylephrine. What is the likely diagnosis?

Options:
✓ Scleritis
  Episcleritis

Explanation:
The key features are severe, deep pain, a nodular swelling of the deep vessels that does not blanch with topical phenylephrine, and an association with a systemic autoimmune disease (rheumatoid arthritis). These are classic signs of nodular scleritis. Episcleritis is less painful, involves superficial vessels that do blanch, and is often idiopathic.

================================================================================
Question 476 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#44. In a non-healing, sloughing corneal ulcer where perforation is expected, what is the best management?

Options:
  Scrapping of the cornea
✓ Conjunctival flap

Explanation:
When a corneal ulcer is non-healing and perforation is imminent, a conjunctival flap (Gundersen flap) can be used as a tectonic support. It brings a vascular supply to the area, promotes healing, and provides a physical barrier to cover the thinned cornea or seal a small perforation.

================================================================================
Question 477 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#45. A female of 40 years presents with painless decrease of vision for four years. The patient has myopia with irregular astigmatism. There is thinning of the central cornea. The most sensitive investigation for this patient is:

Options:
  Refraction
  Ocular biometry
✓ Corneal Topography/Tomography

Explanation:
The combination of progressive myopia, irregular astigmatism, and central corneal thinning is highly suggestive of keratoconus. Corneal topography or tomography is the most sensitive investigation to diagnose early or subclinical keratoconus by mapping the corneal curvature and thickness.

================================================================================
Question 478 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#46. A 4-year-old child is brought by her mother with bilateral white pupils. Anterior segment and pupillary reactions are normal. Ultrasound B-scan was performed and was found to be normal. What is the most probable diagnosis?

Options:
✓ Bilateral congenital cataracts
  Bilateral retinoblastoma

Explanation:
Bilateral leukocoria (white pupils) in a child can be caused by cataracts or retinoblastoma. Since the B-scan ultrasound, which is used to detect intraocular masses like retinoblastoma, was normal, the most probable diagnosis is bilateral congenital cataracts.

================================================================================
Question 479 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#47. A patient who was operated for a cataract four months back comes to you with foggy vision. On examination, there was posterior capsular thickening. Which mode of treatment is most useful?

Options:
  Argon laser
  YAG-laser

Explanation:
Posterior capsular opacification (PCO) is a common complication after cataract surgery, causing foggy vision. The standard treatment is a Nd:YAG laser capsulotomy, where the YAG laser is used to create a clear opening in the center of the opacified posterior capsule.

================================================================================
Question 480 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#48. What is the best option for treating a unilateral cataract in a young adult with unaided visual acuity of 6/6 in the other eye?

Options:
  Phacoemulsification and IOL implantation

Explanation:
In a young adult, correcting a unilateral cataract without an intraocular lens (IOL) would result in aphakia and significant anisometropia, which is intolerable. The standard of care and best option is to remove the cataract via phacoemulsification and implant an IOL to restore clear, focused vision.

================================================================================
Question 481 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#49. A child is hit in the eye by a used disposable syringe by his younger brother 2 days back. He is brought by his parents to the OPD with a painful red eye. His eyelids are swollen, there is ciliary congestion, a hazy cornea, and hypopyon. The pupil is yellowish-white and there is no fundus reflex. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
  Endophthalmitis

Explanation:
The history of a penetrating injury (syringe) followed by severe pain, vision loss, eyelid swelling, hazy cornea, and hypopyon (pus in the anterior chamber) are classic signs of post-traumatic endophthalmitis, a severe intraocular infection. This is an ophthalmologic emergency.

================================================================================
Question 482 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#50. A 35-year-old female presents to the OPD with dimness of vision in the right eye for one year. She gives a history of right cataract extraction but the record is not available. Clinically, visual acuity in the right eye is CF 2m & 6/6 in the left eye. There is now right aphakia with an intact posterior capsule. What is the most appropriate method for restoration of vision in the right eye?

Options:
  Right secondary posterior chamber IOL implantation

Explanation:
The patient is aphakic (missing the lens) but has an intact posterior capsule, which provides crucial support. The best way to restore vision is by implanting a secondary intraocular lens (IOL). Placing it in the posterior chamber (ideally in the sulcus) is the most anatomically sound and stable option.

================================================================================
Question 483 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#51. A one-year-old boy is brought to the OPD by his parents. The doctor diagnosed him as having congenital cataracts. What will happen if his cataracts are not removed at the earliest?

Options:
✓ He will develop stimulus deprivation amblyopia
  He will develop phthisis bulbi

Explanation:
During the critical period of visual development in infancy, a clear retinal image is necessary for the visual pathways to mature. A dense congenital cataract blocks this image, leading to severe and often irreversible stimulus deprivation amblyopia ('lazy eye') if not removed promptly.

================================================================================
Question 484 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#52. The best emergent treatment of an alkali burn at the site of injury is:

Options:
  Wash the eye with vinegar to neutralize alkali
  Immediately irrigate with copious amounts of water or saline

Explanation:
The immediate, critical first step for any chemical burn to the eye, especially alkali burns, is prolonged and copious irrigation with sterile saline or clean water for at least 15-30 minutes. This dilutes and washes away the chemical to minimize damage. Attempting neutralization is dangerous and can cause further injury.

================================================================================
Question 485 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#53. A patient has blunt trauma with a tennis ball and is found to have a hyphema. What is a hyphema?

Options:
✓ Blood in the anterior chamber
  Protein in the anterior chamber

Explanation:
A hyphema is the medical term for blood collecting in the anterior chamber of the eye, the space between the cornea and the iris. It is a common result of blunt-force trauma.

================================================================================
Question 486 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#54. A 13-year-old boy is brought to an ophthalmologist with pain and diffuse swelling of the right upper lid. The boy is febrile and the upper lid area is red, swollen, and tender. The eye itself is white. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Amyloidosis of the lids
  Preseptal cellulitis

Explanation:
Preseptal cellulitis is an infection of the eyelid and soft tissues anterior to the orbital septum. It is characterized by lid edema, erythema, and tenderness. Crucially, the globe itself is not involved, so vision, pupillary reactions, and eye movements are normal, and the eye is 'white' (not congested).

================================================================================
Question 487 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#55. A 32-year-old male presents with a red eye. He was referred to the ophthalmologist with a suspicion of uveitis. The most reliable sign for diagnosing acute anterior uveitis is:

Options:
  Keratic precipitates (KPs) on the Endothelium

Explanation:
While many signs are present in anterior uveitis, the presence of inflammatory cells in the anterior chamber is definitive. These cells can be seen as keratic precipitates (KPs), which are clumps of inflammatory cells stuck to the inner surface of the cornea (the endothelium), and are a hallmark sign of uveitis.

================================================================================
Question 488 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#56. The treatment of intraocular malignant melanoma of the uvea is:

Options:
✓ Enucleation
  Chemotherapy
  Evisceration
  Radiotherapy
  Exenteration

Explanation:
For large uveal melanomas where vision cannot be salvaged, or for tumors that do not respond to other treatments, enucleation (complete removal of the eyeball) has historically been the standard treatment to prevent metastasis. Radiotherapy (e.g., plaque brachytherapy) is now a primary treatment for small to medium-sized tumors to preserve the eye.

================================================================================
Question 489 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#57. The Hirschberg test is used to detect:

Options:
✓ Squint
  Optic atrophy
  Field defect
  Cataract

Explanation:
The Hirschberg test, or corneal light reflex test, is a simple screening tool used to assess ocular alignment and detect the presence of strabismus (squint). It involves observing the position of the light reflex from a penlight on the corneas of both eyes.

================================================================================
Question 490 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#58. Trichiasis is:

Options:
✓ Misdirection of eyelashes toward the globe

Explanation:
Trichiasis is the misdirection of eyelashes, causing them to grow inwards and rub against the cornea and conjunctiva, leading to irritation, pain, and potential corneal damage.

================================================================================
Question 491 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#59. The commonest feature of open-angle glaucoma is:

Options:
  Asymptomatic

Explanation:
Primary open-angle glaucoma is often called the 'silent thief of sight' because it is typically asymptomatic in its early to moderate stages. Patients do not experience pain or noticeable vision loss until significant, irreversible peripheral visual field damage has occurred.

================================================================================
Question 492 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#60. The treatment of choice in recently diagnosed early open-angle glaucoma is:

Options:
  Prostaglandin analogs

Explanation:
Prostaglandin analogs (e.g., latanoprost) are generally considered the first-line medical treatment for open-angle glaucoma. They are highly effective at lowering IOP, have a convenient once-daily dosing schedule, and have a favorable side-effect profile compared to older medications like beta-blockers.

================================================================================
Question 493 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#61. The most common indication for cataract surgery is:

Options:
  Patient unable to perform daily activities

Explanation:
The primary indication for cataract surgery is when the patient's visual impairment from the cataract begins to interfere with their quality of life and ability to perform daily activities such as reading, driving, or recognizing faces. It is a functional, rather than numerical, indication.

================================================================================
Question 494 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#62. Which of the following is the best management of congenital glaucoma:

Options:
  Surgery

Explanation:
Congenital glaucoma is a surgical disease. Medical therapy is only a temporary measure to control intraocular pressure before surgery. The definitive treatment involves angle surgery, such as goniotomy or trabeculotomy, to improve the aqueous outflow.

================================================================================
Question 495 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#63. A patient undergoes a combined cataract and filtering procedure. On the first postoperative day, the patient has an intraocular pressure (IOP) of 43 with a chamber that is flat peripherally and very shallow centrally with no lens-corneal touch. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
✓ Malignant glaucoma (Aqueous misdirection)

Explanation:
The combination of a very high IOP with a uniformly shallow or flat anterior chamber after intraocular surgery is the classic presentation of malignant glaucoma, also known as aqueous misdirection syndrome. This occurs when aqueous humor is misdirected posteriorly into the vitreous cavity, pushing the lens-iris diaphragm forward.

================================================================================
Question 496 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#64. What is one of the factors that minimize surgically induced astigmatism when performing surgery?

Options:
✓ Size and location of the wound

Explanation:
Surgically induced astigmatism is directly related to the corneal incision. Smaller incisions (as in modern phacoemulsification) and incisions placed more peripherally or on the sclera induce less astigmatism. The size and location of the wound are critical factors.

================================================================================
Question 497 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#65. A patient with pseudoexfoliation syndrome is at increased risk for which of the following complications?

Options:
✓ Zonular damage during cataract surgery

Explanation:
Pseudoexfoliation syndrome is characterized by the deposition of fibrillar material throughout the eye, including on the lens zonules. This causes the zonules to become weak and brittle, significantly increasing the risk of zonular dehiscence, capsular bag instability, and vitreous loss during cataract surgery.

================================================================================
Question 498 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#66. A patient with pseudoexfoliation and a very large, hard, brunescent nuclear sclerotic cataract has marked pseudodonesis and more than 180 degrees of zonular dehiscence. What is the best surgical approach?

Options:
  Phacoemulsification employing a capsular tension ring

Explanation:
In cases with significant zonular weakness or dehiscence (as indicated by pseudodonesis), a capsular tension ring (CTR) is essential. The CTR is inserted into the capsular bag to provide circumferential support, stabilize the bag, and allow for safer phacoemulsification and secure in-the-bag IOL placement.

================================================================================
Question 499 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#67. What is the best management for phacolytic glaucoma?

Options:
  Cataract extraction

Explanation:
Phacolytic glaucoma is a secondary open-angle glaucoma caused by high-molecular-weight lens proteins leaking from a hypermature cataract, which then clog the trabecular meshwork. The definitive treatment is to remove the source of the proteins by performing cataract extraction. Medical therapy is used pre-operatively to control IOP.

================================================================================
Question 500 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#68. For any given diopter power, the higher the refractive index, the thinner the intraocular lens optic. Which of the following currently available intraocular lens optic materials possesses the highest refractive index?

Options:
✓ Hydrophobic acrylic

Explanation:
Different IOL materials have different refractive indices. Of the commonly used materials, hydrophobic acrylic generally has the highest refractive index (around 1.55), which allows for a thinner lens profile compared to materials like PMMA or silicone with lower indices.

================================================================================
Question 501 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#69. Which of the following sequelae of a carotid cavernous sinus fistula is the most common cause of visual disability?

Options:
  Glaucoma

Explanation:
A carotid-cavernous fistula causes high pressure in the cavernous sinus, which leads to elevated episcleral venous pressure. This, in turn, severely impairs aqueous outflow and causes a significant, often difficult-to-treat, secondary glaucoma, which is a major cause of vision loss.

================================================================================
Question 502 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#70. During a consultation in the emergency room, you are asked to see a patient with non-infected 2nd-degree burns to the eyelids. The emergency room physicians ask what topical medicine should be placed on the burns to prevent scarring. What would be the most appropriate response?

Options:
  Lubricating ointment

Explanation:
For eyelid burns, the primary goal is to keep the tissue moist to promote healing and prevent contracture and scarring. A bland lubricating ointment (like an antibiotic ointment for prophylaxis) serves this purpose well. There are no specific medications that can 'prevent' scarring in this context.

================================================================================
Question 503 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#71. A 56-year-old man complains of pain around his left eye that has lasted for 2 weeks. The pain increases on extraocular movement. One week ago, he noted blurred vision in the left eye and a slight fever. His visual acuity is 20/40 OD and 20/40 OS. The patient has 5 mm of proptosis in the left eye with erythema and tenderness around the left eyelid. Results of biomicroscopy and dilated fundus examination are normal. What is the most helpful diagnostic test for this patient?

Options:
✓ CT scan of the orbits

Explanation:
The patient's symptoms—proptosis, pain on eye movement, and eyelid erythema—are classic signs of an orbital inflammatory process or mass. A CT scan of the orbits is the most helpful initial diagnostic test to visualize the orbital soft tissues, extraocular muscles, and optic nerve to identify the cause, such as orbital cellulitis, abscess, or orbital pseudotumor.

================================================================================
Question 504 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#72. What is the best way to distinguish a restrictive motility disorder from a paretic disorder after an orbital floor fracture?

Options:
  Forced-duction testing

Explanation:
Forced-duction testing is a clinical maneuver where the anesthetized eye is physically moved with forceps. If movement is limited (a positive test), it indicates a restrictive problem, such as muscle entrapment in a fracture. If the eye moves freely, the limitation is due to a paretic (nerve palsy) cause.

================================================================================
Question 505 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#73. What are the 2 most commonly affected rectus muscles in thyroid eye disease?

Options:
  Inferior and medial

Explanation:
In thyroid eye disease (Graves' orbitopathy), the extraocular muscles become inflamed and enlarged. The involvement follows a typical pattern, remembered by the mnemonic 'I'M SLOW': Inferior rectus, Medial rectus, Superior rectus, Lateral rectus, Obliques. Thus, the inferior and medial recti are the most commonly affected.

================================================================================
Question 506 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#74. What is the most common organism implicated in dacryocystitis?

Options:
  Staphylococcus aureus

Explanation:
Acute dacryocystitis (infection of the lacrimal sac) is most commonly caused by gram-positive bacteria, with Staphylococcus aureus and Streptococcus pneumoniae being the most frequent isolates.

================================================================================
Question 507 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#75. In diabetic retinopathy, the most important feature of the pre-proliferative stage includes:

Options:
  Cotton wool spots

Explanation:
Pre-proliferative diabetic retinopathy (PPDR) is characterized by signs of increasing retinal ischemia. Cotton wool spots (nerve fiber layer infarcts), venous beading, and intraretinal microvascular abnormalities (IRMA) are key features that signify progression from non-proliferative to this more advanced stage.

================================================================================
Question 508 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#76. Regarding hypertensive retinopathy, which of the following is least likely a sign of severe or malignant stage changes:

Options:
  Arteriolar narrowing

Explanation:
Generalized or focal arteriolar narrowing is one of the earliest signs of hypertensive retinopathy (Grade 1-2). Severe or malignant stage changes (Grade 4) are characterized by more advanced signs like flame-shaped hemorrhages, cotton-wool spots, and optic disc swelling (papilledema).

================================================================================
Question 509 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#77. The earliest symptom of nuclear sclerosis after middle age may be:

Options:
  Second sight (myopic shift)

Explanation:
Nuclear sclerosis is a type of cataract where the central part of the lens hardens and increases in refractive index. This causes a myopic shift, where a previously presbyopic or hyperopic patient may find they can read again without glasses. This phenomenon is known as 'second sight'.

================================================================================
Question 510 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#78. Being able to see only Schwalbe's line on gonioscopy means that the angle is:

Options:
✓ Closed

Explanation:
In gonioscopy, if only Schwalbe's line (the most anterior structure) is visible, it means all the functional parts of the angle (trabecular meshwork, scleral spur, ciliary body) are obscured by the peripheral iris. This corresponds to a Grade 0 or closed angle.

================================================================================
Question 511 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#79. Which of the following visual field defects is most characteristically seen in optic neuritis?

Options:
  Central scotoma

Explanation:
Optic neuritis typically involves inflammation of the papillomacular bundle of the optic nerve, which carries signals from the macula. This results in a characteristic central or cecocentral scotoma (a blind spot in the center of the visual field).

================================================================================
Question 512 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#80. All of the following are types of amblyopia except:

Options:
  Anisometropic

Explanation:
Strabismic, anisometropic, and stimulus deprivation are the major types of amblyopia. Ammetropic amblyopia, referring to amblyopia from high but equal refractive error in both eyes (e.g., high hyperopia), is also a recognized type. The question likely contains a typo, but based on common classifications, all listed options are forms or causes of amblyopia. However, if 'Ammetropic' and 'Anisometropic' are considered, 'Ammetropic' is a less commonly used term, making it the most likely intended 'except' option.

================================================================================
Question 513 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#81. With direct ophthalmoscopy, which of the following structures are seen?

Options:
  Retina

Explanation:
Direct ophthalmoscopy provides a highly magnified, upright, monocular view of the fundus. This allows for detailed examination of the posterior pole structures, including the retina, optic disc, macula, and retinal blood vessels.

================================================================================
Question 514 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#82. A neurogenic cause of ptosis is:

Options:
  Third nerve palsy

Explanation:
Neurogenic ptosis is caused by a problem with the nerve supply to the eyelid muscles. The oculomotor nerve (third cranial nerve) innervates the levator palpebrae superioris, the main muscle for eyelid elevation. A palsy of this nerve is a classic neurogenic cause of ptosis.

================================================================================
Question 515 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#83. Which of the following surgical instruments is used in Chalazion surgery?

Options:
  Chalazion clamp and curette

Explanation:
Incision and curettage of a chalazion requires specific instruments. A chalazion clamp (e.g., Desmarres or Lambert) is used to evert the eyelid and provide hemostasis, while a chalazion curette is a small scoop used to remove the granulomatous contents of the lesion. A 'wire vectus' is used in cataract surgery.

================================================================================
Question 516 (KMC - 2024)
