================================================================================

Question:
Q#84. Which of the following is not routinely used in ophthalmology:

Options:
  Punch

Explanation:
While various needles, sutures (like Nylon and Vicryl), and forceps are fundamental to ophthalmic surgery, a 'punch' is not a standard or routinely used instrument in most common procedures. Punches are more associated with procedures like dermal biopsies or sclerostomy in older glaucoma surgeries.

================================================================================
Question 517 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#85. As a fourth-year student, ophthalmology and ophthalmologists inspired you a lot due to elective. They have a clean bloodless surgical field, good income, and a high number of patients. Still, you opted for a general surgery profession. What is a common perceived downside of ophthalmology?

Options:
  Too much saturation

Explanation:
This is a subjective question about career choice. While ophthalmology has many benefits, a common perception among medical students is that it is a highly competitive specialty with a limited number of training spots and that the field may be 'saturated' in desirable practice locations, making it difficult to establish a new practice.

================================================================================
Question 518 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#86. The most common cause of gradual, painless decrease of vision in old age is:

Options:
  Cataract

Explanation:
Age-related cataract is the leading cause of reversible blindness worldwide. It causes a progressive, painless clouding of the natural lens, leading to a gradual decline in vision, glare, and changes in color perception in the elderly.

================================================================================
Question 519 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#87. The insufficiency of accommodation that leads to a decrease in near vision after 40 years of age is called:

Options:
  Presbyopia

Explanation:
Presbyopia is the age-related physiological process where the crystalline lens loses its flexibility, diminishing the eye's ability to accommodate or focus on near objects. This typically becomes noticeable around the age of 40.

================================================================================
Question 520 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#88. A lesion in the optic tract results in:

Options:
✓ Homonymous hemianopia

Explanation:
The optic tract contains nerve fibers from the temporal half of the ipsilateral retina and the nasal half of the contralateral retina. A lesion in the optic tract (posterior to the chiasm) will therefore cause a visual field defect affecting the same half (e.g., the right half) of the visual field in both eyes, which is called a contralateral homonymous hemianopia.

================================================================================
Question 521 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#89. A current epidemic in which eyes are swollen, red, congested, and watery, affecting all family members, is likely due to:

Options:
  Adenovirus

Explanation:
Adenovirus is the most common cause of viral conjunctivitis and is responsible for epidemics of 'pink eye'. It is highly contagious, leading to rapid spread among close contacts like family members, and presents with redness, watering, and sometimes swelling.

================================================================================
Question 522 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#90. All of the following are complications of vernal keratoconjunctivitis except:

Options:
✓ Cataract

Explanation:
Complications of vernal keratoconjunctivitis (VKC) itself include shield ulcers, corneal scarring, and keratoconus. However, cataracts are not a direct complication of the disease process. They are a well-known complication of the long-term topical steroid treatment often required to manage severe VKC.

================================================================================
Question 523 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#91. A 56-year-old diabetic patient presented with decreased vision in both eyes for the last one year. On examination, the anterior segment was normal. Fundoscopy showed retinal hemorrhages in all 4 quadrants with macular edema. How will you measure the macular edema in this patient?

Options:
  OCT scan of the macula

Explanation:
Optical Coherence Tomography (OCT) is the gold standard for diagnosing and quantifying macular edema. It provides high-resolution, cross-sectional images of the retina, allowing for precise measurement of retinal thickness and visualization of intraretinal cysts.

================================================================================
Question 524 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#92. A 15-year-old girl presents to the eye clinic with a 2-day history of blurring in the right eye with pain on ocular movements. Examination reveals a visual acuity of 6/36 in the right eye and 6/6 in the left eye. There is a RAPD. Fundus examination shows a right swollen optic disc. The left fundus is normal. Systemic history and examination are not abnormal. What is the most appropriate diagnosis?

Options:
  Optic neuritis

Explanation:
The classic triad of symptoms for optic neuritis is acute unilateral vision loss, pain on eye movement, and impaired color vision. The key signs are a relative afferent pupillary defect (RAPD) and a swollen optic disc (papillitis). This presentation in a young person is highly characteristic of optic neuritis.

================================================================================
Question 525 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#93. A 35-year-old male presented to Eye OPD with sudden loss of vision in his right eye. He has a history of trauma to the right eye with a cricket ball 2 months ago. On examination, his visual acuity is 6/36 in the right eye and 6/6 in the left eye. He is having a RAPD. What is the likely cause of his right eye RAPD?

Options:
✓ Retinal detachment

Explanation:
A Relative Afferent Pupillary Defect (RAPD) indicates significant damage to the optic nerve or a widespread retinal pathology. A large retinal detachment, especially one involving the macula, can cause enough photoreceptor dysfunction to produce a RAPD. Given the history of severe blunt trauma, a traumatic retinal detachment is a very likely cause.

================================================================================
Question 526 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#94. A 68-year-old woman came to you with difficulty in reading. She is non-diabetic and non-hypertensive. On examination, VA in both eyes is 6/6 for distance. Ocular examination is unremarkable. What is the cause of her reduced near vision?

Options:
  Presbyopia

Explanation:
The patient has excellent distance vision but difficulty with near vision (reading), which is the classic symptom of presbyopia. This is the age-related decline in the eye's ability to accommodate, making it difficult to focus on close objects. At age 68, this is an expected physiological change.

================================================================================
Question 527 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#95. A 60-year-old uncontrolled diabetic woman presented with sudden decreased vision in her right eye. On examination, there are bilateral cataracts. There is no RAPD. The fundus view is not clear. What investigation will you advise to assess any associated posterior segment pathology?

Options:
  B-scan ultrasonography

Explanation:
When the view of the posterior segment is obscured by a dense cataract, B-scan ultrasonography is the investigation of choice. It uses sound waves to create a two-dimensional image of the structures inside the eye, allowing for the detection of pathologies like retinal detachment or vitreous hemorrhage.

================================================================================
Question 528 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#96. A 60-year-old man presented to you with watering and redness in both eyes for the last year. He had a previous history of bacterial conjunctivitis. On examination, you found inward rotation of the lower eyelids. What is your Diagnosis?

Options:
  Lower lid entropion

Explanation:
Entropion is the inward turning of the eyelid margin. This causes the eyelashes to rub against the cornea (trichiasis), leading to chronic irritation, redness, and watering (epiphora).

================================================================================
Question 529 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#97. A 58-year-old man underwent uneventful phacoemulsification surgery for a cataract in his right eye. After 3 days, he presented with severe pain and loss of vision in the right eye. On examination, he was having VA of perception of light in the right eye and 6/6 in the left eye. He has a congested conjunctiva, hazy cornea, and hypopyon with no view of the posterior segment of the eye. Which is the most probable diagnosis?

Options:
  Endophthalmitis

Explanation:
The acute onset of severe pain, profound vision loss, and signs of intense intraocular inflammation (hazy cornea, hypopyon) within days of cataract surgery is the classic presentation of acute postoperative endophthalmitis. This is a devastating intraocular infection and an ophthalmologic emergency.

================================================================================
Question 530 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#98. A 30-year-old man presents to Eye OPD with epiphora and bilateral drowsy eyelids which worsen in the evenings. There is also a history of fatigability. On examination, his visual acuity is 6/6 in both eyes. Anterior and posterior segment examination is unremarkable. There is bilateral ptosis partially obstructing the visual axis and 20 prism diopter esotropia. Which of the following tests would you perform next?

Options:
  Ice pack test

Explanation:
The symptoms of ptosis and diplopia that worsen with fatigue (diurnal variation) are highly suggestive of Myasthenia Gravis. The ice pack test is a simple, quick clinical test for myasthenia. Cooling the eyelid improves neuromuscular transmission, and a positive test shows a temporary improvement in the ptosis.

================================================================================
Question 531 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#99. A 40-year-old male presented to OPD with sudden blurring of vision in his right eye. VA right eye 6/12 and VA left eye 6/6. On fundus examination, there is superior temporal venous engorgement, flame-shaped hemorrhages, and cotton wool spots. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
  Branch retinal vein occlusion

Explanation:
The fundoscopic findings of hemorrhages, venous engorgement, and cotton wool spots are confined to a specific quadrant (superior temporal). This sectoral pattern is characteristic of a Branch Retinal Vein Occlusion (BRVO), where a branch of the central retinal vein is occluded, typically at an arteriovenous crossing.

================================================================================
Question 532 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#100. A 65-year-old patient presented with vertical diplopia for the last 5 days. He has a history of a road traffic accident 1 week ago. On examination, vision in both eyes is 6/6. There is hypertropia of the right eye, and the patient cannot depress the eye in adduction. Which nerve is most probably involved?

Options:
  Trochlear nerve

Explanation:
The trochlear nerve (cranial nerve IV) innervates the superior oblique muscle, which is responsible for intorsion and depression of the eye, especially when the eye is adducted (moved inward). A palsy of this nerve leads to vertical diplopia and hypertropia, with a characteristic inability to depress the adducted eye. It is very vulnerable to traumatic injury.

================================================================================
Question 533 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#101. A 6-month-old infant is brought by parents with complaints of watering, redness, photophobia, and not fixing and following light with the right eye. On examination, he has nystagmus with an enlarged, hazy cornea. IOP is 30 in the right eye and 8 in the left eye. Left eye examination is unremarkable. How will you treat this patient?

Options:
  Trabeculectomy or Goniotomy

Explanation:
The signs of photophobia, epiphora (watering), enlarged hazy cornea (buphthalmos), and high IOP are classic for congenital glaucoma. This is a surgical emergency. The definitive treatment is angle surgery, typically a goniotomy or trabeculotomy, to improve aqueous outflow and normalize the pressure.

================================================================================
Question 534 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#102. A 2-week-old infant is brought to eye OPD with a white pupillary reflex in both eyes since birth and a left eye microcornea. There is no view of the posterior segment. What is the most important immediate step?

Options:
  Urgent referral for examination under anesthesia

Explanation:
Bilateral leukocoria (white reflex) in a newborn is an ocular emergency. The differential includes congenital cataracts, retinoblastoma, and other serious conditions. An urgent referral for an examination under anesthesia (EUA) is required to establish a definitive diagnosis (with ultrasound and detailed exam) and plan immediate management to prevent irreversible vision loss.

================================================================================
Question 535 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#103. A 60-year-old male presented to you with a sudden, profound loss of vision in his left eye. On examination, you find a 'cherry red spot' on the fundus. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
  Central retinal artery occlusion

Explanation:
A 'cherry red spot' at the macula is the pathognomonic sign of a Central Retinal Artery Occlusion (CRAO). It occurs because the ischemic inner retina becomes pale and opaque, contrasting with the reddish color of the underlying choroid, which remains visible through the thin, separately-supplied fovea.

================================================================================
Question 536 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#104. A 65-year-old hypertensive patient presented with decreased vision in both eyes. Visual field examination is consistent with a right homonymous hemianopia. Which is the most likely location of the lesion?

Options:
  Left optic tract

Explanation:
A homonymous hemianopia is a visual field loss on the same side of both eyes (e.g., the right side of vision is gone in both the left and right eye). This type of defect is always caused by a lesion in the retrochiasmal pathway (optic tract, radiation, or visual cortex). A right homonymous hemianopia is caused by a lesion in the left side of the brain, such as the left optic tract.

================================================================================
Question 537 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#105. A 12-year-old child was brought to you with complaints of itchy eyes that are most severe in the summer season and are recurrent. On examination, the child is having VA of 6/18 in the right eye and 6/9 in the left eye. He is having papillae on the upper palpebral conjunctiva, and perilimbal congestion with small follicles (Trantas dots). What is the most probable diagnosis in this case?

Options:
✓ Vernal keratoconjunctivitis

Explanation:
The combination of severe itching, seasonal recurrence (summer/spring), giant papillae on the upper tarsus, and perilimbal Horner-Trantas dots are all classic and pathognomonic features of Vernal Keratoconjunctivitis (VKC).

================================================================================
Question 538 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#106. A 70-year-old woman came to OPD with sudden decreased vision in her left eye with an associated headache. The patient was also complaining of jaw claudication and pain on combing her hair. On examination, her visual acuity in the left eye is 6/60 and in the right eye is 6/6. Her ESR was 60. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
  Arteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy

Explanation:
The presentation of sudden, severe vision loss in an elderly patient, combined with systemic symptoms of giant cell arteritis (headache, jaw claudication, scalp tenderness) and a high ESR, is classic for Arteritic Anterior Ischemic Optic Neuropathy (A-AION). This is a medical emergency requiring immediate high-dose systemic steroids.

Question 539 (KMC - 2024)

Question:
Q#107. A 65-year-old man presented with decreased vision in both eyes for the last 2 years. On examination, he has a visual acuity of CF 3 meters in both eyes. On examination, the anterior segment is normal in both eyes and there is retinal pigment epithelial atrophy with scars in the macular region. He is non-diabetic and non-hypertensive. What is the most probable diagnosis?

Options:
✓ Age-related macular degeneration

Explanation:
The gradual bilateral central vision loss in an elderly patient with findings of macular RPE atrophy and scarring is the classic presentation of the advanced 'dry' or atrophic form of Age-Related Macular Degeneration (AMD).

Question 540 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#108. A 30-year-old female presented to eye OPD with sudden onset redness in the right eye (RE) with photophobia and decreased vision. Systemic history is positive for low back pain. On examination, her visual acuity is 6/18 in RE. There is conjunctival congestion, keratic precipitates (KPs), and cells in the anterior chamber. Fundus examination is unremarkable. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
  Acute Anterior Uveitis

Explanation:
The ocular signs—photophobia, decreased vision, KPs, and anterior chamber cells—are definitive for acute anterior uveitis. The association with low back pain suggests a systemic inflammatory condition like ankylosing spondylitis, which is strongly linked to this type of uveitis.

Question 541 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#109. A 55-year-old woman presented with pain in the left eye since yesterday. She is giving a history of left phacoemulsification 3 days back. On examination, the eye is counting fingers in the left eye with conjunctival congestion and corneal edema along with hypopyon in the anterior chamber. What is the most appropriate treatment?

Options:
✓ Intravitreal antibiotics

Explanation:
The patient has acute post-operative endophthalmitis. This is an ocular emergency requiring immediate treatment to save the eye. The standard of care is an urgent vitreous tap for culture and injection of broad-spectrum intravitreal antibiotics (e.g., vancomycin and ceftazidime).

================================================================================
Question 542 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#110. A 55-year-old female patient presented with decreased vision in the left eye along with a complaint of watering from the left eye. On examination, you found that the patient is having a mature cataract in the left eye with a positive regurgitation test of mucopurulent discharge on the same side. Which procedure will you prefer to do first?

Options:
  Treat the lacrimal sac infection before cataract surgery

Explanation:
A positive regurgitation test indicates chronic dacryocystitis (infection of the lacrimal sac). Performing intraocular surgery like cataract removal in the presence of an active infection is an absolute contraindication due to the extremely high risk of post-operative endophthalmitis. The infection must be treated first, often requiring a DCR surgery.

================================================================================
Question 543 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#111. A 4-month-old infant is brought by parents to Eye OPD with a white pupillary reflex in the right eye since birth. The infant is not fixing and following light with the right eye. EUA reveals retinoblastoma with a retro-lental mass with vitreous seeds in the right eye. Intraocular pressure is 35 in the right eye and 8 in the left eye. Left eye examination is unremarkable. MRI shows no invasion of the optic nerve. How will you treat this patient?

Options:
  Enucleation

Explanation:
In unilateral, advanced retinoblastoma with poor visual potential, high IOP (neovascular glaucoma), and vitreous seeding, enucleation (surgical removal of the eye) is the standard treatment. It is life-saving, removes the tumor completely, and prevents metastatic spread.

================================================================================
Question 544 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#112. A 6-month-old infant is brought by parents with complaints of watering, redness, photophobia, and not fixing and following light with the right eye. On examination, he has nystagmus with an enlarged, hazy cornea. Fundus examination is not possible due to poor view. IOP is 30 in the right eye under sedation. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
  Congenital glaucoma

Explanation:
The classic triad of epiphora (watering), photophobia, and blepharospasm, combined with the signs of an enlarged, hazy cornea (buphthalmos and corneal edema) and elevated IOP, is pathognomonic for congenital glaucoma.

Question 545 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#113. A 50-year-old man presents to the eye OPD with severe pain in his eye for the last 3 days. He gives a history of phacoemulsification and IOL implantation 5 days ago. On examination, his eye has a visual acuity of 6/60 with eyelid swelling, conjunctival congestion and chemosis. The cornea has a fibrous exudate and hypopyon. What is the most probable diagnosis?

Options:
✓ Post-operative endophthalmitis

Explanation:
This is a classic presentation of acute post-operative endophthalmitis: recent surgery followed by severe pain, vision loss, eyelid swelling, intense inflammation (chemosis, exudate), and hypopyon (pus in the anterior chamber).

Question 546 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#114. A 50-year-old presents with dimness of vision in the right eye for a long time. On examination, his visual acuity is 6/36 in the right eye, and there is a right RAPD. Anterior segment examination is unremarkable. The right fundus shows optic atrophy, cupping of the disc, and a significant visual field defect. What is the most appropriate next step of investigation in this patient?

Options:
  Neuroimaging (CT/MRI)

Explanation:
The patient has signs of unilateral optic neuropathy (decreased vision, RAPD, optic atrophy) that does not appear to be glaucomatous (as glaucoma is typically bilateral). It is crucial to rule out a compressive lesion affecting the anterior visual pathway (optic nerve, chiasm). Neuroimaging, specifically an MRI of the brain and orbits, is mandatory.

Question 547 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#115. An 80-year-old Caucasian smoker woman complains of recent problems with reading vision, specifically words appearing distorted and blank patches being present. Her vision is often good in brighter light. On examination, her visual acuity is 6/36 both eyes with no further improvement with refraction. Fundus shows numerous intermediate-size soft drusen in both eyes with choroidal neovascularization (CNV). How will you manage?

Options:
✓ Intravitreal anti-VEGF injections

Explanation:
The patient has wet (neovascular) Age-Related Macular Degeneration (AMD), as evidenced by the presence of choroidal neovascularization (CNV). The standard of care and first-line treatment for wet AMD is intravitreal injections of anti-VEGF (Vascular Endothelial Growth Factor) agents, such as ranibizumab, bevacizumab, or aflibercept.

Question 548 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#116. An 80-year-old Caucasian smoker woman complains of recent problems with reading vision, specifically words appearing distorted and blank patches being present. Her vision is often good in brighter light. On examination, her visual acuity is 6/36 both eyes with no further improvement with refraction. Pupils are reactive to light. Fundus shows numerous intermediate-size soft drusen in both eyes with choroidal neovascularization (CNV). How will you manage?

Options:
✓ Intravitreal anti-VEGF injections

Explanation:
This is a duplicate question. The patient has wet (neovascular) Age-Related Macular Degeneration (AMD), as evidenced by choroidal neovascularization (CNV). The standard of care is intravitreal anti-VEGF therapy to inhibit the growth of abnormal blood vessels and reduce leakage.

Question 549 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#117. A 60-year-old man presented to eye OPD with transient visual loss in the left eye that lasts for a few minutes. These episodes occur several times a day. Ocular examination is unremarkable. Which of the following is the most appropriate initial investigation?

Options:
  Carotid Doppler Ultrasound

Explanation:
The patient is describing amaurosis fugax, a transient monocular vision loss often described as a 'curtain coming down'. This is most commonly caused by an embolus from an atherosclerotic plaque in the ipsilateral carotid artery. A Carotid Doppler ultrasound is the most appropriate initial investigation to assess for carotid artery stenosis.

================================================================================
Question 550 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#118. A 45-year-old laborer presented to your clinic with a complaint of watering and irritation, more frequently during sun exposure. On slit-lamp examination, you observe a wedge-shaped growth of the conjunctiva that extends onto the nasal cornea. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
  Pterygium

Explanation:
A pterygium is a fibrovascular, wing-shaped growth of the conjunctiva that encroaches onto the cornea. It is strongly associated with chronic UV light exposure, as seen in laborers who work outdoors. A pinguecula is similar but does not cross onto the cornea.

================================================================================
Question 551 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#119. A 60-year-old male presented with sudden painless loss of vision in his right eye. On fundus examination, there were flame-shaped hemorrhages in all quadrants, dilated tortuous vessels, and a swollen optic disc, consistent with Central Retinal Vein Occlusion. Which complication will you expect in this patient 3 months after the event?

Options:
✓ Neovascular glaucoma
  Angle closure glaucoma
  Open angle glaucoma

Explanation:
The signs describe ischemic Central Retinal Vein Occlusion (CRVO). A major complication of ischemic CRVO is severe retinal ischemia which triggers the release of VEGF, leading to rubeosis iridis (new vessels on the iris) and subsequent neovascular glaucoma. This typically develops around 90 days (3 months) after the initial event.

================================================================================
Question 552 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#120. A 4-year-old girl has convergent squint of the left eye. On examination, the eye is enlarged in size. What is the most likely underlying cause of this presentation?

Options:
  Congenital glaucoma

Explanation:
The question appears to be malformed. However, interpreting the key signs: in a young child, an enlarged eye (buphthalmos) combined with a squint is highly suggestive of congenital glaucoma. The high intraocular pressure causes the globe to stretch and enlarge, which can also disrupt vision and lead to a secondary strabismus (squint).

Question 553 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50 year old male patient is coming to specialist outpatient complaining of difficulty in swallowing for liquids and solids, regurgitation of food and occasionally chest pain for the last 2 years. He has history of hospitalization for chest infection 9 months back. Examination of the neck is unremarkable and indirect laryngoscopy shows pooling of saliva. Low molecular weight barium swallow shows classical bird beak appearance. What is the most effective treatment for this disease?

Options:
  Botulinum toxin injection
✓ Cardiomyotomy with partial fundoplication
  Cardiomyotomy with complete fundoplication
  Oesophageal dilatation
  Oesophegectomy

Explanation:
The progressive dysphagia for both liquids and solids, regurgitation, pooling of saliva, and the classic 'bird beak appearance' on barium swallow are diagnostic of achalasia cardia. Cardiomyotomy (Heller myotomy) with partial fundoplication is the most effective surgical treatment, reducing lower esophageal sphincter pressure while preventing significant reflux.

Question 554 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A 55-year-old male patient is scheduled for nasal surgery for Ethmoidal polypi. Endoscopic examination shows no landmarks which suggest previous surgery. Further examination shows mucopus and fungal debris. CT Scan also shows heterogeneous opacities in all sinuses. What is the first surgical step in this case?

Options:
✓ a. Antrostomy
  b. Inferior Turbinectomy
  c. Middle Turbinectomy
  d. Polypectomy
  e. Uncinectomy

Explanation:
In revision FESS where landmarks (like the uncinate process) are absent or distorted, identifying the maxillary sinus ostium (Antrostomy) or the orbit is a key initial step for orientation.

Question 555 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50-year-old male patient is coming to specialist outpatient complaining of difficulty in swallowing for liquids and solids, regurgitation of food, and occasionally chest pain for the last 2 years. He has a history of hospitalization for chest infection 9 months back. Examination of the neck is unremarkable, and indirect laryngoscopy shows pooling of saliva. Low molecular weight barium swallow shows classical bird beak appearance. What is the most effective treatment for this disease?

Options:
  a. Botulinum toxin injection
✓ b. Cardiomyotomy with partial fundoplication
  c. Cardiomyotomy with complete fundoplication
  d. Oesophageal dilatation
  e. Oesophagectomy

Explanation:
Achalasia (Bird beak sign) is best treated surgically by Heller's Cardiomyotomy combined with a partial fundoplication (to prevent reflux).

Question 556 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 70 years old lady has an ulcerated nodular mass on her right medial lower eyelid for the last 4 years. The central ulceration has raised rolled edges with dilated blood vessels over the lateral margins.Preauricular and submandibular lymph nodes are not palpable. The most likely diagnosis is

Options:
✓ Basal Cell Carcinoma
  Kaposi Sarcoma
  Merkle Cell Tumor
  Sebaceous Gland Carcinoma
  Squamous Cell Carcinoma

Explanation:
The clinical features of an elderly patient presenting with a long-standing (4 years), slowly growing, ulcerated nodular mass on the medial lower eyelid, characterized by raised rolled edges and telangiectatic vessels (dilated blood vessels), are highly suggestive of basal cell carcinoma (BCC). The absence of palpable regional lymph nodes is consistent with BCC, which rarely metastasizes.

Question 557 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 20 years girl presents to EYE-OPD with a gradual painless swelling in the left lower eyelid for the last two months Palpation of the mass indicates a small nodule in the substance of the eyelid not involving the lid margin. The overlying skin is normal and mobile and eversion of the eyelid shows a mass in the tarsal plate. The girl is a known patient of blepharitis. The most probable diagnosis is:

Options:
  Basal cell carcinoma
✓ Chalazion
  Hordeolum Externum - (Stye.
  Hordeolum Internum
  Squamous cell carcinoma

Explanation:
A gradual, painless, localized swelling within the eyelid, palpable as a nodule in the tarsal plate, with normal and mobile overlying skin, and often associated with a history of blepharitis, is the classic description of a chalazion. It is a chronic lipogranulomatous inflammation of a meibomian gland.

Question 558 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 22 year old boy present to the casualty with severe pain in the right eye for the last two hours. On examination the right lower eyelid is red and swollen with severe tenderness. The lower eyelid margin is normal. On eversion of the eyelid there is appearance of pus shining through the conjunctiva. The probable diagnosis is: OPTION LIST

Options:
  Basal cell carcinoma
  Chalazion
  Hordeolum Externum - (Stye.
✓ Hordeolum Internum
  Squamous cell carcinoma

Explanation:
A hordeolum internum (internal stye) is an acute suppurative inflammation of a meibomian gland within the tarsal plate. It typically presents with severe pain, redness, swelling, and tenderness of the eyelid, and often shows pus pointing or shining through the conjunctiva on eversion of the eyelid.

Question 559 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 32 years old female patient presents to eye OPD with complaints of drooping of the eyelids since last 1 year. She has complaints of increased fatigability. The ptosis is more during the evening and relatively less when she wakes up in the morning. Her ice-pack test is also positive. What is the most likely diagnosis in her case?

Options:
  Ptosis due to traumatic disinsertion of levator muscle
  Ptosis due to oculomotor (3rd nerve. palsy
✓ Ptosis due to Myasthenia gravis
  Ptosis due to Horner's syndrome
  Ptosis due to orbicularis overaction

Explanation:
The characteristic features of fluctuating ptosis that worsens with fatigue (more in the evening, less in the morning) and is associated with increased fatigability, along with a positive ice-pack test (temporary improvement in ptosis with cold application), are highly diagnostic of ptosis due to myasthenia gravis, an autoimmune neuromuscular disorder affecting the neuromuscular junction.

Question 560 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 65 years old man present to EYE-OPD with irritation and foreign body sensation in the left eye for the last one month. On examination the left lower eyelid margin is turned towards the eyeball. The overlying skin is normal. On eversion of the eyelid the lid margin comes to its normal position but inverts again on forceful closure of the eyelid. The most probable diagnosis is:

Options:
  Acute spastic Entropion
  Cicatricial Entropion
  Congenital Entropion
  Epiblepharon,
✓ Involutional Entropion

Explanation:
In an elderly patient, inward turning of the lower eyelid margin (entropion) due to age-related changes, with normal overlying skin and the ability to temporarily correct the lid position on eversion but recurrence on forceful closure (due to preseptal orbicularis override), is characteristic of involutional (senile) entropion.

================================================================================
Question 561 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Drooping of upper eyelid absence of eyelid crease since birth occurs in:

Options:
  Congenital entropion
✓ Simple congenital ptosis
  Horner syndrome
  Congenital 3rdnerve palsy
  Congenital ectropion.

Explanation:
Simple congenital ptosis is characterized by the drooping of the upper eyelid present since birth, often associated with poor levator palpebrae superioris muscle function and an absent or poorly formed upper eyelid crease. This is a common form of congenital ptosis.

================================================================================
Question 562 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 40 years old patient presents to eye OPD with complaints of sudden onset of painful swelling near the medial canthus area. The swelling is tender to touch and red. The patient had previously watering from the eye. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Chronic Dacrocystitis
✓ Acute on chronic dacrocystitis
  Lacrimal gland tumor
  Stye
  Chalazion

Explanation:
The presentation of sudden onset painful, tender, and red swelling near the medial canthus, especially with a history of previous watering (epiphora, suggesting chronic obstruction), is highly indicative of an acute exacerbation of chronic dacryocystitis, often termed 'acute on chronic dacryocystitis'.

================================================================================
Question 563 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A mother has brought her 8 years old son to eye OPD for concerns of redness of eyes and itching. According to the mother, her son frequently rubs his eyes and is unable to go out in the sun due to photophobia. The condition is there since last 4 years. It is typically worse in spring and summers. On examination the patient has cobble-stone papillae on his tarsal conjunctiva. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Bacterial Conjunctivitis
✓ Vernal Kerato-conjunctivitis
  Congenital Naso-lacrimal duct obstruction
  Viral Conjunctivitis
  Corneal Ulcer

Explanation:
The clinical picture of a young boy with chronic, seasonal (spring/summer) itching, redness, photophobia, and the pathognomonic finding of 'cobble-stone' papillae (giant papillae) on the tarsal conjunctiva is classic for Vernal Keratoconjunctivitis (VKC).

================================================================================
Question 564 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 14 yrs old boy, who is a known patient of allergic conjunctivitis, presents to eye OPD complaining of painless, progressive dimness of vision. On examination the boy has progressive myopia and astigmatism. Distant direct ophthalmoscopy shows oil droplet reflex. There is bulging of the lower eyelid when the patient looks down. Retinoscopy shows irregular scissor reflex. Slit lamp exam shows thinning and forward bulging of the central cornea. What is the diagnosis?

Options:
  Arcus senile
  Keratoglobus
  Band keratopathy
  Lipid keratopathy
✓ Keratoconus

Explanation:
The clinical presentation is classic for keratoconus: progressive myopia and astigmatism, oil droplet reflex (Charleaux sign), bulging of the lower lid in downgaze (Munson's sign), scissoring reflex on retinoscopy, and corneal thinning with ectasia. A history of allergic conjunctivitis (and eye rubbing) is a strong risk factor.

Question:
A 14 years boy, who is a know patient to allergic conjunctivitis, presents to eye OPD complaining of painless, progressive dimness of vision. On examination the boy has progressive myopia and astigmatism. Distant direct ophthalmoscopy shows oil droplets reflex. There is bulging of the lower eyelid when the patient looks down. Question 567 (NWSM - 2023)

Question:
A 2 months old infant is brought to eye OPD by his mother having noticed a white pupil in his left eye. After examination a diagnosis of congenital cataract is made by the Ophthalmologist. Which of the following is the most common type of congenital cataract presenting with visual impairment?

Options:
  Subcapsular cataract
  Nuclear
  Polar
✓ Lamellar
  Oil droplet

Explanation:
Lamellar (zonular) cataract is the most common type of congenital cataract. It typically affects a zone of the lens and is frequently associated with visual impairment.

  Krypton laser

Question 569 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
55 Years old lady comes to eye OPD with the complaint of gradual dimness of vision in both eyes. On examination the intraocular pressure in both eyes is 30mmHg. Anterior segments are normal. Fundi show advanced optic disc cupping Visual fields show double arcuate and temporal wedge scotomas. The diagnosis of the lady is :

Options:
  Cataract
  Age related macular degeneration
  Diabetic retinopathy
✓ Glaucoma
  Hypertensive retinopathy

Explanation:
High IOP (30 mmHg), advanced optic disc cupping, and characteristic visual field defects (arcuate scotoma) are pathognomonic for Glaucoma (specifically POAG given the normal anterior segment).

Question 570 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 40 days baby is brought by his parents for evaluation of watering from eyes. On examination, the patient has excessive lacrimation. He has cloudy corneas with Haab'sstriae. His corneal diameters are 13 mm. His intra-ocular pressure is 28 mm of Hg in both eyes. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Congenital Naso-lacrimal duct obstruction
✓ Congenital glaucoma
  Congenital cataract
  Retinoblastoma
  Bacterial corneal ulcer

Explanation:
Haab's striae (breaks in Descemet's membrane), enlarged corneal diameter (>12mm in infant), cloudy corneas, and elevated IOP are definitive signs of Congenital Glaucoma.

Question 571 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 70 yrs old lady has developed loss of vision and pain in her right eye. On examination the patient has advanced diabetic eye disease, new vessels over the iris and angle of the anterior chamber with adhesion formation. The intraocular pressure is 50mmg. This type of glaucoma is called

Options:
  Pigmentary glaucoma
  Inflammatory giaucoma
✓ Neovascular glaucoma
  Phacomorphic glaucoma
  Pseudoexfoliation

Explanation:
Glaucoma caused by the growth of new vessels (neovascularization) in the angle, often secondary to ischemic conditions like diabetic retinopathy, is Neovascular Glaucoma.

Question 572 (NWSM - 2023)

Question:

Options:
  Congenital cataracts
✓ Congenital glaucoma (buphthalmos)
  Congenital rubella infection
  Megalocomea
  Birth trauma to cornea

Corneal haze and enlargement (buphthalmos) in an infant are key signs of Congenital Glaucoma.

Question 573 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
58 yr old lady having cataracts in both eyes presents to eye emergency department with the complaint of sudden onset of pain and gross dimness of vision in her right eye. On examination of the right eye the cornea is edematous, anterior chamber is shallow, pupil is mid-dilated, with dense cataract, intraocular pressure is 60mmg. The lady is suffering from :

Options:
  Pigmentary glaucoma
  Inflammatory glaucoma
  Neovascular glaucoma
✓ Phacomorphic glaucoma
  Psudoexfoliation glaucoma

Explanation:
Phacomorphic glaucoma is a secondary angle-closure glaucoma caused by an intumescent (swollen) cataract pushing the iris forward and closing the angle. The presence of a 'dense cataract' (likely intumescent) causing a shallow chamber and high IOP fits this diagnosis.

================================================================================
Question 574 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 30 yrs old man is suffering from right chronic anterior Uveitis for the last 3 months. On examination he has aqeous flare and cells in the right anterior chamber. He has 360 degrees posterior synechiae and intraocular pressure is 40mmHg. The man is suffering from

Options:
  Pigmentary glaucoma
✓ Inflammatory glaucoma
  Neovascular glaucoma
  Phacomorphic glaucoma
  Pseudoexfoliation

Explanation:
Glaucoma secondary to uveitis (inflammatory glaucoma) is caused by inflammation. 360-degree posterior synechiae (seclusio pupillae) cause pupillary block and iris bombe, leading to angle closure and high IOP. It is a complication of the inflammation.

Question 575 (NWSM - 2023)

Question:
A 45 years old lady visits Ophthalmology outpatient department with complaints of mild discomfort in her eyes since last 2 months. It is associated with gradual deterioration of vision. On examination, she has intracular pressure of 28 mm of Hg in both her eyes. The cup to disc ratio in her eyes are 0.8. What is the first line drug used to treat her condition?

Options:
  Cyclopentolate eye drops
  Dexamethasone eye drops
  Infusion Mannitol
✓ Prostaglandin analogues
  Intravitreal anti-VEGF

Explanation:
Prostaglandin analogues are the first-line medical therapy for Primary Open Angle Glaucoma.

Question 576 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 70 years old gentleman who is otherwise fit presents to eye OPD with gradual progressive visual loss for last 2 years. His visual acuity were recorded as 6/24 both eyes and improved to 6/9 with pinhole. He was previously emmetropic but now his refractive error was -2.00 diopter. Pupillary responses and intraocular pressures are normal. 0m distant direct ophthalmoscopy there is media opacity which does not move with movement of the eye. The most likely diagnosis is :

Options:
  Asteroid hyalosis
  Corneal opacity
✓ Nuclear sclerosis cataract
  Posterior vitreous detachment
  Vitreous haemorhage

Explanation:
The myopic shift (-2.00 D in a previously emmetropic elderly patient) is classic for Nuclear Sclerosis. The media opacity being stationary (in the lens) rather than floating (vitreous) confirms cataract.

Question:

✓ Retinal detachment

✓ Retinoblastoma
  Refractive error

Question 580 (NWSM - 2023)

Question:

Eye examination reveals a large whitish retinal mass involving more than half of the retina and protruding into the vitreous cavity. A clinical diagnosis of retinoblastoma is made. Options:

  Brachytherapy
  External beam radiotherapy
  Laser photocoagulation
✓ Enucleation

Explanation:
Extension into the optic nerve makes it a high-risk advanced tumor (Group E/extraocular extension risk). Enucleation is required to prevent systemic spread.

Question 603 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A I-year young boy is brought by his parents to Ophthalmology OPD. The parents want to seek opinion regarding treatment of congenital cataract in Right eye of their infant. What is the best treatment option in this case?

Options:
✓ Keep under observation until 2-years of age
  Perform lens matter aspiration and lens implant after 2-years of age
  Perform lens matter aspiration now and implant lens at 2-years of age
  Prescribe glasses and call for follow-up after 1 year
  Advise the parents that it will clear spontaneously

Explanation:
Congenital cataract causing visual deprivation must be removed early (usually by 4-6 weeks for unilateral) to prevent amblyopia. Lensectomy/aspiration is done. IOL implantation in infants is controversial but often delayed until 2 years, using contact lenses/glasses in the interim.

Question 607 (NWSM - 2023)

Question:
A 55 years male presented to eye OPD with severe headache and closure of his right eye. On examination the patient has right ptosis, variable limitation of inward upward and downward movements. Pupil is dilated and poor reactive to light. Outward movement of the eye is normal. The most probable diagnosis is:

Options:
✓ Paralytic exotropia
  Paralytic esotropia
  Intermitant exotropia
  Alternation exotropia
  Accomodative esotropia

Explanation:
This describes a 3rd Nerve Palsy (ptosis, mydriasis, motility limit). Since the eye is deviated outward (because lateral rectus is intact), it presents as an exotropia. It is paralytic.

Question 608 (NWSM - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 23 year old patient comes to Ophthalmology outpatient department with complaints of redness and pain in his right eye. The pain is dull, continuous and associated with decreased vision. On examination, he has circumciliary redness, mild corneal edema, and +3 cells and flare in the anterior chamber. The rest of eye examination is unremarkable. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Allergic Conjunctivitis
  Nodular Scleritis
  Bacterial conjunctivitis
✓ Anterior Uveitis
  Acute congestive glaucoma

Explanation:
The classic triad of Anterior Uveitis (Iritis/Iridocyclitis) includes deep throbbing pain, circumciliary (ciliary) flush, and the presence of cells and flare in the anterior chamber. The cells and flare are signs of active inflammation and breakdown of the blood-aqueous barrier.

Question 609 (NWSM - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 15 years old boy with vernal keratocunjunctivitis presents with gradual loss of vision in both of his eyes. He gives history of using topical dexamethasone eyedrops for many years. On examination his visual acuity in the right eye was 6/12 and left was 6/60. Both corneas were clear. His intraocular pressure was 24mmHg in the right and 30mmHg in the left eye. On fundoscopy cup-disc ratio was 0.5 in the right and 0.8 in the left eye. What is the probable diagnosis:

Options:
  Keratoconus
✓ Steroid induced glaucoma
  Optic atrophy
  Congenital glaucoma
  Keratoglobus

Explanation:
Prolonged use of topical steroids (dexamethasone) can lead to secondary open-angle glaucoma, characterized by raised intraocular pressure and glaucomatous optic nerve damage (increased cup-disc ratio). This fits the history and findings perfectly.

================================================================================
Question 610 (NWSM - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 65 yrs old lady who is a known patient of Glaucoma comes to eye OPD for visual field testing. On examination she has a central visual field of less than 10 degrees. In which category of visual impairment does she fit according to WHO classification?

Options:
  Mild visual impairment
  Moderate visual impairment
  Normal
  Severe visual impairment
✓ Blindness

Explanation:
According to the WHO classification of visual impairment, a visual field constricted to less than 10 degrees around the point of fixation constitutes Blindness (Category 3), regardless of visual acuity.

================================================================================
Question 611 (NWSM - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A young boy presents with rapid onset of right orbital swelling and pain associated with malaise and fever. On examination right eyeball is proptosed axially. There is periorbital and lid oedema with red warm overlying skin and tenderness. Extraocular movements are restricted and painful. Vision is decreased and there is relative afferent pupilary defect. CT scan brain is normal. The most probable diagnosis is:

Options:
  Pre septal orbital cellulitis
  Acute dacryocystitis
✓ Orbital cellulitis
  Cavernous sinus thrombosis
  Thyroid eye disease (orbitopathy)

Explanation:
The presence of proptosis, painful restricted eye movements (ophthalmoplegia), and decreased vision/RAPD distinguishes Orbital Cellulitis (post-septal) from Pre-septal cellulitis. Cavernous sinus thrombosis would likely present with bilateral signs or more severe neurological deficits.

Question 612 (NWSM - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 55-year-old male smoker presents with hoarseness and a progressively worsening cough. He also complains of difficulty swallowing. Laryngoscopy reveals a lesion involving the right vocal cord with irregular borders and areas of ulceration. What is the most appropriate next step?

Options:
  Empiric antibiotic therapy
  Immediate surgical excision
  Chest X-ray to evaluate for metastasis
✓ Biopsy of the lesion
  High-dose corticosteroid therapy

Explanation:
A suspicious, ulcerated laryngeal lesion in a smoker strongly suggests malignancy (Squamous Cell Carcinoma). The definitive next step to establish the diagnosis is a direct laryngoscopy and biopsy of the lesion.

Question 613 (NWSM - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50 years old obese lady is complaining of dimness of vision in both eyes. On examination her vision in the right eye is 6/60 and 6/36 in the left eye. On examination of the fundi, there are retinal microaneurysms, hard exudates, haemorrhages and macular oedema. In addition there are new vessels at the optic disc and in retinal. The lady is suffering from:

Options:
  Central retinal vein occlusion
  Hypertensive retinopathy
  Central retinal artery occlusion
✓ Diabetic retinopathy
  Toxemia of pregnancy

Explanation:
The findings of microaneurysms, dot/blot hemorrhages, hard exudates, macular edema, and specifically 'new vessels' (neovascularization) are characteristic of Proliferative Diabetic Retinopathy.

Question 614 (NWSM - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A Mother brings her 4-week-old baby to ophthalmology OPD with symptoms of epiphora and sticky mucoid discharge that accumulates on the eyelid margin and lashes. On examination, the regurgitation test is positive. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Bacterial conjunctivitis
✓ Congenital nasolacrimal duct obstruction
  Blepharitis
  Acute dacryoadenitis
  Viral keratitis

Explanation:
Epiphora and sticky discharge in a newborn with a positive regurgitation test (mucopurulent reflux upon pressure over the lacrimal sac) is diagnostic of Congenital Nasolacrimal Duct Obstruction (CNLDO).

Question 615 (NWSM - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 2 years old boy is brought to the eye OPD by his parents for concerns of misalignment of the eyes. According to the parents the patient's eyes are deviated inwards. On Hirschberg test, the eyes are convergent and deviation is present all the time and in all position of gaze. What is your most likely diagnosis?

Options:
✓ Esotropia
  Exotropia
  Hypertropia
  Hypotropia
  Orthophoria

Explanation:
Inward deviation of the eyes is termed Esotropia. Exotropia is outward deviation.

Question 616 (NWSM - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 years old gentleman comes to you because he cannot see clearly for distance. His present reading glasses are 5 years old. He is otherwise fit. His visual acuity is 6/5 in both eyes corrected with glasses. His refractive error is -2.0 diopter sphere in both eyes. On examination his pupillary responses are normal. Anterior segment examination is normal with deep anterior chambers. His intraocular pressures were recorded as 28 and 30mmHg in the right and left eye respectively. His central corneal thickness is 515 microns in each eye. On fundoscopy he has got cupping of 0.6 in both eyes with nerve fibre layer haemorrhage along the superior border of the left optic disc. Visual field examination shows arcuate scotoma in both eyes. Most likely diagnosis is:

Options:
  Myopic degeneration of the retina
  Ocular hypertension
✓ Primary open angle glaucoma
  Pseudoexfoliative glaucoma
  Primary angle closure glaucoma

Explanation:
The patient has raised Intraocular Pressure (IOP > 21 mmHg), Glaucomatous Optic Nerve damage (Cupping 0.6, RNFL hemorrhage), and Visual Field defects (Arcuate scotoma). With deep anterior chambers, the angle is open. This confirms Primary Open Angle Glaucoma.

================================================================================
Question 617 (NWSM - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50 years old male was involved in a road traffic accident 2 weeks back. He received trauma to left side of face and temporal bone. He had a concussion and was unconscious for 2 days which he spent in ICU. When he regained consciousness, the attending doctors noted asymmetry of his left side of face. The patient had a left facial palsy and even after 12 days, there was no improvement in his facial function. The test that would best indicate the chances of recovery of facial nerve at 2 weeks would be:

Options:
  Electromyography
✓ Electroneuronography
  Minimal nerve excitability test
  Maximal nerve stimulation test
  Tympanogram

Explanation:
Electroneuronography (ENoG) is the most valuable prognostic test for facial nerve paralysis when performed between 3 to 14 days post-injury (ideally around day 10-14). It quantifies the degeneration; >90% degeneration indicates a poor prognosis and may necessitate surgical decompression.

Question 618 (NWSM - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 40 years farmer came to eye opd with the complaints of redness and a triangular shaped fleshy mass at the medial limbus with the apex towards cornea for the last 1 year. The most probable diagnosis is:

Options:
✓ pterygium
  psudopterygium
  pingicula
  scleritis
  episcleritis

Explanation:
A triangular fibrovascular growth extending from the conjunctiva onto the cornea (apex towards cornea) is a Pterygium. It is common in farmers due to UV exposure.

Question 619 (NWSM - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 40 years old male presented with complaints of left side nasal bleeding and pain from the last 6 months. O/E, patient had a fungating growth arising from left maxillary sinus and extending out in the nasal cavity. There were extensive erosions but no cervical Iymhadenopathy. The next step in management of this patient would be:

Options:
✓ Biopsy under local anaesthesia
  chemotherapy
  nasal endoscopy
  surgical resection
  wait and watch

Explanation:
A fungating mass with erosions is highly suspicious for malignancy (e.g., Squamous Cell Carcinoma of the Maxillary sinus). A biopsy is required to confirm the histological diagnosis before planning definitive treatment (surgery/chemoradiation).

Question 620 (NWSM - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 65 years old man has an ulcerated nodular mass on his right medial lower eyelid for the last 4 years. The central ulceration has raised rolled edges with dilated blood vessels over the lateral margins. Regional lymph nodes are not palpable. The most likely diagnosis is:

Options:
✓ Basal Cell Carcinoma
  Kaposi Sarcoma
  Merkle Cell Tumor
  Sebaceous Gland Carcinoma
  Squamous Cell Carcinoma

Explanation:
This describes a Basal Cell Carcinoma (BCC), the most common malignant eyelid tumor, characterized by pearly rolled edges and telangiectasia.

Question 621 (NWSM - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 09 months old baby is brought to Ophthalmology outpatient department. The parents are complaining of him not following objects and inattention to light. There is no history of prematurity. On examination the patient has white pupillary reflex in both his eyes. Rest of the clinical examination is normal. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Retinoblastoma
  Coat's disease
✓ Congenital cataracts
  Retinopathy of prematurity
  Toxocariasis

Explanation:
Bilateral leukocoria (white reflex) in an infant causing visual inattention is most commonly caused by Congenital Cataracts. While Retinoblastoma is a critical differential, cataracts are statistically more common causes of bilateral leukocoria in the absence of a visible mass description, and 'inattention to light' strongly suggests dense media opacity causing deprivation.

Question 622 (NWSM - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 2-year-old boy is brought by his concerned parents to ophthalmology OPD for a white pupillary reflex in his right eye. On examination, there is a white pupillary reflex and outward deviation of the eye. B-scan ultrasonography shows an intra-ocular, calcified mass. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Toxocariasis
  Coats's disease
  Congenital cataracts
✓ Retinoblastoma
  Persistent hyperplastic primary vitreous

Explanation:
Intraocular calcification in a child with leukocoria is virtually pathognomonic for Retinoblastoma.

Question 623 (NWSM - 2025)

Question:
A 28 years old female presented to eye OPD with complaints of right eye painfull swelling at the medial canthus. There is associated epiphora and redness. on examination there is a swelling near the medial canthus, the overlying skin is red, it is tender to touch and purulent discharged is present. she most likely has:

  chronic dacryocystitis
  preseptal cellulitis
✓ acute dacryocystitis
  bacterial conjunctivitis
  acute dacryoadenitis

A painful, red, tender swelling at the medial canthus (location of the lacrimal sac) with purulent discharge is diagnostic of Acute Dacryocystitis.

Question 624 (NWSM - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 45 years old female who was on chemotherapeutics for her breast cancer presented with facial pain from the last 10 days. O/E extensive black eschars and crustings were noted in both sides of nasal cavity. She underwent FESS and aggressive surgical debridement. Post op she was put on amphotericin to control the spread of her nasal pathology. Which of the following parameters will need strict monitoring:

Options:
  Blood sugar levels
  Cardiac enzymes
  Electrolytes
  Prothrombin Time
✓ Renal profile

Explanation:
Amphotericin B is the drug of choice for Mucormycosis but is highly nephrotoxic. Monitoring the Renal Profile (Creatinine, Urea) and electrolytes (potassium) is mandatory during treatment.

Question 625 (NWSM - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 37 years female patient presented to eye opd with pain and photophobia. On Slit lamp examination she is having corneal ulcer with feathery edges. Following is the most helpful investigation?

Options:
  Bacterial culture
  Viral profile
✓ Methamine silver
  Culture media enriched with E-coli
  Interferon Gamma releasing assay

Explanation:
A corneal ulcer with 'feathery edges' and satellite lesions is characteristic of Fungal Keratitis. Methamine silver (Gomori Methenamine Silver) is a special stain used to visualize fungal elements.

Question 626 (NWSM - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 2-year boy is brought to the outpatient department with right upper eyelid droop since birth. Examination reveals an absent right upper lid crease and 5 mm of ptosis. Which surgical procedure is the most appropriate?

Options:
✓ Frontalis Sling
  Levator resection
  Fasanella Servat procedure
  Observation
  Aponeurotic strengthening

Explanation:
Severe congenital ptosis (>4mm) with poor levator function (indicated by the absent lid crease) is best treated with a Frontalis Sling operation, which couples the lid to the frontalis muscle.

Question 627 (NWSM - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 years old male patient came to Ophthalmology outpatient department with complaints of decreased vision in his right eye since last 1 year. The decreased vision is painless, gradual and not associated with any trauma. The visual acuity in right eye is counting fingers close to the eye. On examination he has white pupillary reflex. Rest of the clinical examination is normal. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Retinoblastoma
  Relative afferent pupillary defect
✓ Age related cataract
  Coat's disease
  Age related macular degeneration

Explanation:
Painless, gradual vision loss in an elderly patient leading to a white pupil implies a Mature (or Hypermature) Senile Cataract. Retinoblastoma is a childhood tumor.

Question 628 (NWSM - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 42 yr old man presents to eye OPD with complaint of transient obscuration of vision in both eyes lasting 10-15 seconds many times a day. He also has a headache which is more severe early in the morning and is aggravated by coughing, sneezing and straining. He also reports sudden episodes of nausea and vomiting. Visual acuity and pupils are normal. On fundoscopy optic discs are hyperemic, elevated with blurred margins and engorged veins at the disc margins with absent venous pulsation. MRI brain shows a tumor in the posterior cranial fossa. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
✓ Papilloedema
  Anterior ischemic optic neuropathy
  Pseudopapilloedema
  Optic neuritis

Explanation:
Bilateral optic disc swelling secondary to raised intracranial pressure (evidenced by morning headaches, vomiting, transient visual obscurations, and an intracranial tumor) is the definition of Papilloedema.

================================================================================
Question 629 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 45 years male presented to eye OPD with a complaint of right inward deviation in primary position and sudden diplopia. Extra ocular movements are normal except right lateral rectus restriction. Diplopia increases on ipsilateral gaze. What’s the probable diagnosis:

Options:
  a. 3rd nerve palsy
✓ b. 6th nerve palsy
  c. Duane retraction syndrome
  d. Convergence spasm
  e. Restrictive thyroid ophthalmopathy

Explanation:
The 6th cranial nerve (Abducens) innervates the lateral rectus. Palsy results in an inability to abduct the eye (lateral rectus restriction), leading to esotropia (inward deviation) and diplopia that worsens on looking towards the affected side.

================================================================================
Question 630 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 65-year-old patient presents with complaints of difficulty in swallowing, especially when consuming solid foods. He also mentions occasional regurgitation of undigested food, often accompanied by foul-smelling breath. On examination, you notice a bulge in the neck region during swallowing. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  a. Tonsillitis
  b. Gastroesophageal reflux disease
✓ c. Zenker's Diverticulum
  d. Laryngitis
  e. Laryngocele

Explanation:
Dysphagia, regurgitation of undigested food, halitosis, and a neck lump are characteristic signs of a pharyngeal pouch (Zenker's Diverticulum).

Question 631 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A three years female child is brought to eye opd with convergent squint for the last 6 months. Extraocular movements and fundi are normal. Ac/A ratio is normal. Cycloplegic refraction shows hypermetropia of 6 diopters . eyes are staright with glasses. Whats your probable diagnosis:

Options:
✓ a. Accomodative esotropia
  b. Infantile esotropia
  c. Exotropia
  d. Paralytic esotropia
  e. Paralytic exotropia

Explanation:
Esotropia that is fully corrected by hypermetropic glasses indicates refractive accommodative esotropia.

Question 632 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 28 years lady came to eye OPD with the complaint of a painless swelling in the left upper lid with normal overlying skin. Palpation of the lesion indicates a small nodule in the substance of upper eyelid. Conjunctiva of the upper eyelid is red over the nodule. The most probable diagnosis is:

Options:
✓ a. Chalazion
  b. Externum Hordeolum (Stye)
  c. Basal cells carcinoma
  d. Squamous cells carcinoma
  e. Sebaceous gland carcinoma

Explanation:
A painless nodule within the substance of the eyelid (tarsal plate) is characteristic of a Chalazion (internal hordeolum/meibomian cyst). The conjunctival redness over the nodule confirms its internal location.

Question 633 (NWSM - 2023)

Question:
13 years boy presents to eye OPD with pain in the right lower eyelid. The lesion is tender and the eyelid margin is red and edematous.pus is pointing towards the eyelid margin. What’s the most probable diagnosis:

  a. Chalazion

  d. Squamous cells carcinoma
  e. Sebaceous gland carcinoma

Explanation:
A painful, tender swelling at the eyelid margin with pus pointing externally is a Hordeolum Externum (Stye).

Question 634 (NWSM - 2023)

Question:
A 60 years old male presents with acute onset of right orbital pain, he has periocullar redness and swelling. Conjunctival injection, chemosis, lid edema and proptosis is present. The size of extra ocular muscle is normal. Thyroid function tests are normal. Whats the most probable diagnosis:

Options:
✓ a. Orbital cellulitis
  b. Preceptal cellulitis
  c. Thyroid Eye Disease
  d. Idiopathic orbital eye disease
  e. Dermoid cyst of the orbit

Explanation:
The presence of proptosis, chemosis, and pain distinguishes orbital cellulitis from preseptal cellulitis. Normal muscles and thyroid function exclude thyroid eye disease.

Question 635 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 3 years old male child is brought to eye OPD with a complaint of outward deviation of the right eye. According to the mother of the child the deviation is only seen when the kid is fatigued, ill health and in bright light. The child usually closes one eye in bright light. Cover uncover test shows exophoria. Whats your probable diagnosis:

Options:
  a. Paralytic exotopia
  b. Paralytic esotropia
✓ c. Intermittant exotropia
  d. Alternation exotropia
  e. Infantile esotropia

Explanation:
Exotropia that manifests intermittently (e.g., during fatigue or bright light) and is controlled by fusion (phoria on testing) is Intermittent Exotropia.

Question:
A 3-year young boy is brought by his concerned parents after they noticed white pupillary reflex in his left eye. On examination, you found the cornea and lens are clear.   c. Toxocariasis
  d. Toxoplasmosis
  e. Congenital Cataract

Leukocoria (white reflex) with an intraocular mass showing calcification on ultrasound is highly specific for Retinoblastoma.

Question 637 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
1 year old male baby is presented to eye OPD with severe swelling of eyelids and forward bulging of the eyeball with restricted extraocular movements and the patient has fever. The most probable diagnosis is:

Options:
✓ a. Orbital cellulitis
  b. Preceptal cellulitis
  c. Thyroid eye disease
  d. Idiopathic orbital eye disease
  e. Dermoid cyst of the orbit

Explanation:
Severe eyelid swelling with proptosis (forward bulging), restricted eye movements, and fever indicates infection posterior to the orbital septum, which is Orbital Cellulitis.

Question 638 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 4-years old patient has Retinoblastoma involving her right eye. The patient is advised to undergo complete removal of the eyeball. The rest of orbital contents will not be excised. What is the procedure described above named?

Options:
  a. Evisceration
  b. Classical exenteration
  c. Lid sparing (Modified) exenteration
✓ d. Enucleation
  e. Eyeball Resection

Explanation:
Removal of the entire eyeball while leaving the orbital contents (muscles, fat) behind is called Enucleation. This is the standard surgery for retinoblastoma.

Question 639 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
An 8 years old male child is presented to eye OPD with severe swelling and redness of right upper and lower eyelids. His extra ocular movements are normal. Whats your probable diagnosis:

Options:
  a. Orbital cellulitis
✓ b. Preceptal cellulitis
  c. Thyroid eye disease
  d. Idiopathic orbital eye disease
  e. Dermoid cyst of the orbit

Explanation:
Eyelid swelling and redness with *normal* extraocular movements and no proptosis suggests the infection is limited to the tissues anterior to the orbital septum, known as Preseptal Cellulitis.

A middle aged women who is a known patient of hyperlipidemia presents with bilateral slightly raised creamy-yellowish, plaque like lesions in the skin of upper eyelid near the medial canthus. The most probable diagnosis is:

Options:
  a. Chalazion
  b. Externum Hordeolum (Stye)

Yellowish plaques on the eyelids, often near the medial canthus, associated with hyperlipidemia are Xanthelasma.

Question 643 (NWSM - 2023)

Question:
An 80 years old female presents to eye OPD with the complaints of persistent right epiphora. On examination the right lower eyelid is everted away from the eyeball. She has right chronic conjunctivitis and exposure keratitis. the most probable diagnosis is:

Options:
  a. Trichiases
✓ b. Ectropion
  c. Entropion
  d. Districhiases
  e. Psudo districhiases

Explanation:
Eversion (outward turning) of the lower eyelid is called Ectropion. It causes epiphora and exposure problems.

Question 644 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 1-year young boy is brought by his parents to Ophthalmology OPD. The parents want to seek opinion regarding treatment of congenital cataract in Right eye of their infant. What is the best treatment option in this case?

Options:
  a. Keep under observation until 2-years of age
  b. Perform lens matter aspiration and lens implant after 2-years of age
✓ c. Perform lens matter aspiration now and implant lens at 2-years of age
  d. Prescribe glasses and call for follow-up after 1 year
  e. Advise the parents that it will clear spontaneously

Explanation:
Congenital cataract requires early surgical removal (aspiration) to prevent deep amblyopia. Intraocular lens (IOL) implantation is often deferred until the eye has grown (around 2 years), using contact lenses or glasses in the interim.

Question 647 (NWSM - 2023)

Question:
A 6 months male baby is brought to eye OPD with inward deviation of eyes for the last two months. On examination the angle of deviation is 60 prism diopter. There is no refractive error and fundi are normal. The most probable diagnosis

Options:
  a. Accomodative esotropia
✓ b. Infantile esotropia
  c. Exotropia
  d. Paralytic esotropia
  e. Paralytic exotropia

Explanation:
Infantile (Congenital) Esotropia is characterized by large angle deviation appearing within the first 6 months of life, usually without significant refractive error.

Question 648 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 55 years male presented to eye OPD with severe headache and closure of his right eye. On examination the patient has right ptosis, variable limitation of inward upward and downward movements. Pupil is dilated and poor reactive to light. Outward movement of the eye is normal. The most probable diagnosis is:

Options:
✓ a. Paralytic exotropia
  b. Paralytic esotropia
  c. Intermitant exotropia
  d. Alternation exotropia
  e. Accomodative esotropia

Explanation:
The clinical picture describes a 3rd nerve palsy (Oculomotor nerve palsy) causing ptosis, mydriasis, and limitation of adduction/elevation/depression. The unopposed action of the lateral rectus causes the eye to turn outward, resulting in paralytic exotropia.
